الإسناد حقيقة لكن بعد إخراج المستثنى نعم : إذا كان عمومه باعتبار تعلق النفي واقتضائه العموم عقلاً كما ذهب إليه المصنف لا يصح الإخراج ولا التخصيص، وإلا لزم التناقض إلا أن يراد الإخراج والتخصيص عن العموم البدلي الذي يكون في النكرات، ثم يعمم بورود النفي في الباقي، لكن على هذا يجوز الاستثناء عن النكرة في الإثبات أيضاً، هذا والله أعلم ما هو الصواب .

مستعملة الضمير

وهذه الجماعة (قالوا) في إبطال الرأي الأول (أولاً): لو لم يكن المراد بالمستثنى منه (الكل) بل كان المراد منه الباقي لزم عود الضمير إلى النصف في نحو : اشتريت الجارية إلا نصفها لأن المذكور سابقاً حينئذ هو النصف، والضمير إنما يعود إلى المرجع المذكور سابقاً، وعود الضمير إلى النصف باطل ، إذ يكون المعنى : اشتريت نصف الجارية إلا نصفها فيكون المخرج الربع) وقد كان المقصود استثناء النصف هذا خلف ثم الربع إذا كان مستثنى بقي الربع وهو المراد بالجارية حينئذ، فيكون المخرج ربع الربع ، وهكذا إلى غير النهاية (قلنا) لا نسلم أن الضمير يعود إلى النصف بل المرجع اللفظ باعتبار المفهوم الموضوع له فالجارية في النصف والمرجع الجارية باعتبار المفهوم اللغوي وفيه نظر ظاهر، فإن حقيقة أن يعود إلى المراد بالمرجع لا إلى ما وضع له المرجع، وسيصرح المصنف به أيضاً، كيف لا وهل هذا إلا مثل أن يقال : رأيت أسداً مسلحاً، ثم يرجع الضمير إليه باعتبار الأسد المفترس، فلا يجوز إلا بالتكلف المحض المستغنى عنه، فإنه يجوز أن يستعمل الجارية في معناها كما مر ، ثم يرجع الضمير إليها فتدبر (و) قالوا :(ثانياً : إجماع أهل العربية أنه إخراج بعض عن كل ولا يمكن الإخراج عن الحكم بعد ثبوته فإنه تناقض، ولو لم يكن الإخراج عن المستثنى منه بطل الإخراج مطلقاً ويلزم خلاف الإجماع، فلا بد من تناول المستثنى منه للمستثنى (قلنا : المراد لأهل الإجماع من لفظ الإخراج الإخراج) تقديراً) بمعنى المنع عن الدخول، وكونه بحيث لولا الاستثناء لدخل فيه (و) المراد بلفظ الكل (الكلية باعتبار المفهوم) اللغوي (ظاهراً) لا باعتبار المراد وفيه أنه لا بد للتأويل من ضرورة ملجئة لا سيما في كلام أهل الإجماع فإنه لو كان مرادهم هذا المحمل البعيد لبين أحد ومن البعيد عادة أن يهمل هذا الجم الغفير في موضع الاشتباه العظيم فتدبر (و) قالوا (ثالثاً فيه أي في كون الباقي مراداً من لفظ المستثنى منه إبطال نصوصية العدد إذ صح حينئذ إرادة عدد من عدد، وهذا هو الذي مر من صدر الشريعة أقول فرق بين المفهومية والمراد بالحكم فإن معنى ربما يكون مفهوماً بحسب اللغة، ولا يكون مراداً كما في المجاز ( وليس العدد نصاً إلا باعتبار الأوّل أي باعتبار كونه مفهوماً لا باعتبار المراد وفيه أنه منع لمقدمة منقولة من أهل العربية فلا يقبل من غير حجة، والقول بكونه نصاً باعتبار انفهام المفهوم اللغوي، فليس مخصوصاً بالعدد، فإن كل لفظ في المفهوم اللغوي بمعنى هو المفهوم من اللفظ، وإن لم يرد في بعض المواضع بل النصوصية ليست إلا نصوصية الإرادة فافهم. ولا تلتفت إلى ما يبدىء احتمال كونه نصاً غير الاستثناء، قال في «التحرير» مجيباً عن هذا الوجه إن النصوصية بمعنى عدم احتمال الغير

نص

أن

أن لا تكون من اللفظ نفسه، بل إنما تكون من خارج فلو كان العدد نصاً كان نصوصية بخارج وها هنا الخارج وهو الاستثناء قائم دال على أنه أريد به معنى آخر فيكون نصاً في الباقي بعد الاستثناء، ولا يبعد أن يقال : معنى نصوصية العدد عدم صحة التجوز فيه مما وضع له إلى مرتبة تحتانية أو فوقانية وبالجملة لا يجوز إطلاق عدد على آخر، ولا يحتمل هذا النحو من التجوز، وقد صرح به أهل العربية فلا مجال للمنع هذا ثم إنه قد يستدل على أصل المدعي بأنه لو كان المراد من المستثنى منه الباقي تجوّزاً لم تبق النصوص أي المفسرات مفسرات الاحتمال الاستثناء هناك، فبقي احتمال المجاز، وعلى هذا ينطبق جواب التحرير انطباقاً تاماً فإن هذه النصوصية من خارج، وإذا كان هناك استثناء يكون نصاً في الباقي، والأظهر أن يقال: إن المفسر كما أنه بطل فيه احتمال المجاز الآخر من الخارج يبطل فيه احتمال الاستثناء أيضاً من خارج فتأمل فيه وقال القاضي : المجموع) وهو عشرة إلا ثلاثة (موضوع بإزاء سبعة) يعني المستثنى منه مع أداة الاستثناء والمستثنى موضوع بإزاء الباقي كما يوضع لشيء اسمان مفرد ومركب وإليه مال كلام طائفة من (الحنفية بل محققيهم ومنهم صدر الشريعة رحمه الله تعالى (أقول : يلزم عليه أن يكون وضع له بل لكل عدد أسماء غير متناهية، فإن مراتب الأعداد لا تقف عند حد وكل عدد إذا استثنى منه ما زاد به على عدد معين بقي ذلك العدد، وقد قلتم : إن المجموع موضوع بإزاء الباقي، فلزم أن تكون المراتب كلها مع استثناء ما زادت به على عدد موضوعة بإزائه فتدبر) فإن استحالة اللازم في حيز الخفاء، كيف لا وقد وضعت الألفاظ المركبة الغير المتناهية بالوضع النوعي بإزاء معانٍ غير محصورة وأيضاً : كما أنه يجوز وضع لفظ بإزاء معان غير متناهية بوضع واحد كذلك يجوز العكس أيضاً فافهم (ورد أيضاً بلزوم عود الضمير في إلا نصفها إلى جزء الاسم لأن الجارية إلا نصفها بمنزلة معد يكرب حينئذ، والجارية جزؤه أعجبني قولهم هذا بعد فهمهم من قول القاضي أن الجارية إلا نصفها صار من قبيل بعلبك، فأين الضمير حتى يرجع بل الضمير حينئذ مثل زاي زيد نعم كان في الأصل ضميراً راجعاً ولم يكن جزء لفظ حينئذ فافهم (و) رد بلزوم تخصيصه وإفادته الحكم المخالف (كمفهوم اللقب) فإنه حينئذ أفاد الحكم على اسم جنس نفي الحكم عما عداه، وهو مردود عند الجمهور وإن قال به من لا يعتد به (و) رد بلزوم التركيب) أي تركيب الاسم (من) كلمات (ثلاثة أقول بل التركيب (من) كلمات أربعة في نحو ثلاثون إلا أحد عشر وهو) أي تركيب اسم من ثلاثة حال كونه (في غير المحكي نحو تأبط شراً، فإنه يجوز (و) الحال (الأول): غير مضاف نحو : أبي عبد الله فإنه جائز، اتفاقاً ولا معرب) ظاهره يفيد أن امتناع التركيب من ثلاثة إنما هو إذا لم يكن الأول معرباً، وهنا كذلك، فينبغي أن لا يمتنع، وهو خلاف المدعي والأظهر في العبارة الأول معرب غير مضاف والأولى أن يجعل من التعريب فيكون إشارة إلى جواز ذلك في الأسماء المنقولة الأعجمية ولا حرف خلاف اللغة بالاستقراء ثم لما كان هذه الإيرادات إنما ترد إذا أراد القاضي أنها كلمات ركبت وجعلت كلمة واحدة وكان (قول القاضي المنسوب إليه عند هذه الإرادة مع قطع النظر عن لزوم تلك الاستحالات اسماً
The chain of transmission is real, but after extracting the excepted [term] yes: if its generality is based on the attachment of negation and its requirement of generality intellectually, as the author holds, then extraction and specification are not valid, otherwise contradiction would follow unless extraction and specification are intended from the alternative generality that occurs in indefinite nouns, then it becomes general by the occurrence of negation in the remainder, but on this, exception from an indefinite is also permissible in affirmative statements. This, and God knows best what is correct. The pronoun is used. And this group (said) in refuting the first opinion (firstly): If the intended meaning of the thing from which exception is made (mustathna minhu) were not (the whole) but rather it intended the remainder, then the return of the pronoun in expressions like 'I bought the slave-girl except half of her' would necessarily refer to half, because what was mentioned earlier then is half, and the pronoun only returns to the previously mentioned referent, and the return of the pronoun to half is invalid, for then the meaning would be 'I bought half of the slave-girl except half of her', so the excluded amount would be a quarter, while the intended was the exception of half, this is fallacy; then the quarter, if it is excepted, remains a quarter, and that is what is intended by the slave-girl then, so the excluded amount would be a quarter of a quarter, and so on to infinity. (We said): We do not concede that the pronoun returns to half; rather the referent is the word in consideration of the meaning for which it is positioned, so 'slave-girl' in 'half' and the referent is 'slave-girl' in consideration of the linguistic meaning, and this is evidently problematic, for the reality is that it returns to what is intended by the referent, not to what the referent is positioned for, and the author will also state this explicitly. How could it not be so? Is this not like saying: 'I saw an armed lion', then the pronoun returns to it in consideration of the predatory lion? That would not be permissible except with pure affectation that is unnecessary, for it is permissible to use 'lion' in its meaning, then the pronoun returns to it. So reflect. (And) they said (secondly): The consensus of the Arabic grammarians is that exception is the extraction of a part from the whole, and extraction from the ruling after its establishment is impossible, for that is a contradiction. If the extraction were not from the mustathna minhu, then extraction would be invalid absolutely, leading to violation of consensus. So it is necessary that the mustathna minhu encompasses the excepted. (We said): What the people of consensus intend by the word 'extraction' is extraction in estimation, meaning preventing from entry, and its being such that were it not for the exception would have entered into it. (And) what is intended by the word 'whole' is totality in consideration of the linguistic meaning apparently, not in consideration of the intended meaning. And therein that interpretation requires an compelling necessity, especially in the statements of the people of consensus, for if they intended this far-fetched interpretation, someone would have clarified it, and it is far from the norm that this vast multitude would neglect it in a place of great obscurity. So reflect. (And) they said (thirdly): In this, i.e., in considering the remainder as intended from the word mustathna minhu, there is invalidation of the specificity of number, for then it would be permissible to intend one number from another number. This is what was mentioned from Sadr al-Shariah. I say: There is a difference between being understood and being intended by the ruling, for a meaning may be understood linguistically but not intended, as in figurative speech. And a number is not a specific text (nass) except in consideration of the first, i.e., in consideration of its being understood, not in consideration of the intended. And therein is a prevention of a premise transmitted from the Arabic grammarians, so it is not accepted without proof. The statement that it is a nass due to the understanding of the linguistic meaning is not specific to numbers, for every word in the linguistic meaning is the meaning understood from the word, even if not intended in some contexts. Rather, specificity (nassiyya) is only specificity of intention. So understand. And do not pay attention to what may be suggested as the possibility of its being a nass other than exception. He said in 'al-Tahrir' responding to this aspect: Indeed, nassiyya in the sense of not admitting of other [interpretation] that it is not from the word itself, but rather from something external. So if number were a nass, it would be nassiyya by external, and here the external, which is exception, stands indicating that another meaning is intended, so it becomes a nass for the remainder after exception. And it is not far to say: the meaning of nassiyya of number is the invalidity of figurative extension from what it is positioned for to a lower or higher level. In short, one number cannot be applied to another, and this type of figurative extension is not possible. And the Arabic grammarians have stated this clearly, so there is no room for prevention. Then, it may be argued for the original claim that if the intended meaning of the mustathna minhu were the remainder figuratively, then the specific texts (nusus), i.e., the explicators, would not remain explicators of the possibility of exception there, so the possibility of figurative interpretation would remain. And on this, the response of al-Tahrir applies perfectly, for this nassiyya is external, and if there is exception, it becomes a nass for the remainder. And the most apparent is to say: The explicator (mufassar) just as the possibility of other figurative interpretation is invalidated by external, so too the possibility of exception is invalidated by external. So reflect on it. And al-Qadi said: The sum, i.e., ten except three, is positioned for seven, meaning the mustathna minhu with the exception particle and the excepted are positioned for the remainder, just as a thing has two names, simple and compound. And towards this leans the statement of a group of Hanafis, indeed their verifiers, among them Sadr al-Shariah, may God have mercy on him. I say: This would entail that it is positioned for it and for every number there would be infinite names, for the levels of numbers have no limit, and every number when it is excepted from it what increases it over a certain number, that number remains. And you have said: The sum is positioned for the remainder, so it would follow that all levels with the exception of what they are increased by over a number are positioned for it. So reflect. For the impossibility of the necessary consequence is not hidden. How could it not be, given that infinite compound words have been positioned by type (wad' naw'i) for unlimited meanings. And also: just as it is permissible to position a word for infinite meanings with one act of positioning, so too the reverse is permissible. So understand. And it was also refuted by the necessity of the pronoun in 'except half of her' returning to a part of the name, because 'the slave-girl except half of her' becomes like 'Ma'di Karib' then, and 'slave-girl' is its part. I am amazed at their statement after their understanding from al-Qadi's statement that 'the slave-girl except half of her' becomes like 'Ba'labakk'. So where is the pronoun that it should return? Rather the pronoun then is like the 'zay' of 'Zayd'. Indeed, originally it was a pronoun returning, and it was not then part of the word. So understand. (And) it was refuted by the necessity of its specification and its conveying a contrary ruling (like the concept of the title), for it would then convey a ruling on a common noun, negating the ruling from everything else, which is rejected by the majority, although some insignificant people accept it. (And) refuted by the necessity of composition, i.e., composition of the name from three words. I say: Rather the composition is from four words in something like 'thirty except eleven', and it is a composition of a name from three in the case of non-quoted [expressions] like 'Ta'bat Sharran', for it is permissible. And the first state: non-construct, like 'Abi Abdullah', for it is permissible by agreement and not [fully] inflected. Its apparent meaning indicates that the prohibition of composition from three is only when the first is not inflected, and here it is so, so it should not be prohibited, which is contrary to the claim. And the more apparent in the expression is that the first is inflected not in construct, and it is better to take it from Arabicization (ta'rib), indicating the permissibility of that in transferred non-Arabic names. And no letter violates the language by induction. Then, since these objections only arise if al-Qadi intended that they are words composed and made into one word, and (the statement attributed to al-Qadi with this intention, disregarding the necessity of those impossibilities, is a name.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute about the meaning of the mustathna minhu (the thing from which exception is made) in exception expressions. The author presents three main objections from an opposing group against the Hanafi position that the mustathna minhu originally means the whole (kull), not the remainder (baqi). Opponents argue: 1) If mustathna minhu meant the remainder, a pronoun in expressions like 'I bought the slave-girl except half of her' would refer to half, leading to infinite regress. The Hanafi response asserts that the pronoun returns to the original referent (the whole) in consideration of its linguistic meaning, not the part. 2) The consensus of Arabic grammarians defines exception as extracting a part from the whole, so mustathna minhu must be the whole. The Hanafi reply interprets 'extraction' as 'prevention from entry' and 'whole' as totality in linguistic understanding, not intended meaning. 3) If mustathna minhu could mean the remainder, numerical specificity (nassiyyat al-adad) would be invalidated. The Hanafi response distinguishes between linguistic understanding and actual intention; numbers are nass only in terms of linguistic comprehension, and the presence of exception indicates a different intention. The author then discusses al-Qadi's view that the compound 'ten except three' is a single word for seven, refuting it with the necessity of infinite names and other logical/linguistic problems. The passage concludes with further rebuttals regarding pronoun reference, specific rulings, and composition rules. Throughout, the author maintains the Hanafi position that the mustathna minhu must be given its literal meaning (the whole) barring strong contextual indication to the contrary.

Key Terms
المستثنى منه (al-mustathna minhu): The thing from which exception is made; in this dispute, whether it refers to the whole (kull) or the remainder (baqi).
الاستثناء (al-istithna): Exception; a linguistic operation of excluding a part from a whole.
نص العدد (nass al-adad): The specificity of numeric expressions; that numbers cannot be used figuratively to refer to other numbers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position defended in the passage, what is the original meaning of the mustathna minhu in an exception statement like 'اشتريت الجارية إلا نصفها'?
A) The remainder after exception
B) The whole (the entire slave-girl)
C) The half mentioned in the excepted phrase
D) The compound expression 'الثلاثة إلا عشرة'

Key Takeaway
The mustathna minhu is the whole in literal sense; exception does not physically extract but prevents entry into the ruling, preserving linguistic and logical coherence.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
10 min
Excepting the Majority (Istithna' al-Akthar) – Dispute and Proof
Dispute on excepting the majority with proof from Quran and Hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of the major madhhabs on istithna' al-akthar, identify the Hanafi proof from Quran and Hadith, and evaluate the opposition's argument.

Core Idea
Excepting the majority is permissible in Hanafi usul because the Quran and Hadith show that Allah excepted the majority of deviators from His servants.

This lesson examines a key dispute in usul al-fiqh: can one except the majority (istithna' al-akthar) from a general statement? The Hanafi school argues for permissibility in non-numerical contexts, citing the Quranic verse about Iblis and the hadith of the 999 from Ya'juj and Ma'juj. We will analyze the proofs and understand why the Hanbalis and some others dissent.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its evidence
Identify what the Hanafis hold about excepting the majority and note the primary Quranic verse (15:42) they use.
The Hanafis allow excepting the majority in non-numbers.
2
Identify the opposing views
List which madhhabs or scholars oppose excepting the majority (Hanbalis, al-Baqillani) and their reasoning.
The opposition argues that excepting the majority is like excepting all.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
How do the Hanafis distinguish excepting the majority from excepting all? Note the point that the majority still leaves some individuals.
The Hanafis emphasize the difference between akthar and kull.
4
Examine the hadith proof
The passage uses a hadith from Bukhari. Explain how the ratio 1:1000 supports excepting the majority.
The hadith shows that the excepted group (the ummah) is the minority.
5
Determine the practical consequence
What is the thamarah al-khilaf? How does this dispute affect actual rulings in contracts or oaths?
The ruling affects the validity of exceptions in legal documents.

والأكثر من الشافعية والمالكية على جواز استثناء النصف والأكثر منه بعد اتفاقهم على منع استثناء الكل وإن كان أخص منه في المفهوم ومنعهما الحنابلة قيل إنما يمنعون الأكثر فقط دون النصف والقاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني من الشافعية وقيل بمنعهما إن كان المستثنى منه عدداً وفي البديع قال به القاضي آخراً لنا في جواز استثناء الأكثر في غير العدد أولاً قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ عِبَادِى لَيْسَ لَكَ عَلَيْهِمْ سُلْطَنُ إِلَّا مَنِ اتَّبَعَكَ مِنَ الْغَاوِينَ ﴾ [الحجر: ٤٢] خطاباً لإبليس حين قال فبعزتك لأغوينهم أجمعين ﴾ [ص: ۸۲] ومن ههنا بيانية لأن الغاوين كلهم متبعوه بالضرورة الدينية فلا تكون للتبعيض فاستثنى الغاوين عن عبادي وهم أي الغاوون أكثر لأن قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا أَكْثَرُ النَّاسِ وَلَوْ حَرَصْتَ بِمُؤْمِنِينَ ﴾ [يوسف: ١٠٣] خطاباً مع صلوات الله عليه وآله وأصحابه دل على أن الأكثر ليس بمؤمن وكل من ليس بمؤمن فهو غاو فالأكثر غاوون، وهم مستثنون عن عباد الله، فصح استثناء الأكثر، ثم إن الأولى أن يستدل على أكثرية الغاوين بما صح في الخبر كما يدل عليه ما روى البخاري عن أبي سعيد الخدري رضي الله عنه أنه قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : يقول الله يوم القيامة : يا آدم يقول : لبيك ربنا وسعديك، فينادي بصوت : إن الله يأمرك أن تخرج من ذرّيتك بعثاً إلى النار . قال : يا رب وما بعث النار؟ قال: من كل ألف أراه قال : تسعمائة وتسعة وتسعون، فحينئذ تضع الحامل حملها ويشيب الوليد وترى الناس سكارى وما هم بسكارى ولكن عذاب الله شديد، فشق ذلك على الناس حتى تغيرت وجوههم فقال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : من يأجوج ومأجوج تسعمائة وتسعة وتسعون ومنكم واحد ، أنتم في الناس كالشعرة السوداء في جنب الثور الأبيض أو كالشعرة البيضاء في جنب الثور الأسود، إني لأرجو أن تكونوا ربع أهل الجنة، فكبرنا، ثم قال: «ثلث أهل الجنة فكبرنا، ثم قال: شطر أهل الجنة فكبرنا فنسبة أهل الجنة إلى أهل النار نسبة الواحد إلى الألف، وأن نسبتنا إلى يأجوج ومأجوج نسبة الملح إلى الطعام، ويأجوج ومأجوج كفرة غاوون، وأما الاستدلال بهذه الآية فللمناقشة فيه مجال فإنه يجوز أن يكون الناس معهودين هم العرب أو أهل مكة ويدل عليه قوله عز من قائل : ﴿وَلَوْ حَرَصْتَ [يوسف: ۱۰۲] كما لا يخفى، ثم الاستدلال إنما يتم لو لم يكن المراد الناس والملائكة أجمعين من عبادي، ولو لم تكن إضافة العباد للتعظيم والاستثناء منقطع أي ليس لك على عبادي لفظ
And the majority of Shafi'is and Malikis are of the opinion that it is permissible to except half and more than half, after their agreement on prohibiting excepting all, even if it is more specific in meaning. And the Hanbalis prohibit both, but it is said that they only prohibit the majority only, not half. And Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani from the Shafi'is, and it is said that he prohibited both if the exception-from is a number. And in al-Badi' it is said that the Qadi later held that. For us, in the permissibility of excepting the majority in non-numbers, first is the statement of Allah Almighty: 'Indeed, My servants, there is for you no authority over them except those who follow you among the deviators' [15:42] addressed to Iblis when he said 'By Your might, I will surely mislead them all' [38:82]. And 'min' here is explanatory because the deviators are all his followers by religious necessity, so it is not partitive. So He excepted the deviators from 'My servants', and they - i.e., the deviators - are the majority, because the statement of Allah Almighty: 'And most of the people, even if you strive, are not believers' [12:103] addressed to him with the prayers of Allah upon him, indicated that the majority are not believers, and everyone who is not a believer is a deviator, so the majority are deviators, and they are excepted from the servants of Allah, so excepting the majority is valid. Then it is better to prove the majority of deviators by what is authentic in the tradition, as indicated by what Bukhari narrated from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, may Allah be pleased with him, that he said: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'Allah will say on the Day of Resurrection: O Adam. He will say: Here I am, our Lord, at Your service. Then a voice will call: Allah commands you to bring forth from your offspring a delegation for the Fire. He will say: O Lord, what is the delegation for the Fire? He will say: From every thousand - I think he said - nine hundred and ninety-nine.' Then at that time the pregnant will drop her load and the child will age, and you will see people drunk but not drunk, but the punishment of Allah is severe. This distressed the people until their faces changed. Then the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'From Ya'juj and Ma'juj nine hundred and ninety-nine, and from you one. You among the people are like a black hair on the side of a white bull, or like a white hair on the side of a black bull. I hope that you will be a quarter of the people of Paradise.' We said 'Allahu Akbar.' Then he said: 'A third of the people of Paradise.' We said 'Allahu Akbar.' Then he said: 'Half of the people of Paradise.' We said 'Allahu Akbar.' So the ratio of the people of Paradise to the people of Hell is one to a thousand, and our ratio to Ya'juj and Ma'juj is like salt to food. And Ya'juj and Ma'juj are disbelieving deviators. As for deducing from this verse, there is room for discussion, because it is possible that 'the people' is specific to the Arabs or the people of Mecca, as indicated by His statement, the Exalted: 'But if you strive' [12:102] as is clear. Then the deduction is only valid if the intended meaning of 'My servants' is not all people and angels, and if the addition of 'servants' is not for glorification, and the exception is disjunctive, meaning 'you have no authority over My servants' - the word.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage details the dispute among the madhhabs regarding the permissibility of excepting the majority (istithna' al-akthar). The Shafi'is and Malikis allow excepting half or more than half, though they unanimously prohibit excepting all. The Hanbalis prohibit excepting the majority, but some say only the majority, not half. Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani also prohibited it when the exception-from is a number, later adopting this view. The Hanafis (implied by 'lana') argue for the permissibility of excepting the majority in non-numerical contexts, using the Quranic verse (15:42) where Allah says to Iblis that he has no authority over His servants except the deviators who follow him. Since the deviators are the majority (as shown by 12:103: most people are not believers), this verse is an example of excepting the majority. The passage also references a hadith from Bukhari showing that out of every thousand, 999 are from Ya'juj and Ma'juj, and only one from the ummah, indicating that excepting the majority is correct. However, the author notes that the Quranic proof is open to discussion because 'the people' may refer specifically to the Arabs or Meccans, and that the verse might be interpreted as a disjunctive exception. Nevertheless, the hadith provides stronger support.

Key Terms
استثناء الأكثر (istithnā' al-akthar): إخراج أكثر من نصف أفراد المستثنى منه
الغاوون (al-ghāwūn): الذين اتبعوا إبليس وهم الكفار والعصاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Hadith of the intercession
D) Hadith of the pond

Key Takeaway
Excepting the majority is permissible in Hanafi usul, supported by the Quranic verse on Iblis and the hadith of the 999, distinguishing it from excepting all.

Hanafi Rebuttal of the Majority Exception Prohibition
Hanafi rebuttal of Hanabila/Qadi arguments against excepting the majority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi and Hanbali arguments on excepting the majority in istithna' and state the Hanafi position with its proof.

Core Idea
Excepting the majority is linguistically valid; ugliness affects eloquence, not legal validity.

This passage examines a key dispute between the Hanafi school and the Hanabila/Qadi on the permissibility of excepting the majority in istithna'. The Hanafis argue that it is not a denial after admission but a longer expression, and that ugliness does not invalidate a statement linguistically. We will analyze both sides and the Hanafi rebuttals.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanbali position
Identify the two main arguments of the Hanabila and the Qadi: (1) it is a denial after admission with no necessity for the majority, (2) it is ugly because the remainder is too small.
The Hanbalis permit only the exception of the lesser due to necessity.
2
Analyze the first Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafis respond that it is not a denial but a longer expression of the same meaning; the speaker is free to choose words. Also, if it were invalid, the Quran would not use it.
They cite the existence of such exceptions in the Quran as evidence.
3
Analyze the second Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafis argue that ugliness does not affect linguistic validity; it only affects eloquence. They provide a counterexample (ten except a da'iq and daniq) where a small exception is also ugly but still valid.
The key distinction is between linguistic validity and eloquence.
4
Determine the Hanafi position
The Hanafi position is that excepting the majority is linguistically valid (sahih) but may be inelegant (mustaqbah). It does not invalidate the statement.
The author sides with the Hanafis, as indicated by 'قلنا'.
5
Apply the principle
Practice applying this rule to similar statements: e.g., 'I owe you ten except nine' is valid and means one is owed.
The same logic applies to oaths and contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحنابلة والقاضي قالوا :أولاً: الأصل (عدمه أي عدم جواز الاستثناء مطلقاً لا استثناء الأقل، ولا الأكثر ولا المساوي (لأنه إنكار بعد إقرار) وهو لا يجوز وخالفناه في الأقل للضرورة (لأنه ينسى) الأقل كثيراً (فيستدرك) بخلاف الأكثر أو النصف، لأنه قلما ينسى، فلا ضرورة فبقي على الأصل، ومن حكى خلاف الحنابلة في الأكثر فقط نسب هذا الاستدلال إلى القاضي قلنا :أولاً: لا نسلم أن الأصل عدمه، وليس هو إنكاراً بعد إقرار، بل هو أداء المقصود بطريق أطول، ولا حجر على المتكلم في التعبير، والعجب من القاضي مع قوله بأن المركب موضوع بإزاء الباقي كيف ادعى أنه إنكار بعد إقرار، وقلنا ثانياً لو صح ما ذكره لما وقع الاستثناء في كلامه تعالى، لأنه بريء عن الضروريات وعن النسيان و (قلنا) ثالثاً ما ذكرتموه مظنة والمظنة لا تعارض (المئنة فإن وجود هذا النحو من الاستثناء ثبت بلا ريب فتدبر (و) قالوا (ثانياً عشرة إلا تسعة ونصف وثلث وثمن مستقبح وليس إلا لأن الباقي ثلث الثمن أقل فلا يجوز (قلنا) ما ذكرتم (منقوض بعشرة إلا دائقاً ودانقاً إلى عشرين) فإنه مستقبح والمجموع ثلث العشرة فلو كان الاستقباح موجباً لعدم الصحة لما صح في صورة استثناء الأقل أيضاً (والحل) أنَّا لا نسلم أن الاستقباح لبقاء الأقل بل الاستقباح للطول) من غير فائدة و لا ينافي الاستقباح (صحة العبارة) لغة وإنما ينافي البلاغة (ولا كلام) لما (في البلاغة) بل نقول استثناء الأكثر فيما يخل البلاغة مستقبح كاستثناء الأقل فيما لا يخل لا فتدبر .
The Hanbalis and the Qadi said: First, the principle is its non-permissibility, i.e., the non-permissibility of exception absolutely—neither exception of the lesser, nor the greater, nor the equal—because it is a denial after admission, and that is not allowed. We opposed them in the case of the lesser due to necessity, because one often forgets the lesser and thus corrects it. As for the greater or half, one rarely forgets it, so there is no necessity; thus it remains on the original principle. Whoever reported the disagreement of the Hanbalis only in the case of the greater attributed this argument to the Qadi. We said: First, we do not concede that the principle is its non-permissibility; it is not a denial after admission, but rather a delivery of the intended meaning by a longer route, and there is no restriction on the speaker in expression. It is surprising from the Qadi, with his statement that the composite is placed for the remainder, how he claimed it is denial after admission. Second, we said: if what he mentioned were correct, then exception would not occur in the speech of Allah—because He is free from necessities and forgetfulness. Third, we said: what you mentioned is a probable indication, and a probable indication does not contradict a definite indication. Indeed, the existence of this type of exception is established without doubt, so ponder. (And) they said, secondly: 'Ten except nine and a half, and a third, and an eighth' is ugly, and it is only because the remainder is less than a third of the eighth, so it is not permissible. We said: what you mentioned is refuted by 'ten except a da'iq and a daniq up to twenty'—for it is ugly, and the total is a third of ten. If ugliness necessitated invalidity, then it would not be valid in the case of excepting the lesser as well. (The solution) is that we do not concede that the ugliness is due to the smallness of the remainder; rather the ugliness is due to the length without benefit. Ugliness does not contradict the linguistic validity of the expression; it only contradicts eloquence. There is no dispute about what is in eloquence. Rather we say: excepting the greater when it violates eloquence is ugly, just as excepting the lesser when it does not violate is not ugly. So ponder.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute between the Hanafis and the Hanabila/Qadi on the permissibility of excepting the majority in istithna'. The Hanabila argue that exception of the majority is not allowed because it is a denial after admission, and there is no necessity for it since one rarely forgets the majority. The Hanafis respond that it is not a denial but a longer expression of the same meaning, and the speaker is free to choose his words. They also argue that if it were invalid, the Quran would not use it. The second argument from the Hanabila is that excepting a large part leaves a small remainder, which is ugly and thus invalid. The Hanafis refute this by showing that even in cases of excepting a small amount, the expression can be ugly, so ugliness does not affect linguistic validity but only eloquence. The Hanafi position is that excepting the majority is linguistically valid, though it may be inelegant. The author supports the Hanafi view.

Key Terms
استثناء الأكثر (istithnā' al-akthar): استثناء أكثر من نصف المستثنى منه، أو ما يبقى منه أقل من المستثنى.
الاستقباح (al-istiqbāḥ): كون العبارة مستقبحة في الذوق اللغوي لطولها أو عدم فائدتها.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is invalid because it is a denial after admission.
B) It is valid only if the remainder is more than half.
C) It is linguistically valid but may be inelegant.
D) It is invalid because it is ugly.

Key Takeaway
Excepting the majority is linguistically valid; ugliness does not invalidate but only affects eloquence.

Metaphorical Interpretation in Agency: The Case of Khusumat
Dispute on interpretation of 'khusumat' and validity of exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of metaphorical interpretation (majaz) to resolve the conflict between literal meaning and Sharia prohibition in agency contracts, and evaluate the ikhtilaf between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on the validity of excepting iqrar from wakala for khusumat.

Core Idea
When literal meaning leads to prohibition, the term shifts to a metaphor to preserve legal validity.

This passage explores a dispute between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding whether an exception of iqrar (confession) from a general agency for khusumat (litigation) is valid. The key issue is whether 'khusumat' must be taken literally (prohibited dispute) or metaphorically (permissible answering in court). Imam Muhammad adopts the metaphorical interpretation to avoid invalidating the agency, while Abu Yusuf argues that denial (inkar) fully exhausts the meaning, making the exception void. The passage illustrates how Hanafi usul employs majaz when haqiqah leads to impermissibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal problem
A person appoints an agent for 'khusumat' (litigation) but excepts 'iqrar' (confession). Is the exception valid? The answer hinges on the meaning of khusumat.
2
State Imam Muhammad's position
Imam Muhammad: the exception is valid because khusumat is interpreted metaphorically as 'any response in court', which includes both confession (iqrar) and denial (inkar).
The literal meaning (quarrel) is prohibited by Quran 8:46.
3
Analyze the dalil for metaphorical shift
The primary proof is Quran 8:46 'Do not dispute', which prohibits the literal khusumat. Taking the literal meaning would make the agency invalid (since agency for a prohibited act is void). Hence, to preserve validity, the term must be taken metaphorically.
4
Contrast Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf argues that denial (inkar) is synonymous with khusumat, so the exception exhausts the excepted-from and is invalid. He does not adopt the metaphorical interpretation.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The author favors Imam Muhammad's view because the context of exception (istithna') itself prompts a metaphorical reading, and because haqiqah is abandoned due to prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتأمل فيه والحق عند العليم بأحكامه وإنما أجازه أي استثناء الإقرار من الخصومة الإمام (محمد لاعتباره الخصومة مجازاً في الجواب) مطلقاً في مجلس القضاء، وهو متناول للإقرار قصداً (لأن الحقيقة ههنا (مهجورة شرعاً) لأنها حرام لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَتَزَعُوا ﴾ [الأنفال: ٤٦]) والمهجور شرعاً كالمهجور عرفاً، فلا يحمل عليها بل ينتقل إلى المجاز، ثم هجران الحقيقة أن، لا ينتقل الذهن إليها من إطلاق اللفظ وهذا غير ظاهر في لفظ الخصومة، فإن الحرمة لا توجب أن لا تستعمل الخصومة في معناها، فالأولى أن يقرر هكذا الحقيقة غير مرادة لأنها محرمة شرعاً، والتوكيل بالمحرم باطل فلو أبقى على الحقيقة بطل التوكيل، فلا بد من الحمل على مطلق الجواب في مجلس القضاء، ولعلهم أرادوا بهجران الحقيقة الهجران في التوكيل بها خاصة لبطلان التوكيل بها، فلا ينتقل الذهن في عرف المؤمنين المتشرعين من التوكيل بالخصومة إلا إلى التوكيل بالجواب، كما لا ينتقل من المجامعة إلا إلى الفعل الحلال في عرفهم فتدبر وعلى هذا أي كون الخصومة مجازاً عن مطلق الجواب في مجلس القضاء (صح استثناء الإنكار أيضاً عنده لكونه فرداً منه وبطل عند أبي يوسف للاستغراق) أي لكونه مستغرقاً للمستثنى منه لكونه مساوياً لها في المفهوم، فإن الإنكار هو الخصومة، هذا والعجب أنه أبطل الاستثناء ولم يحمل الخصومة على المجاز بقرينة الاستثناء مع كونه تشديداً فتدبر (ولها فروع) مذكورة (في «الهداية» في كتاب «الإقرار يطول الكلام بذكرها .
So reflect on it. The truth, according to the Knower of His rulings, is that Imam Muhammad permitted it, i.e., the exception of iqrar from khusumat, considering khusumat to be metaphorical for 'answering' absolutely in the court of judgment, and it comprehends iqrar intentionally because the literal meaning here is abandoned by Law because it is prohibited due to His statement: 'And do not dispute' [8:46]. And what is abandoned by Law is like what is abandoned by custom, so it is not applied but rather one moves to the metaphor. Then the abandonment of the literal meaning means that the mind does not move to it upon uttering the word, but this is not apparent in the word khusumat because prohibition does not necessitate that khusumat is not used in its meaning. So it is better to state that the literal meaning is not intended because it is prohibited by Law, and agency for something prohibited is invalid; if the literal meaning were retained, the agency would be invalid. Therefore, it must be construed as absolute 'answering' in the court of judgment. Perhaps they meant by abandonment of the literal meaning the abandonment specifically in agency because agency is invalid thereby. So the mind, in the custom of religious believers, moves from agency of khusumat only to agency of answering, just as it moves from sexual intercourse only to the lawful act in their custom. Reflect. And upon this—i.e., that khusumat is metaphorical for absolute answering in the court of judgment—the exception of inkar (denial) is also valid according to him because it is an instance of it, and it is invalid according to Abu Yusuf because it is exhaustive, i.e., it encompasses the excepted-from because it is equal to it in concept, for denial is khusumat. It is surprising that he invalidated the exception and did not interpret khusumat metaphorically due to the context of exception, even though it would be a strengthening. Reflect. And it has branches mentioned in al-Hidayah in the Book of Iqrar, and the discussion would be lengthy.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal validity of excluding iqrar (confession) from a general agency for khusumat (litigation) in Hanafi law. The author explains that Imam Muhammad allowed the exception by interpreting 'khusumat' metaphorically to mean any response in court, thus including iqrar. The literal meaning is abandoned because litigation (in a quarrelsome sense) is prohibited by the Quran (8:46), so the term must be taken metaphorically. If taken literally, the entire agency would be invalid since agency for a prohibited act is void. The author critiques that the mere prohibition of an act does not prevent the mind from understanding the literal meaning; rather, the key is that the agency itself would be void if the literal meaning were intended. Thus, the only way to salvage the agency is to interpret 'khusumat' as 'answering'—a permissible act. Abu Yusuf disagreed, arguing that 'denial' (inkar) is fully included in the meaning of khusumat, so the exception exhausts the excepted-from and fails. The author finds Abu Yusuf's position inconsistent for not applying the metaphorical interpretation despite the context of exception.

Key Terms
الخصومة (al-khusumat): Literally litigation or dispute; here interpreted metaphorically as any response in the court of judgment.
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): The literal, original meaning of a word
المجاز (al-majaz): Metaphorical meaning adopted when literal meaning is prohibited
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): The exception, here of iqrar from the general term khusumat

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the word 'khusumat' has no established literal meaning in Sharia.
C) Because Abu Yusuf insisted on a metaphorical interpretation.
D) Because the Quran commands all disputes to be avoided entirely.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when the literal meaning of a contract term leads to a prohibited act, the term is interpreted metaphorically to preserve the validity of the legal act.

The Problem of Kalimat al-Tawhid and Its Resolution Through 'Urf al-Shar'
Discusses the famous usul problem in 'لا إله إلا الله' and its resolution via 'urf al-shar'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of 'urf al-shar' to resolve ambiguous textual expressions and construct a proof chain from text to ruling.

Core Idea
The Kalimat al-Tawhid is resolved not by philosophical precision but by the custom of the Sharia.

The phrase 'لا إله إلا الله' poses a famous usul problem: what is the implied predicate? Two estimations lead to logical difficulties. The Hanafi answer, based on 'urf al-shar', avoids these difficulties by deferring to Sharia usage rather than strict linguistic or philosophical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguity
The predicate (khabar) of the negated noun 'إله' is omitted; two possible estimations are 'al-mawjud' (existent) or 'al-mumkin' (possible).
This ambiguity is a classic example of a mujmal (ambiguous) text requiring bayan (clarification).
2
Analyze each estimation
If 'al-mawjud': the meaning is 'no existent god except Allah', which does not negate the possibility of another god. If 'al-mumkin': the meaning is 'no possible god except Allah', which does not affirm Allah's existence.
Both fail to convey complete tawhid on a strict linguistic reading.
3
Recognize the problem
Neither estimation alone achieves the intended theological meaning; a strict analytical approach leads to a logical impasse.
This shows the limitation of applying pure linguistic/philosophical methods to sacred texts.
4
Apply the Hanafi principle
The phrase is built on 'urf al-shar' (Sharia custom), not on strict linguistic rules. In Hanafi usul, ambiguous texts are clarified by the custom of the Lawgiver.
'Urf al-shar' includes the customary usage of the Sharia community and the understood meanings of key terms.
5
Derive the resolution
Both estimations are permissible; the speaker can choose either based on the intended meaning in the Islamic context. The Kalimat al-Tawhid is valid as understood by the Sharia.
The resolution avoids philosophical puzzles by deferring to the authority of Sharia custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ههنا فوائد : الفائدة الأولى : ( في كلمة التوحيد إشكال مشهور فإن المقدر) للخبرية (إما الموجود) فالمعنى لا إله إلا الله (فلم يلزم منه (عدم إمكان إله سوى الله تعالى) ولا يتم التوحيد الكامل (وإما الممكن فالمعنى: لا إله ممكن بالإمكان العام المقيد بالوجود إلا الله (فلم يلزم منه وجوده تعالى فلم يفد التوحيد أصلاً (ويجاب أوّلاً كما نقل عن شارح المختصر» بأن كلمة التوحيد) مبنى (على) عرف الشارع فلك اختيار كلا الشقين إن شئت قدر الموجود، وإن شئت
Then here are benefits: The first benefit: (In the Kalimat al-Tawhid there is a famous problem: for the presupposed predicate (al-muqaddar) is either the existent (al-mawjud), so the meaning is 'There is no god except Allah', but from this the impossibility of a god other than Allah is not entailed, and complete tawhid is not achieved; or the possible (al-mumkin), so the meaning is 'There is no possible god with general possibility qualified by existence except Allah', but from this His existence is not entailed, so it does not benefit tawhid at all. (And it is answered) firstly, as transmitted from the commentator of al-Mukhtasar: that the Kalimat al-Tawhid is based on the custom of the Lawgiver (urf al-shar'), so you may choose either of the two senses: if you wish, estimate the existent, and if you wish...
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage is a brief discussion of a famous usul problem concerning the Kalimat al-Tawhid ('لا إله إلا الله'). The problem arises from the grammatical analysis: the sentence is a negative exception construction, and it requires an implied predicate (khabar) for the negated noun 'إله'. If the predicate is estimated as 'al-mawjud' (the existent), then the phrase means 'There is no existent god except Allah', which implies that no other god exists, but this does not logically negate the possibility (imkan) of another god, so it does not achieve the complete tawhid of negating all potential deities besides Allah. If the predicate is estimated as 'al-mumkin' (the possible), then the meaning is 'There is no possible god (by general possibility qualified by existence) except Allah', but this does not entail the actual existence of Allah, so the phrase fails to affirm Allah's existence. The first answer, attributed to the commentator of al-Mukhtasar (likely a Hanafi source), resolves this by stating that the phrase is based on the custom and usage of the Sharia (urf al-shar'), not on philosophical or linguistic precision. Therefore, the speaker may choose either estimation according to the intended meaning in the Islamic context. The text cuts off, but the implication is that both estimations are valid within the framework of Sharia usage, and the problem is resolved by referring to the customary understanding of the words of tawhid in the Islamic tradition.

Key Terms
كلمة التوحيد (kalimat al-tawhid): The phrase 'لا إله إلا الله', which encapsulates the Islamic principle of monotheism.
المقدر (al-muqaddar): The presupposed or implied element in a sentence, here the predicate (khabar) of the negated noun.
موجود (mawjud): Existent: one possible predicate meaning 'there is no existent god except Allah'.
ممكن (mumkin): Possible: one possible predicate meaning 'there is no possible god except Allah' (with general possibility).
عرف الشارع (urf al-shar'): The custom and usage of the Lawgiver (Sharia), which provides the correct interpretive framework for religious texts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first answer to the problem of the Kalimat al-Tawhid as mentioned in the passage?
A) It should be interpreted solely according to the estimation 'al-mawjud'.
B) It should be interpreted solely according to the estimation 'al-mumkin'.
C) It is based on the custom of the Sharia (urf al-shar'), so both estimations are possible.
D) The phrase is not intended to convey theological meanings.

Key Takeaway
The Kalimat al-Tawhid is understood according to Sharia custom, resolving the predicate ambiguity without philosophical impasse.

Analysis of Exception in Surah An-Nur
Debating whether the exception is muttasil or munqati'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle distinguishing muttasil and munqati' exception to analyze the legal implications of istithna in a Quranic verse.

Core Idea
A disconnected exception (munqati') does not negate the original ruling for the excepted; it only removes a subsequent attribute or implication.

When the Quran says 'except those who repent', does it nullify all previous penalties for the repentant? The Hanafi school, through rigorous linguistic and legal analysis, determines that the exception is disconnected (munqati'), meaning the repentant still undergo punishment but are freed from eternal condemnation. This lesson unpacks the arguments for this position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of the verse
The verse contains a series of legal rulings (flogging, rejection of testimony, declaration of fisq) followed by an exception. Determine the scope of the exception.
2
Analyze the grammatical addressees
Note that the first part uses plural address (rulers) while the exception switches to singular (Prophet). This difference suggests the exception is not conjoined to the initial ruling.
Third argument in the passage.
3
Evaluate the logical implications of a connected exception
If the exception were connected, it would imply the repentant were never sinful, which is contradictory because they repented from sin.
Fourth argument in the passage.
4
Apply the Hanafi rule of istithna
In Hanafi usul, a disconnected exception does not negate the original hukm for the excepted; it only removes a subsequent description or attribute.
5
Derive the legal ruling
The repentant qadhif (false accuser) is still flogged, but their testimony is accepted after repentance, and they are no longer considered fasiq for future matters.
Final ruling: flogging applies, testimony accepted later.

The third article on linguistic principles. If the exception were from the essence, it would entail the non-establishment of the ruling for the excepted, and the result would be: 'Those are the sinful, the disobedient to Allah, except the accusers who repented, for they are not sinful but obedient' (and this) is contrary to reality, for all are sinful, repentant and otherwise. If they were not sinful, from what did they repent? Rather, the repentant become righteous after repentance, and the rest remain perpetually in sin, not that the repentant were never characterized by it at all. (In summary, connection from 'those' or from the generality of conditions is not sound except with an unacceptable contrivance according to the experts. (Lexically) as if it is said the exception is from conditions, and the meaning: those are sinful at all times except the time of repentance from it, which the wording of the excepted rejects except with an estimate that is dispensable; or semantically, as if it is excepted from 'those' by making their sinfulness as if it were no sin. Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a detailed textual analysis of the exception (istithna) in Surah An-Nur, verse 4-5. The author, 'Abd al-'Ali al-Ansari in his commentary on Musallam al-Thubut, examines whether the exception 'illa alladhina tabu' (except those who repent) is connected (muttasil) or disconnected (munqati'). The discussion focuses on grammatical and contextual arguments to determine the legal effect. The Hanafi position, as represented by the author of al-Hidayah, is that the exception is disconnected, meaning it does not remove the repentant from the ruling of being sinful or from the rejection of their testimony. The text presents four arguments: first, the difference in line of address (khitab) between the initial part (addressed to the rulers in plural) and the exception part (addressed to the Prophet in singular) prevents conjunction; second, the nominal sentence cannot be conjoined to the imperative due to syntactic reasons; third, alternative interpretations of conjunction are proposed but rejected; fourth, the exception from the essence or description leads to contradictions—if from 'those' (the accusers), it implies they were not sinful before repentance, which is false; if from the description of fisq, it is impossible because the essence is not part of the description. The author supports the munqati' view, which aligns with Hanafi usul: the exception does not negate the preceding ruling for the repentant but rather indicates that after repentance, they are no longer considered fasiq for future rulings. This analysis demonstrates the Hanafi method of careful grammatical and legal reasoning in interpreting Quranic texts.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna): An exception, a particle (illa) excluding something from a general ruling.
متصلة (muttasil): Connected exception where the excepted is part of the general category.
منقطعة (munqati'): Disconnected exception where the excepted is not part of the general category or is excluded from a different aspect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the repentant are no longer sinners
B) Because the addressee of the exception differs from the initial address, preventing conjunction
C) Because the exception is from the essence, which is invalid
D) Because the exception is connected to the predicate

Key Takeaway
A disconnected exception (munqati') does not negate the original ruling for the excepted; it only removes a subsequent attribute.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Conditional Oath and Divorce of Two Wives
Three opinions on conditional divorce when only one wife enters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate three intra-Hanafi interpretations of a conditional oath addressed to multiple subjects, and apply the principle of maximizing prevention (man') to determine the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
A conditional oath is interpreted to maximize prevention; alternativity in the condition is preferred.

When a husband addresses an oath of divorce to both his wives using a plural condition ('if both of you enter'), jurists differ on the outcome if only one enters. This passage presents three Hanafi opinions and an extended discussion about whether the condition should be read as distributive, collective, or alternative. The dispute turns on the purpose of oaths and how language interacts with intent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal case and the three opinions
The husband says to two wives: 'If both of you enter, you are both divorced.' Only one enters. Three opinions: (a) only the one who entered is divorced, (b) neither is divorced, (c) both are divorced.
Each opinion corresponds to a different interpretation of the condition.
2
Distinguish the types of condition: distributive vs. collective vs. alternative
Distributive: each condition applies individually (first opinion). Collective: both must occur together (second). Alternative: any one triggers the result (third).
The third opinion takes the condition as alternative (badalan) while the result remains collective (jam'an).
3
Analyze the rationale (talil) for the preferred opinion
The purpose of the oath is to prevent (man') entry. An alternative condition is more restrictive because it triggers the result on any single breach, thus stronger deterrence. Therefore, the condition should be interpreted as alternative to achieve maximum prevention.
The author argues this is 'أبلغ' (more effective) for the oath's purpose.
4
Evaluate the objection and the author's response
Mirzajan criticizes the distinction between condition and result as arbitrary since the wording is identical. The author responds that the purpose justifies the distinction — the condition is alternative to achieve prevention; the result is collective because divorce affects both. This shows the role of maqasid in tarjih.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the tarjih is clear despite the fragment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرع * قال : إن دخلتما فأنتما طالقان مخاطباً لاثنتين من زوجاته فدخلت إحداهما دون الأخرى قيل : تطلق هي لأن الشرط متحد وهو دخول واحدة واحدة والجزاء كذلك هو طلاق كل وقد وجد شرط طلاق الداخلة فتطلق ثم أشار إلى العلة بقوله وطلاق كل بدخولها يعرف بالعرف وهذا أوفق بمذهبنا من أن مقابلة الجمع بالجمع تقتضي انقسام الآحاد على الآحاد وقيل : لا تطلق واحدة منهما لأن الشرط دخولهما جميعاً ولم يوجد، فلا يترتب الجزاء وقيل ليس هذا ولا ذاك بل تطلقان معاً لأن الشرط لطلاق كل دخولهما بدلاً وقد وجد فيترتب الجزاء وعلى هذا الشرط متعدد بدلاً والجزاء معاً قيل في حواشي مرزاجان على شرح المختصر فيه تحكم لعدم الاختلاف في اللفظ بين الشرط والجزاء فالحكم بالبدلية في الشرط دون الجزاء تحكم بحت أقول المقصود من اليمين المنع من الدخول ولا شك أن أخذ الشرط بدلاً أبلغ فيه، فهو المرجح لأخذ الشرط بدلاً دون الجزاء (فتدبر) وفيه أن
Branch: He said, 'If both of you enter, then both of you are divorced,' addressing two of his wives. Then only one of them entered without the other. It was said: She is divorced, because the condition is singular, i.e., the entrance of each one individually, and the result is also singular, i.e., the divorce of each. And the condition for the divorce of the one who entered has occurred, so she is divorced. Then he pointed to the cause by his saying, 'And the divorce of each by her entrance is known by custom,' and this is more in accordance with our school than that confronting the plural with the plural necessitates the division of individuals over individuals. And it was said: Neither is divorced, because the condition is the entrance of both together, and it did not occur, so the result does not follow. And it was said: Not this nor that; rather both are divorced, because the condition for the divorce of each is their entrance alternatively, and it has occurred, so the result follows. According to this, the condition is multiple alternatively, and the result is together. It was said in the glosses of Mirzajan on the commentary of al-Mukhtasar: There is arbitrariness in this because there is no difference in wording between condition and result, so ruling with alternativity in the condition but not in the result is pure arbitrariness. I say: The purpose of the oath is to prevent entry, and there is no doubt that taking the condition as alternative is more effective for that, so it is the preponderant reason for taking the condition as alternative without the result. So reflect. And in it that...
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage presents a legal dispute (ikhtilaf) within the Hanafi school about the interpretation of a conditional oath of divorce addressed to two wives: 'If both of you enter, you are both divorced.' When only one enters, three opinions arise. The first opinion (consistent with Hanafi methodology) holds that the one who entered is divorced, as the condition is understood distributively (each entrance triggers divorce for that wife). The second opinion says neither is divorced because the condition requires both to enter. The third opinion says both are divorced, interpreting the condition as alternative (entrance of either triggers divorce for both). The author prefers the third because it better achieves the oath's purpose of preventing entry, and takes the condition as alternative while keeping the result as collective (both divorced). A gloss by Mirzajan criticizes this distinction as arbitrary, but the author defends it based on the oath's intent. This exemplifies how usul principles (types of conditions) are applied to fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
بدلاً (badalan): Alternative condition: any one of the specified matters triggers the result.
جمعاً (jam'an): Collective condition: all matters must occur together to trigger the result.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view in this passage, what happens when a man tells his two wives 'If both of you enter, you are both divorced' and only one enters?
A) Only the one who entered is divorced.
B) Neither is divorced.
C) Both are divorced.
D) The marriage is annulled.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the purpose of an oath (man') takes precedence over literal phrasing when interpreting conditions, leading to the preference for an alternative condition to maximize deterrence.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Double Linkage in Preposing the Subject: Basran vs Kufan Debate
Analyzing the grammatical debate on preposing the subject in nominal clauses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Basran analysis of double linkage (al-nisba marratayn) to interpret legal conditional sentences in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Preposing the noun creates double linkage: verb to pronoun, then noun to verb — affecting conditionals.

This passage delves into a seemingly grammatical dispute that has profound implications for usul al-fiqh: the analysis of sentences like 'Zaydun qama'. At stake is how we understand the structure of conditional statements in legal texts — whether the apodosis is an independent predicate or part of a linked construction. The author defends the Basran view that the verb is predicated on an implied pronoun, creating a double attribution, and argues that this aligns with the insights of balaghah and explains number agreement patterns. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to parse the precise legal force of conditional clauses in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
State the Basran claim about 'Zaydun qama'
Identify that the Basrans held there is an implied pronoun subject (damir manawi), the explicit noun is a mubtada', and the verb is predicated of the pronoun, yielding double attribution.
This is the core of the double linkage theory.
2
Explain the Kufan counter-claim
The Kufans argued that the pure Arab perceives only one attribution (standing to Zayd) regardless of word order, and therefore the preposed noun is simply the subject.
This objection relies on the intuitive understanding of the native speaker.
3
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf for usul
The analysis of conditional sentences: if the apodosis is an independent predicate (Basran), the condition restricts a separate ruling; if not, the condition may be part of the same predicate.
This affects how we interpret exceptions and conditions in legal texts.
4
Apply the double linkage to conditional sentences
Take 'I honor you if you enter'. According to Basrans, 'I honor you' is not an independent report but a predicate tied to an implied apodosis; the double linkage explains the suspension.
The verb's inherent expectancy of a subject is key.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The author sides with the Basrans, citing balaghah evidence that preposing adds emphasis (secondary meaning) and verb agreement patterns confirm the pronoun analysis.
This is the mufta bihi view in Hanafi usul.

Issue. Then the apparent meaning of this speech suggests [something] specified from condition like exception in rulings except in its following the sentences, for it is not like exception but (to all it is advanced in estimation) so it is advanced over all (since its right is precedence) in speech like interrogation and desire. And it has preceded that condition also necessitates a contrary ruling in what it excludes, like exception. Perhaps the similarity is in exceptional rulings. Then the statement that condition is delayed, since it contradicts the saying of the Basran grammarians, he intended to invalidate it and said: As for the Basran saying in like 'I honor you if you enter', what precedes is a predicate, i.e., an independent declarative sentence and not the apodosis, and if he said 'what precedes is a sentence' it would be more comprehensive, (and the apodosis is omitted) because it indicates it (and therefore it is not made jussive) although being a present tense verb, which is made jussive as condition and apodosis. (So it does not indicate) this speech except an honor restricted and suspended on entry, so what precedes is not an absolute report of honor (and therefore it is not false on the supposition of no honor due to no entry); if it were an absolute ruling it would be false. (And the restriction) i.e., restriction of honor and suspending it on entry twice is not understood by necessary (innate) understanding. So what precedes is not a report of a restricted honor that explains the estimated apodosis like the sentence after the implied object on condition of explanation, like 'Zaydan I struck him'. (This) And as for their saying: what precedes is not made jussive if it is present tense, we say: perhaps because preposing nullifies the operation of the conditional particle, so consider. (It was said) in the glosses of Mirzajan on the commentary of al-Mukhtasar, like what (they said, i.e., the Basrans, that in 'Zaydun qama' there is a pronoun that is the subject, and what precedes is a subject (mubtada'), and innate understanding denies it, for the understood) from it (in preposing and postponing) i.e., preposing the explicit and postponing it (is one) i.e., the attribution of standing to Zayd (and hence the pure Arab who has not learned grammar rules does not distinguish between them) i.e., between preposing and postponing (in meaning, so the truth is with the Kufan scholars where they permitted preposing the subject) according to what the author of al-Muhakimat transmitted. I heard from the reviewer of divine secrets, my father (may his secret be sanctified) repeatedly that this transmission does not match grammar books; indeed the Kufan and Basran scholars all agree that the subject does not precede at all, and in the case of preposing the explicit noun that precedes is a subject (mubtada') by agreement, and hence they agree on the verb agreeing with it in preposing in singular, dual, and plural because it carries a pronoun, not in the case of postponing, but they always require the verb to be singular, so consider. (I say: The scholars of balaghah agree on the difference between the two cases of preposing and postponing according to secondary meanings) i.e., qualities and additional advantages beyond the original intended meaning understood from speech, such as being a rejection of denial and other things. Here, from preposing an emphatic ruling is understood, not from postponing. (So the denial) i.e., the denial of innate understanding of the difference is perhaps due to lack of (natural fluency to understand subtleties of speech. As for the pure Arab not distinguishing, if he is a commoner not eloquent, then (it is not regarded if his lack of distinction is granted; how? Since he does not distinguish between 'what I said' and 'I said what'—the first indicates negation of speech from the speaker while affirming it from someone else, unlike the second which indicates negation from him while remaining silent about others, and so on from other speeches. So what is not regarded as a distinction between 'ana' (I) with these two speeches, likewise it is not regarded as a lack of distinction in our case. (And if the pure Arab is eloquent, we do not grant that he does not distinguish; rather he understands in preposing the attribution twice, unlike postponing. (How) would he not distinguish? (And the basis of balaghah scholars is only the understanding of the eloquent Arabs). This is solid speech. Then he wanted to explain the point and said: (And the secret of the difference is that the verb according to its reality is waiting for connection to something not yet mentioned because it contains a complete predication needing a specific subject. If (the thing) is mentioned after it, then that is the one attributed to it; (otherwise) it is not mentioned, so its connection is considered to what precedes besides the linkage that requires that the preceding be connected to what is mentioned after it. (So the linkage is observed a second time, and that is the meaning of the implied pronoun. And it may be objected that the reality of the verb awaiting connection to what has not been mentioned is denied; and to something else it is granted, but it does not necessitate connection and linkage a second time such that a meaning that is intended is derived. But the matter is easy for one who has served the literary sciences, so it is established. And from here, i.e., because of the difference between preposing and postponing (it is correct: qama al-zaydaani because it is predicated to the postponed, so the verb is singular; unlike al-zaydaani qama because it is predicated to the pronoun referring to the preposed, so concord is lost. So the truth) here (i.e., in like 'Zaydun qama' (is with the Basran scholars) regarding the verb being predicated to the pronoun and understanding the double linkage. (This) so preserve it, for it is worthy of preservation.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This is a deep grammatical dispute relevant to usul al-fiqh because the interpretation of conditional sentences and the function of the apodosis affects legal rulings. The passage argues against the Basran grammarians' claim that in a sentence like 'I honor you if you enter', the preceding part 'I honor you' is not a full predicate but only part of a conditional construction. The author refutes this by showing that the understanding of restriction is not innate and that the preposed part does not function as an independent report. The discussion then extends to the debate on preposing the subject (faa'il) versus making it a subject (mubtada'). The author reports that some claim the Kufans allow preposing the subject, but he clarifies that both Basrans and Kufans agree the preposed noun is a subject, not a preposed grammatical subject. The verb agrees in number because it carries a pronoun. The key insight from balaghah is that preposing and postponing create different secondary meanings: preposing gives emphasis. The 'secret of the difference' is that the verb inherently awaits connection to something not yet mentioned; if the subject follows, that's the attribution; if not, the verb connects to what precedes via an implied pronoun, creating a double linkage. This explains why in 'Zaydun qama' the verb is singular (predicated of the pronoun) and why 'qama al-zaydaani' is correct (verb agrees with postponed subject). The author concludes that the truth lies with the Basrans: in such structures the verb is predicated of a pronoun and double linkage is understood. This is a critical refinement of the matn's position, using arguments from balaghah and grammar.

Key Terms
تقديم الفاعل (taqdeem al-faa'il): Placing the subject (faa'il) before the verb, which in this debate is analyzed as making the preposed noun a subject (mubtada') rather than a grammatical subject.
ضمير منوي (dameer manawiy): An implied pronoun that serves as the actual subject of the verb when the explicit noun is preposed.
النسبة مرتين (al-nisba marratayn): Double attribution: the verb is attributed to the implied pronoun and the preposed noun is attributed to the implied verb, resulting in two acts of predication.
جملة شرطية (jumla shartiyya): A conditional sentence consisting of a condition (shart) and a response (jawab).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Basran double linkage analysis is essential for understanding the structure of conditional sentences in Hanafi usul.
