Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
6 min
Three Rebuttals to the Claim of Impossibility of Abrogating Specific by General
Refuting the argument that abrogating a specific rule with a general one is impossible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on abrogating a specific text by a general text and evaluate the three rebuttals provided against the opposing view.

Core Idea
Abrogating a specific by a general is possible because the 'certainty' of the specific is only incidental, not linguistic.

In this passage, the author of Fawatih al-Rahamut defends the Hanafi position that a later general text can abrogate an earlier specific text. The opponent argues this is impossible because it would replace a certain ruling with a probable one. The author provides three subtle rebuttals that dissect the nature of certainty and the logical structure of abrogation. Understanding these arguments is crucial for mastering Hanafi usul al-fiqh and engaging in inter-madhhab discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
State the argument that abrogating a specific (certain) text by a general (probable) text is impossible.
The opponent's reasoning: the specific gives certainty for an individual, while the general gives only probability; certain cannot be overturned by probable.
2
Analyze the first rebuttal
Examine the claim that the 'specific' is itself general from another perspective, so abrogation is not of a pure specific.
The specific text is general in the sense that it applies to multiple individuals; abrogation only affects some of its individuals.
3
Analyze the second rebuttal
Understand that the certainty of the specific is only for some individual, not all individuals, and that individual is not absolutely certain.
The specific's certainty is limited; the general's probability for each individual is comparable, so no priority.
4
Analyze the third rebuttal
Grasp that the certainty for a particular individual is rational (aqlī) not linguistic (lughawī); it is a necessary implication of the general's meaning, not the direct meaning itself.
Thus abrogating the rational implication by a later textual ruling does not destroy linguistic certainty.
5
Evaluate the overall Hanafi position
Synthesize the three rebuttals and conclude that the Hanafi stance is logically sound and the opponent's objection is refuted.
The author demands proof from the opponent, showing confidence in the Hanafi argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يرد عليه أولاً أنه لا يتم في الخاص من وجه من العام فإنه عام أيضاً، والانتساخ فيه لحكم بعض أفراده مع بقائه في البعض الآخر، وحكم هذا البعض مظنون مع عموم المدعي) لهذا الخاص من وجه أيضاً كما يظهر من الدليل (الأول) فلم يتم التقريب، إلا أن يقال : المدعي وإن كان عاماً لكن قد ثبت بما ذكرنا عدم جواز انتساخ الخاص المطلق بالعام المطلق، فيعم الحكم لعدم القائل بالفصل، وعلى هذا يلغو الكلام كله، فإنه يمكن أن يقال إن الدليل دال على عدم جواز انتساخ الخاص المقدم بالعام فلا يجوز انتساخ العام المقدم بالخاص بالنسبة إليه وبالعكس لعدم القول بالفصل ثم لنا أن نعكس ونقول العام المتقدم منسوخ بالخاص : بالنسبة إليه المتأخر لعدم المانع فيه فيجوز نسخ الخاص الحقيقي بالعام المتأخر عنه، وكذا نسخ الخاص بالنسبة إليه به لعدم القول بالفصل فهذا كله لغو وتعب (و) أقول، ثانياً : إنما يتم لو قيل بالتخصيص لفرد ما دون جميع الأفراد لأن المقطوع هو فرد ما وأما الأفراد، فمظنونة فلا إخراجه من العام الذي ورد بعده إذ لا أولوية (وهو خلاف المذهب) فلم يصح يتم التقريب، إلا أن يقال : إنهم أو ردوا هذا الإبطال مذهبنا لا لإثبات مذهبهم بأنه يلزم عليكم إبطال المقطوع بالمظنون فتدبر (و) أقول (ثالثاً : القطع بهذا المعنى عقلي لا لغوي، فإن الوضع لكل واحد واحد لا لفرد ما ، فهو إنما يفهم عقلاً، لا أنه موضوع له وهو المراد بالعقلية، فلا تتوجه المناقشة بأن القطع فيه لغوي أيضاً من جهة أنه علم من اللغة عدم جواز إبطال العام بالكلية، وبالجملة إن دلالة العام على فرد ما ليس مقصوداً في الوضع والاستعمال، بل لأنه لازم من اللوازم (فإذا أبطلنا المحتمل بالمحتمل أي (الإفراد الموضوع لها العام الذي هو الخاص بالنسبة إلى العام المتأخر (بالإفراد الموضوع لها العام الثاني (لزم عقلاً انتفاء المطلق قطعاً إذ كان إنما يفهم الملازمة بينه وبين الأفراد، وإذا ارتفعت الأفراد ارتفع ما هو من لوازمه في الفهم (فبطل القاطع) وهو فرد ما (بالقاطع ) هو لزوم بطلانه لبطلان الإفراد، والحاصل أن النسخ بالذات إنما هو لكل واحدٍ واحد من أفراد الأوّل بكل من الثاني وهما مظنونان مدلولان مطابقة، وأما انتفاء فرد ما المفهوم في كل واحد فإذا كان مفهوماً بالعرض بطل بالعرض ولا استحالة فيه وإن ادعى استحالة بطلان القاطع بالمظنون على هذا النحو نمنعه ونطالبه بالبرهان (تأمل)
He replies, firstly: It does not apply to the specific in one respect from the general because it is also general. The abrogation concerns the ruling of some of its individuals while it remains for others, and the ruling of that some is probable along with the generality of the claim. So the approximation is not complete. Unless it is said that the claim, though general, is established by what we mentioned of the impermissibility of abrogating an absolute specific by an absolute general, so the ruling applies due to no one making a distinction. And upon this, the entire speech becomes void. Then we can reverse and say the earlier general is abrogated by the specific with respect to it, and similarly the specific by the general with respect to it due to no distinction being made. So all this is idle talk and effort. Secondly: It would only apply if it were said that specification is for some individual rather than all individuals, because the certain is some individual, while the individuals are probable, so it is not excluded from the general that came after it since there is no priority – and that is against the school. So the approximation is not valid. Unless it is said that they presented this refutation of our school not to establish their school, but because it entails for you the abrogation of the certain by the probable, so reflect. Thirdly: Certainty in this sense is rational, not linguistic. The positing is for each individual, not for some individual. It is understood rationally, not that it is posited for it. That is what is meant by rationality. So the objection that the certainty is also linguistic because it is known from the language that it is impermissible to abolish the general entirely does not apply. In short, the general's indication of some individual is not intended in the positing and usage, but is a necessary implication. So if we abrogate the probable by the probable – i.e., the individuals for which the general (which is specific relative to the later general) was posited, by the individuals for which the second general was posited – then the absolute necessarily ceases rationally, since the necessary connection between it and the individuals is understood. When the individuals are removed, what is their necessary implication in understanding is removed. So the certain (some individual) is negated by the certain (the necessary negation due to the negation of individuals). The result is that abrogation essentially is for each individual of the first by each of the second, and both are probable as literal indications. As for the negation of some individual understood in each, if it is understood incidentally, it is negated incidentally, and there is no impossibility. If one claims the impossibility of negating the certain by the probable in this manner, we deny it and demand proof.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of an opponent's argument against abrogation of a specific ruling by a general one. The opponent claimed that abrogating a certain (specific) ruling with a probable (general) ruling is impossible. The author provides three counter-arguments. First: The specific ruling in question is actually general from another perspective, so the abrogation is not of a pure specific. Second: The certainty of the specific is only for some individual, not all individuals, and the individual is only probable, so it is not impossible. Third: The certainty attributed to the specific is rational, not linguistic; the identification of a particular individual is a necessary implication of the general, not its direct meaning. Therefore, abrogation of the specific by the general is possible. The author dismisses the opponent's objection and demands proof.

Key Terms
الخاص (al-khass): A text that is specific in its indication, either by nature or by specification.
العام (al-amm): A text that is general in its indication, covering all individuals of its subject.
النسخ (al-naskh): Abrogation: lifting a previous ruling by a later one.
القاطع (al-qati'): Certain, a ruling that is definitive in its indication.
المظنون (al-maznun): Probable, a ruling that is uncertain in its indication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third rebuttal, why is it possible to abrogate a specific ruling by a general ruling?
A) Because the specific is always probable, not certain.
B) Because the certainty of the specific for an individual is only rational, not linguistic.
C) Because the general is more authoritative.
D) Because abrogation is allowed only for general texts.

Key Takeaway
The abrogation of a specific by a general is permissible because the certainty of the specific is incidental, not linguistic.

The Master's Command Without Will: Evidence for the Amr-Irada Distinction
Debating whether command equals will with proofs and rebuttals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between command (amr) and will (irada) by citing the master-slave example and identifying the two types of divine will.

Core Idea
Command (amr) is not identical to creative will (irada takwiniyya); a master may command an act he does not will to occur.

In this lesson, we examine a key Hanafi usul distinction: command (amr) is not the same as divine will (irada). The text presents the counterclaim that command equals will, and then refutes it with the example of a master who commands his slave to be beaten but does not actually will the beating. This example demonstrates that command can exist without creative will, paving the way for the Hanafi classification of irada into creative (takwini) and legislative (tashri'i).

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing claim
State the claim that command is the will of the act, as held by some scholars.
2
Examine the master-slave analogy
The master commands the slave to be beaten but does not intend the act to occur; he only wants an excuse. Consider why this is not a real command but a mere form.
3
Distinguish between creative and legislative will
Define irada takwiniyya (creative, necessitates occurrence) and irada tashri'iyya (legislative, does not necessitate). Command falls under the latter.
4
Apply the distinction to the Quranic verse
How does the verse 'Allah only wishes to remove impurity...' (33:33) support the Hanafi view when interpreted as legislative will?
5
Synthesize the thamarah al-khilaf
If command were will, all commanded acts would necessarily occur. Since they do not, command cannot be identical to creative will.

قول القائل، والأخير ليس قول الأعلى وأجيب بأن المراد فعل حقيقة) وفي «التهديد» ليس افعل حقيقة (وفي الحكاية والتبليغ ليس قوله ولا أمراً منه فإن القرآن ليس قول النبي) ولا أمره (صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه لا يستلزم الوقوع فإن الإرادة عندنا صفة مخصصة لأحد طرفي المقدور بالوقوع فلا يتخلف المقدور عنه، ولذا قال الإمام الهمام فيما روي عنه في الفقه الأكبر المعاصي بإرادة الله تعالى دون أمره وعند المعتزلة الداعي إلى الفعل من اعتقاد نفع أو علم مصلحة، وفسر بعضهم بعدم الكراهة قالوا تعلق هذا النحو من الإرادة القديمة لا يوجب وقوع المراد كذا قالوا وفيه ما فيه، ولك أن تقول : الإرادة تكوينية وهي ما ذكر ويجب وقوع المراد في القديمة بحسبها، وتشريعية وهي تبيين المشروعات، واقتضاؤها مع الرضا أو إعطاء المثوبة، وهي لا تستلزم المراد قطعاً، ولعلهم أرادوا هذا النحو من الإرادة، وبهذا فسر بعض ثقاتنا من أهل السنة رفعهم الله تعالى وخذل أعداءهم قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّمَا يُرِيدُ اللهُ لِيُذْهِبَ عَنكُمُ الرِّجْسَ أَهْلَ الْبَيْتِ وَيُطَهِّرَكُمْ تَطْهِيرًا ) [الأحزاب: ۳۳] (فاندفع) بما قرروا أو قررنا (ما) في المختصر لا يحنث لعدم قضائه في الغد فدل على أن الشرط لم يوجد و أنه تعالى ما شاء) وإلا حنث فثبت الأمر بدون المشيئة وهي الإرادة، فالأمر غيرها (وفيه ما فيه أما أولاً : فلأن الاستثناء بالمشيئة إبطال لليمين فلا حلف فلا حنث لأجل هذا الانعدام وجود المعلق عليه، وأما ثانياً فلأن الإرادة القولية غير التكوينية كذا في «الحاشية»، وإن حققته رجع إلى ما قررنا.
The statement of the speaker, and the latter is not the statement of the highest authority. It is answered that what is meant is the actual verb. In 'al-Tahdid', [the imperative] is not actually 'if'al'. In narration and reporting, it is not his statement nor a command from him, because the Quran is not the statement of the Prophet, nor his command, but rather the statement of Allah and His command, and He is the actual speaker. The command of a lower [person] to a higher as an act of superiority is not considered a command linguistically among them, but customarily, so its exclusion is fine. I say: or it is said that the lower is more general, whether actually or by pretense, and in the command of a lower to a higher, the elevation of the lower is claimed, and this is kindness to one who does not accept it, so it is wasted. And a group among them said that command is the will of the act. It was objected that the one who prepares an excuse for beating his slave—and in some copies 'al-muhaddad' i.e., the one who is threatened by another—and on this, his statement 'for his excuse' relates to his saying 'he commands his slave' to do an act but does not intend that he perform it in order to complete the excuse, because a rational person does not want to belie himself. Thus the command is separate from the will, so it is not the same. It is not hidden that a similar case occurs in request, for he commands but does not request the act from him, because a rational person does not request the belittlement of himself. Rather, the investigation is that this is not a command in reality but only a form, for it is a request of a form. So they may say it is a will of a form, so consider. And the distinction between will and request by the impossibility of separation in will without request is not conceded by the opponent, for he interpreted will as not entailing occurrence. Indeed, will according to us is an attribute that specifies one of the two sides of the possible to occur, so the possible does not separate from it. Hence the eminent imam said, as reported from him in al-Fiqh al-Akbar: 'Sins are by the will of Allah without His command.' According to the Mu'tazila, the motive to action is from belief in benefit or knowledge of interest, and some interpreted it as the absence of dislike. They said that the attachment of this kind of eternal will does not necessitate the occurrence of the object of will — thus they said, and there is something in it. You may say: Will is creative (takwini), which is what was mentioned, and the occurrence of the willed is necessary according to it, and it is legislative (tashri'i), which is the clarification of legislations, and its requirement is with satisfaction or giving reward, and it does not entail the object of will at all. Perhaps they intended this kind of will, and with this some trustworthy scholars of Ahl al-Sunnah interpreted the verse: 'Allah only wishes to remove impurity from you, O people of the Household, and to purify you thoroughly' [al-Ahzab 33:33]. So what was in al-Mukhtasar was repelled: if command were will, all commanded acts would occur because they do not separate from the requirement of will. And Abu Ishaq argued for the invalidity of command being will with the case of the existing debt: he is commanded to pay it, and if the debtor swore 'by Allah I will pay it tomorrow if Allah wills', he does not break his oath for not paying the next day, indicating that the condition did not exist and that Allah did not will it — otherwise he would have broken his oath. Thus command is established without the will (mashi'a), which is will, so command is other than it. There is something in it: first, because the exception by the will nullifies the oath, so there is no oath, so there is no breaking for this non-existence of the existence of the conditioned thing. Second, because the verbal will is different from the creative will, as in al-Hashiya. If you investigate, it returns to what we established.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute on whether the command (amr) is identical to the will (irada). The author begins by addressing an earlier claim that the imperative is not actually command in certain contexts like narration. He then presents the view of a group who equate command with will, and refutes it using the example of a master who commands his slave to be beaten without actually willing the act—his purpose is merely to create an excuse. The Hanafi position distinguishes between takwini will (creative, which necessarily brings about the willed object) and tashri'i will (legislative, which does not necessitate occurrence). The verse of purification (33:33) is interpreted as tashri'i will. Abu Ishaq's proof using a debtor's oath is refuted by noting that the exception nullifies the oath, and that the will in question is verbal, not creative. Thus the Hanafi position stands: command is not identical to will.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): The linguistic form demanding an action; in this passage, it is distinguished from will (irada) and is not necessarily linked to the commander's creative will.
الإرادة (al-irada): A divine attribute that specifies one of two possibilities for occurrence; it is divided into creative (takwini) which necessitates occurrence, and legislative (tashri'i) which does not.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the status of a master's command to his slave to be beaten when the master does not actually will the beating?
A) It is a valid command that must be obeyed.
B) It is a form (surah) of command, not a real command.
C) It is a sinful command because it is a lie.
D) It is an example of legislative will.

Key Takeaway
Command (amr) is legislative will (irada tashri'iyya), not creative will (irada takwiniyya); thus a command can exist without the commander's creative will.

The Imperative Does Not Imply Repetition: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Disputing whether the command requires repetition or just once

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether the imperative implies repetition and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the Hanafi proof structure.

Core Idea
The Hanafi imperative requests the absolute nature of an act; once performed, the request is satisfied — repetition is never implied by the command itself.

One of the foundational usul debates asks: when Allah commands an action, does that command demand only a single performance, or does it imply repetition across one's lifetime? The answer shapes how we understand obligations like prayer and zakat. Hanafi usulis argue that the imperative is fulfilled by one instance, while some Shafi'is hold it may imply repetition. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position and its proofs, as presented in our passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
Read the opening lines to pinpoint what is disputed: whether the imperative (amr) implies repetition (takrar) or merely a single performance (marrah).
2
State the Hanafi position and its two main proofs
The Hanafi school holds that the command does not imply repetition. Extract the two proofs: (1) the form of the command indicates only request; the material indicates the absolute nature; (2) the verb 'if'al' can be qualified with 'once' or 'times', showing generality, not specificity for one instance.
Proof 1 relies on consensus of Arabic grammarians.
3
Understand the Shafi'i objection
The opposing school argues that repeated acts like prayer and zakat demonstrate that the command itself implies repetition. They also analogize to prohibition, which is held to imply repetition.
The passage attributes this view to some Shafi'is, e.g., al-Razi and al-Amidi.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that repetition in ibadat arises from external causes (time, year), not from the command. The prohibition analogy is rejected because separate proof establishes its repetition implication.
Also, using the command for repetition would be metaphorical, violating the principle of literal interpretation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if someone performs fajr once, it is sufficient; the command does not require repeating it within the same time without a new cause. The Shafi'i view would potentially require repetition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة الأمر لطلب الفعل مطلقاً عندنا فيبرأ المأمور (بالمرة) أي بإتيان الفعل مرة ويحتمل التكرار ) بطريق استعمال المطلق في المقيد والتكرار إتيان الفعل مرة بعد أخرى، فهو ملزوم العدد واختاره الإمام الرازي والآمدي وكلاهما من الشافعية، ثم ظاهر قوله عندنا يقتضي أن هذا مذهب الحنفية وليس كذلك فإنه صرح الإمام فخر الإسلام أنه لا يحتمل التكرار، وفي البدیع صرح به وقال عندنا وأيضاً سينص المصنف أنه لا يحتمل العدد عند الحنفية وإذا لم يحتمل العدد لم يحتمل التكرار بالطريق الأولى، فالحق في الترجمة ما في «التحرير» أنه لا يحتمل التكرار، ورد المصنف إياه بأنه يأبى عنه الدليل الثاني وجواب شارح المختصر» رد فإنه يجب مجازاً (وهو قول أكثر الشافعية) وهذا مخالف لما نقل مشايخنا عنهم، ويأبى عنه بعض فروعهم ظاهراً، والله أعلم (وقيل بالوقف) في استعمالها في المرّة أو التكرار (للاشتراك) بينهما (أو الجهل بالحقيقة واختاره الإمام) ونقل الآمدي ما اختاره عنه ، كذا في الحاشية لنا أولاً إجماع أهل العربية على أن هيئة الأمر لا تدل إلا على الطلب في الاستقبال) من المأمور (وخصوص (المطلوب من الصوم والصلاة والحج وغير ذلك (من خصوص المادة وهي الطبيعة من حيث هي هي فالأمر إنما هو لطلب الطبيعة في الاستقبال والتكرار من مصاديقها فيحتمله في الحاشية لمانع أن يمنع انحصار دلالة الهيئة في الطلب في الاستقبال فإن الصيغة عند الخصم للمرة، فالحصر كأنه تقرير للدعوى، لعله أراد به منع الإجماع وإلا فلا مساغ، ولك أن تمنع أن المادة أن الطبيعة من حيث هي فإنه سيجيء هي الأمر مختصر المصدر المفرد الدال على الوحدة فتأمل وانتظر فإنه سيجيء ما عليه وله إن شاء الله تعالى ثم لنا أن نقرر هذا الدليل على عدم احتمال التكرار فنقول : الصيغة للطلب والمادة للطبيعة، فالأمر إنما يدل لطلب الطبيعة، فإذا أتى المأمور بفرد واحد انقطع الطلب ويلغو الفعل مرة أخرى فإنه غير مطلوب والتكرار ليس إلا الإيقاع بعد أخرى، ولما امتنع كون الفعل الثاني مطلوباً امتنع كون التكرر مطلوباً فلا يحتمل الأمر التكرار وأما تجوزه فيه من قبيل إطلاق المطلق في المقيد فلا يصح لأن المصدر ادعى عدم احتمال التكرار واستدل بهذا الدليل الذي لا يدل عليه بل ينافيه فتدبر . هذا عندي في هذا المقام والقوم بنوا عدم الاحتمال على اعتبار الوحدة في مفهوم المبدأ وسيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى (و) لنا (ثانياً) صح افعل مرة أو مرات فيكون افعل عاماً في المرة والمرات ولا دلالة للعام على الخاص فلا دلالة على المرة بحسب الوضع فيصح إطلاقه على المرات من قبيل إطلاق المطلق في المقيد فإن قلت يجوز أن يكون الوضع للمرة ويكون التقييد بالمرة تأكيداً وبالمرات تجوزاً قال : ( والحمل على التأكيد والمجاز خلاف الظاهر) فإن التأسيس والحقيقة أصل لا يعدل عنه من غير دليل فاندفع) ما في شرح المختصر» أن احتمال الصيغة لهما لا يمنع ظهور أحدهما بل يجوز أن تكون الصيغة ظاهرة في المرة وتحتمل المرات، وفي أن هذا تسليم لدعوى المستدل من احتمال التكرار نعم : لم يثبت بعض دعواه من كونه لطلب الفعل مطلقاً، فالأولى أن يقال احتمال الصيغة حال التقييد بهما لا يمنع نصوصيته حال الإطلاق في أحدهما وجه الدفع ظاهر، إنه يلزم على هذا التجوّز، وهو خلاف الأصل (أقول) هذا الدليل منقوض) بلا تفعل مرة أو مرات على المذهب الأشهر) من للتكرار وجوابه أنه التكرار لا بنفس الطلب عند الإطلاق ولا بالتجوز كما عرفت لكن لا يمنع أن يقيد بقيد التكرار، فيكون المطلوب من مجموع الكلام التكرار وليس فيه تجوز حتى يكون خلاف الأصل، ولا يلزم منه احتماله عند الإطلاق إذ لا دلالة للأعم على الأخص إلا بالتجوز، وقد عرفت أن التجوز على هذا النمط لا يجوز فتأمل وتشكر أصحاب التكرار (قالوا : أولاً تكرار الزكاة والصلاة والصوم، وغيرها مع أنها مأمورات أنت لا يذهب عليك أنه لا يتم التقريب فإن مدعاهم كان وجوب التكرار إلى الإمكان والصلاة ونحوها لم يتكرر كذلك لا يقال لم يتكرر للحرج لأنه لو سلم فلا يصح استدلالاً على وجوب التكرار لخروجها عما هو حقيقة عندهم، فلا المجازي دليلاً على دخول التكرار في المعنى الحقيقي فافهم (قلنا) ليس تكرره من الصيغة، بل (من غيره)، ونحن لا نمنع التكرار من خارج (وهو) أي الغير الموجب للتكرار تكرر (السبب) وهو الوقت في الصلاة وتكراره ظاهر وفي الزكاة السبب النصاب وهو وإن لم يكن يتكرر لكن الحول أقيم ،مقامه، وهو متكرر فافهم وعورض بالحج فإنه مأمور غير متكرر، بل إنما وجب
Issue: The command is for requesting the action absolutely according to us, so the commanded person is absolved by (the single instance) i.e., by performing the action once, and it potentially implies repetition by way of using the absolute in the restricted sense. Repetition is performing the action once after another, so it is concomitant with number. Imam al-Razi and al-Amidi (both Shafi'is) chose this. Then the apparent meaning of his saying 'according to us' suggests that this is the Hanafi madhhab, but it is not so, for Imam Fakhr al-Islam explicitly stated that it does not imply repetition. In al-Badi' he explicitly said so and said 'according to us'. Also the author will later state that it does not imply number according to the Hanafis, and if it does not imply number, it does not imply repetition a fortiori. So the correct view in the translation is what is in al-Tahrir: that it does not imply repetition. The author refuted it by saying the second proof forbids it. The reply of the commentator of al-Mukhtasar is a refutation, for our speech must match the speech of al-Mukhtasar and its commentator, as is obvious. Al-Ustadh said: Repetition is necessary for the duration of one's life if possible, and upon this are a group of jurists, theologians, and many usulis. (They hold) that it is for the single instance, and does not imply repetition when absolute, metaphorically. This is the view of most Shafi'is. But this contradicts what our shaykhs transmitted from them, and some of their subsidiary rulings apparently forbid it. And Allah knows best. (And it was said: suspension) in its use for the single instance or repetition (due to homonymy) between them (or ignorance of the reality, and Imam chose it) and al-Amidi transmitted what he chose from him. Likewise in al-Hashiyah. For us: Firstly, the consensus of Arabic grammarians that the form of the command indicates only the request in the future from the commanded person, and the specificity of the requested (from fasting, prayer, hajj, etc.) is from the specificity of the material, which is the nature as such. So the command is only for requesting the nature in the future, and repetition is among its instances, so it potentially implies it. In al-Hashiyah: A preventer could prevent restricting the indication of the form to request in the future, for the formula according to the opponent is for the single instance, so the restriction seems like a statement of the claim. Perhaps he intended to deny consensus, otherwise there is no scope. And you could deny that the material is the nature as such, for it will come that the command is the abbreviated verbal noun indicating unity. So reflect and wait, for it will come what is upon it and for it, God willing. Then we can present this proof for the non-implication of repetition: we say: the formula is for request, the material is for the nature, so the command indicates only the request of the nature. When the commanded person performs a single instance, the request ceases, and performing the action another time is null, for it is not requested. Repetition is nothing but performing after another. Since it is impossible for the second act to be requested, it is impossible for the repetition to be requested, so the command does not imply repetition. As for its being permissible figuratively by way of using the absolute in the restricted sense, it is not valid because the verbal noun taken in the verb cannot be manipulated in a way that derivation forbids. Derivation is only valid for what can be integrated into the meaning of the verb. It is established by consensus of Arabic grammarians that what is integrated is the nature as such or the nature restricted by dispersed unity, so it is absolutely not permissible to intend repetition, because it takes it out of being an absolute nature and being a single one by dispersion. So the command does not imply it, neither literally nor metaphorically, and that is the desired conclusion. Based on what we have established, the objection against al-Tahrir is repelled: that it claimed non-implication of repetition and used this proof which does not indicate it but rather contradicts it. So reflect. This is my view at this point, and the people built non-implication on considering unity in the concept of the source. It will come, God willing. (And) for us (secondly): 'Do' is valid with 'once' or 'times', so 'do' is general for once and times. The general does not indicate the specific, so there is no indication for the single instance by virtue of the lexical meaning. So its use for times is by way of using the absolute in the restricted sense. If you say: it could be that the lexical meaning is for the single instance, and the qualification with 'once' is an emphasis, and with 'times' is metaphorical. He said: (Carrying it upon emphasis and metaphor is contrary to the apparent meaning) because establishing (the literal meaning) and the real meaning is the principle, not deviated from without proof. So (the objection) in Sharh al-Mukhtasar is repelled: that the formula's potential for both does not prevent one of them from being apparent. Rather it is possible that the formula is apparent for the single instance and potentially for times. This is a concession to the claim of the proponent of repetition. Yes: some of his claim (that it is for requesting the action absolutely) is not established. So it is better to say: the formula's potential when qualified with both does not prevent its being explicit when absolute for one of them. The repelling is apparent: that it necessitates metaphor, which is contrary to the principle. (I say) This proof is refuted by 'do not do once or times' according to the more famous madhhab (that 'do not' is for repetition). The answer: the proof there indicates repetition, so 'do not do once' is carried metaphorically and 'times' as emphasis, unlike the command. So the principle is not deviated from here. Then know that this also does not suffice to establish potential, for we say the command is for requesting the reality and does not imply repetition, neither by itself when absolute nor metaphorically, as you know. But it does not prevent it from being qualified by a repetition qualifier, so the requested from the entire speech is repetition, and there is no metaphor in it to be contrary to the principle. And from this it does not follow that it implies it when absolute, for the more general does not indicate the more specific except metaphorically, and you know that metaphor in this manner is not permissible. So reflect and thank the proponents of repetition. (They said: Firstly, the repetition of zakat, prayer, fasting, etc., although they are commanded acts. It does not escape you that the approximation is not complete, for their claim was the obligation of repetition to the extent possible, and prayer and such are not repeated likewise. It is not said they are not repeated due to hardship, for even if conceded, it is not a valid inference for the obligation of repetition, because they deviate from what is the real meaning according to them, so the metaphorical does not serve as evidence for the inclusion of repetition in the real meaning. Understand. (We say) Their repetition is not from the formula, but (from something else), and we do not deny repetition from an external factor. (That external factor) necessitating repetition is the repetition of (the cause), which is time in prayer, and its repetition is obvious. In zakat, the cause is the nisab, and although it does not repeat, the year takes its place, and it repeats. Understand. And it was objected by hajj: it is commanded and not repeated, rather it is only obligatory once.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute about whether the imperative (amr) implies repetition (takrar) or just a single performance (marrah). The author is clarifying the Hanafi position. At the outset, he attributes to the Hanafis that the command is for the absolute request of the action, and the commanded person is absolved by performing it once. However, he notes that some scholars (including Imam al-Razi and al-Amidi, both Shafi'is) hold that it does potentially imply repetition. But he then corrects the impression that this is the Hanafi view: Imam Fakhr al-Islam and the author of al-Badi' explicitly state that the command does not imply repetition. In fact, the Hanafi view is that it does not even imply number, so it certainly does not imply repetition. The author then presents two main proofs for the Hanafi position. First, he argues from the consensus of Arabic grammarians that the form of the command (hay'at al-amr) only indicates a request in the future, and the specific action comes from the lexical material (maddah). Since the material indicates the absolute nature (tabi'ah min haythu hiya), the request is for that nature. When the commanded person performs one instance of that nature, the request is fulfilled. A second act would not be requested, so repetition cannot be implied. He also argues from the verb 'if'al' which can be qualified with 'once' or 'times', showing that it is general and does not specify the single instance. If it were for the single instance, using it for 'times' would be metaphorical, which is contrary to the principle of using the literal meaning without proof. He then responds to objections, particularly the Shafi'i argument that the repetition of acts like prayer and zakat shows that the command implies repetition. The Hanafi response is that repetition in these cases comes from an external cause (sabab), such as the recurrence of time for prayer or the passage of the year for zakat, not from the command itself. The dispute is resolved: according to the Hanafis, the command does not imply repetition; it only demands the action once. The passage also mentions that some held a position of suspension (waqf) between the two possibilities, but the Hanafi position is clear.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): The imperative form requesting an action from a higher authority.
التكرار (al-takrar): Performing the same action once again after the first performance.
المرة (al-marra): A single instance of performing the action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in the passage, what is the primary justification for the view that the imperative does not imply repetition?
A) The repetition of prayer and zakat shows that the command requires multiple performances.
B) The form of the command indicates only request in the future, and the material indicates the absolute nature, so one instance satisfies the request.
C) The verb 'if'al' can only be used with 'once', not 'times'.
D) Prohibition implies repetition, so by analogy command also implies repetition.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi imperative requests the absolute nature of an act; once performed, the request ceases — repetition is never implied by the command itself.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
The Imperative Does Not Imply Repetition or Number
Hanafi usul dispute: imperative semantics and its fiqh implications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that the imperative does not imply repetition or number to a given fiqh scenario, and explain the linguistic evidence from the indefinite noun (nakirah).

Core Idea
The imperative demands a single act; repetition and number are not implied by the command itself.

In Hanafi usul, a simple command like 'strike!' is understood to require one instance of striking. But do commands imply doing it again and again (takrar) or multiple times (adad)? This lesson explores the Hanafi position—based on linguistic analysis of the infinitive (masdar)—and contrasts it with the Shafi'i view. Understanding this dispute is critical for deriving rulings, especially in matters like divorce where a single utterance can count as multiple divorces, but not because the command itself indicates multiplicity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
Recognize that the dispute is whether the imperative form implies repetition (takrar) and number (adad). The Hanafis deny both; the Shafi'is affirm the possibility.
Repetition and number are distinct: repetition involves sequential performance, number can be simultaneous (e.g., one utterance intending three divorces).
2
State the Hanafi position and its linguistic proof
The imperative derives from the infinitive (masdar), which is an indefinite noun (nakirah). Indefinite nouns denote a single instance in affirmative contexts (e.g., 'rājul' means one man). Therefore, the command demands the essence once, not repeatedly or in numbers.
The proof is aqli (rational) based on language rules.
3
State the Shafi'i position and its proof
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the imperative can bear number because it is general in possibility. He invokes a Quranic verse (5:101) where the Prophet's companions asked about things and were warned, implying that a command could be repeated.
The Hanafis respond that the verse was about hardship, not the imperative's scope.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response and objections
The Hanafis rebut that the indefinite noun inherently indicates singularity; the Shafi'i proof from the Quranic verse is weak because it does not address the linguistic nature of the imperative. Moreover, compliance is achieved with one act; additional acts are void. They also address objections that the nakirah might denote the essence without number, and that the infinitive's meaning may not fully carry over into the derived verb.
The passage provides three objections and responses. Focus on the core argument: the imperative is for the essence once.
5
Apply to the example of divorce (talaq)
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) appears in divorce. A single utterance 'You are divorced' with the intention of three divorces: Hanafis validate this via 'unity of genus' (three is all owned divorces, hence one category), not because the imperative implies number. Shafi'is validate because they argue the imperative can bear number.
This shows how a usul principle affects actual fiqh rulings.

The third article on linguistic principles. Understand correctly. The shared between one time and repetition, or it is used as evidence for the possibility of repetition, for without possibility there would be no question. The author said: 'All are probable in the apparent sense, and it may be used as evidence for repetition also because the questioner intended repetition, then he saw great difficulty and became confused due to this conflict, so he asked. This is a call from afar, so he turned away from it. The answer from the one who denies the possibility of repetition is that it is permissible that the matter was confused for him: whether it is repeated with repetition of the cause like prayer, or is it similar to the months of Hajj? So he asked. So no proof stands with this possibility. This is supported by the fact that he (the Prophet) became angry and it is narrated in the Sunnah that the verse 'Do not ask about things which if disclosed to you will harm you' [5:101] was revealed concerning this. So reflect. Issue: The imperative form does not imply generality and pure number according to the Hanafis, contrary to al-Shafi'i. Most of our scholars did not single out this issue, but they branched the issue of divorce (talaq) onto the issue of repetition. It was objected in al-Tahrir that many divorces may occur with one pronouncement, so there is no repetition; hence this branching is invalid. Rather, it is an independent issue. Perhaps for this reason he singled it out. This is supported by the fact that repetition is performing the commanded act—the essence—again and again, while number is its multiplicity or abundance. This is absolutely more general than the first because performing the essence again and again occurs only within multiple individuals, and that is number. Multiplicity and abundance may occur with one performance, as in divorce. The claim of generality from one aspect is wrong. Then the required thing is not divorce itself but its occurrence (tatliq), which is the essence. So its multiplicity necessitates repetition. Some teachers of his time strengthened its foundations. In the stronghold of principles, multiplicity branches from repetition. As for the example of divorce, it is not multiple unless its pronouncement is considered repeated, necessarily because the multiplicity of a thing is by repetition of the cause. Even if the utterance is one, it is not considered; consideration is only given to the Shar'i consideration upon which rulings are based. Moreover, Imam Fakhr al-Islam was not satisfied with repetition only but added the word 'generality' as well, so there is no objection against him. Reflect. (Because for 'strike', its meaning is 'he struck a strike') because it is derived from the infinitive which is indefinite, as definiteness is incidental. It is singular and indefinite in affirmative without evidence of generality. So (it is for the one, so it is not said for two men 'a man'). Thus it is not for the essence as such, otherwise it would be correct to apply it to the dual because the essence exists in it. So it is known that its meaning is the essence with a restriction of unity. So it cannot imply multiplicity due to contradiction, so it does not imply number at all. This very point indicates the negation of repetition and that it is for one time. There is scrutiny in it from several aspects. First: That the indefinite noun is placed for the essence and the tanwin indicates unity and distribution, so it is not said of two 'a man'. Thus it does not necessitate that it is placed for the one. Its refutation has already passed: that then it would necessitate that the mentioned word, when used as an absolute object for emphasis, would be stripped of its meaning. This is the second. Granted that the indefinite is placed for the one, but why is it not permissible that the essence stripped of definition and indefinition—the utmost is that the infinitive is not used in it at all? So reflect. Third: Granted that it is the indefinite infinitive, but we do not accept the obligation of its full meaning remaining within the verb, because the wording of the infinitive is not included in it in its form, but only its root. So the survival of its meaning for which the root-form combination was placed is not necessary; rather, part of it may remain, namely the essence which exists equally in the one and the many. It can be said: The apparent texts of language scholars indicate that the full meaning of the infinitive is embedded in the verb. So reflect. As for what we have mentioned, it can be explained in a way that nothing is refuted, because the command is to demand the essence of the origin as such, and it does not indicate number at all, neither really as is apparent, nor does compliance occur by performing the number, but by performing one and the other is void. For by performing one, it is true that he performed the required essence. As for repetition, it is apparent that compliance occurred with the first and the second time is void. As for performing the number at once, compliance occurs with one, not specified, and the other is void. Moreover, multiplicity without succession and repetition is impossible, for the multiplicity of an action with unity of place and time is unintelligible. In 'my divorce', the required thing is not divorce itself, for it is not an essence, but the pronouncement (tatliq) which is the essence. And it is not multiplied except if it is separated really or in the estimation of the Lawgiver. And it is not valid as a metaphor either, for there is no manipulation of the essence taken in the verb by intending individuals, for that manipulation contradicts derivation, because the Arabs agree that the essence taken is only unconditioned. So reflect and be thankful. Then since the primary understanding from real unity, he corrected and said: 'But unity may be real, so it is valid without intention because it is the primary understanding. And it may be relational, which is unity of genus, and it is not primary to understanding, so it is valid with intention.' Hence, the intention of three for a free woman and two for a slave woman in 'divorce yourself' or 'divorce my wife' is valid, because three for free and two for slave are all individuals of the genus, so they are one by genus. As for two for a free woman, it is pure number with no aspect of unity, so intending it is not valid. This is contrary to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, for according to him intending number is valid because the command can bear it. An objection was raised: three is not the entire genus, because divorce applies to these divorces as it applies to divorces occurring on other women; they are also some individuals of the genus like two. The answer: The point is that all individuals of divorce owned are three or two, for the genus owned by everyone is three or two only. Another objection: just as three is one by the genus it partakes in, so too two are also one by genus, so it would necessitate that intending both three and two is valid. And if the relational and aggregative unity is intended, just as three is a relational aggregate, so too two is also. So a difference must be shown. Some teachers asserted that relational unity, whatever it may be, is not sufficient; rather, a real consideration is necessary, and that is only in all individuals of the genus, for they are one genus. As for the levels below it, there is no consideration by which it becomes one. There is something in it. Rather, the correct answer is that not every two things coming together become one; rather, there must be a Shar'i consideration of rulings that attach to the whole different from rulings of the parts. Three for a free woman is a whole with rulings other than those of individuals, for they cause immediate separation and severe irrevocability and exit of the subject from the capacity of marriage. And these rulings are not the sum of the rulings of individuals, so three is one divorce customarily and legally. As for two, since they have no rulings other than the rulings of individuals, they do not have...
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut is a detailed exposition of the Hanafi position on the imperative (صيغة الأمر). The author opens by stating the core ruling: the imperative form, according to the Hanafis, does not imply generality (عموم) nor pure number (عدد محض). This is in direct opposition to Imam al-Shafi'i (رحمه الله), who held that the imperative can bear the meaning of number. The passage then delves into the linguistic and rational arguments supporting the Hanafi view. First, the author clarifies that 'repetition' (تكرار) and 'number' (عدد) are distinct: repetition is performing the commanded act again and again, while number is the multiplicity of the act, which may occur without repetition (e.g., one utterance of divorce counts as multiple divorces due to the number of intended divorces, but not repetition). However, the Hanafis argue that the imperative demands the essence of the act (مبدأ) as such, which is a single instance. The word 'strike' (ضرب) is derived from the infinitive (مصدر) which is an indefinite noun (نكرة) and denotes a single instance (واحد). Just as 'man' (رجل) is not applied to two men, the imperative is restricted to one occurrence. The author then addresses objections: (1) the indefinite noun is for the essence, not the number one; (2) the infinitive used in the imperative may not retain its full meaning; (3) the meaning of the infinitive may be partially retained. Ultimately, the Hanafi position is that the imperative is for the essence, and compliance is achieved by one performance; additional performances are superfluous. The passage then discusses the types of unity: real unity (وحدة حقيقية) which is apparent and requires no intention, and relational unity (وحدة اعتبارية) which is unity of genus (وحدة الجنس) and requires explicit intention. This distinction is crucial in divorce (طلاق): intending three divorces for a free woman is valid because three constitutes all owned divorces, hence a unity of genus; similarly, two for a slave woman. However, intending two for a free woman is invalid because it is pure number without any aspect of unity. The author further contrasts the Hanafi view with al-Shafi'i, who allows intention of any number because he believes the imperative can bear number. Hanafi response: not every collection is a unity; the Lawgiver (الشارع) must attach specific rulings to the whole that differ from the parts. Three divorces in a free woman have unique legal effects (immediate separation, severe irrevocability) that make them a single legal unit, whereas two do not have such distinct effects, so they remain multiple. The passage thus provides a comprehensive treatment of the imperative's scope and its implications in fiqh, particularly in the context of talaq.

Key Terms
صيغة الأمر (ṣīghat al-amr): The imperative verb form as a linguistic construction used to command an action.
تكرار (takrār): Performing the commanded act repeatedly after the first performance.
عدد (ʻadad): The multiplicity or quantity of the act, which may occur without repetition (e.g., multiple divorces in one utterance).
وحدة الجنس (waḥdat al-jins): Unity of genus, where multiple items are considered one because they encompass all individuals of the genus (e.g., three divorces for a free woman as the entire owned genus).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafis, why does the imperative not imply pure number (عدد محض)?
A) Because the imperative is derived from the infinitive which is an indefinite noun, and an indefinite noun in an affirmative statement denotes a single instance.
B) Because the Quran says 'Do not ask about things', which restricts commands to one time.
C) Because the Prophet (ﷺ) became angry when asked about repetition, indicating commands are for once.
D) Because the imperative cannot include more than one act due to the nature of Arabic verbs.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafis hold that the imperative form commands a single act; it does not imply repetition or number, and any valid multiplicity (e.g., three divorces) arises from a separate Shar'i consideration like unity of genus, not from the imperative's meaning.

Critiquing the Shafi'i Cautionary Argument for Fawr
Critiquing the Shafi'i argument from caution for fawr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Shafi'i argument based on caution (ihtiyat) for immediate action and articulate the Hanafi response that unfounded doubt does not create an obligation.

Core Idea
Caution only binds when doubt is justified; unfounded doubt creates no obligation.

The Shafi'i school argues that because the command is certain and permission to delay is doubtful, one must act immediately to avoid sin. In this passage, the Hanafi commentator dismantles this argument, showing that the doubt itself is baseless since the established evidence (dalīl atamm) supports that commands do not require immediate action (fawr). We will trace the debate and understand why caution alone cannot create an obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two Shafi'i positions
The text mentions 'ghulat' (extremists) who hold that even immediate action does not guarantee compliance, and 'muqtasidun' (moderates) who hold that immediate action ensures compliance but delay does not. Understand the gradation.
The moderates' view is the basis for the caution argument.
2
Extract the Shafi'i proof from caution
The Shafi'i argument: the demand (talab) is certain, but permission to delay is doubtful (mashkūk). Therefore, caution requires immediate action to be safe from sin. Identify the core logical structure: certainty + doubt → precaution.
The doubt is about whether delay is allowed.
3
Analyze the Hanafi critique
The Hanafi commentator objects that the doubt itself is not justified. The 'most complete proof' (dalīl atamm) shows that commands indicate a common capacity (al-qadr al-mushtarak), not fawr. Thus, there is no real doubt, and caution does not apply.
The burden of proof is on the one claiming that delay is sinful.
4
Clarify the concept of 'uhdah' (liability)
The passage distinguishes between liability for the act itself (exited by performing the essence) and liability for sin (which may remain if the time is missed). The Hanafi view holds that if there is no evidence for fawr, delay does not incur sin.
This nuance is crucial for understanding the dispute.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
In acts like prayer with a wide time window, the Hanafi school permits delay within the time without sin, while the Shafi'i view would obligate immediate action to avoid potential sin. Recognize the real-world impact.
This affects daily practice and fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام على ما نقل عن البرهان أما الشافعية فذهب غلاتهم إلى أنه إن بادر عقيب الفهم لم يقطع بكونه ممتثلاً لجواز أن يكون غرض الآمر هو التأخير، وهذا شر عظيم في حكم الوقف، وذهب المقتصدون إلى أن من بادر أول الوقت كان ممتثلاً قطعاً. وإن أخر لم يقطع بخروجه عن العهدة، وهذا هو المختار، وبالجملة الذي أقطع به أن المكلف مهما أتى بالفعل فإنه بحكم الصيغة موقع للمطلوب، وإنما التوقف في أنه هل يأثم بالتأخير مع كونه ممتثلاً بأصل المطلوب ؟ انتهى . ولعل احتجاجه بأن الطلب متحقق والشك في جواز التأخير، فوجب الفور، وهذا بظاهره ،مختل، فإن الدليل إنما يدل على وجوب الفور قطعاً، وكان أول الكلام يدل على التوقف، ثم أول كلامه يدل على عدم الخروج عن العهدة وآخره على الخروج، والشك إنما هو في الإثم في التأخير لا في بقاء العهدة بالواجب، فيمكن أن يحمل كلامه على أن الأمر المطلق للقدر المشترك كما عند الجمهور، لكن الشك في أن المبادرة واجب بدليل زائد، كالأمن عن الفوات أم لا، والمراد بالعهدة تعلق الإثم، وحاصل الدليل أنه لما شك في جواز التأخير وجب الفور احتياطاً ليأمن عن الإثم، وعلى هذا لا نزاع، لكن يطالب بالدليل على الشك في الإثم بالتأخير، ويمكن أن يكون مقصوده أنه لا يدري أنه للفور أو للقدر المشترك، ففيه المبادرة يخرج عن العهدة بالواجب قطعاً، وإن أخر لم يقطع بالخروج عن العهدة، لأنه يحتمل أن يكون الفور فبقي إثم التأخير في الذمة، وإن لم يبق نفس الواجب في الذمة، والمراد بالمطلوب في قوله : فإنه بحكم الخ ... نفس الواجب، فيحكم بأنه في أدائه ممتثل والتوقف في الإثم بالتأخير فيه لأجل التوقف في تقييده بالفور، وعليه حمل كلامه وقرر دليله بقوله : (وجوب الفور، وجواز التأخير مشكوك والطلب ،محقق، فيجب البدار احتياطاً لثبوت الامتثال فيه قطعاً (إذ لو أخر فإنه وإن امتثل باعتبار إيقاع أصل المطلوب) وهو نفس الفعل لكنه يحتمل الإثم باعتبار عدم إيقاعه في زمانه فإن للفعل حيثيتين حيثية نفسه وحيثية كونه واقعاً في زمان، ففي التأخير ممتثل باعتبار أنه أداء نفسه واحتمال الإثم باعتبار إيقاعه في غير أوانه، وفيه نظر ظاهر، فإنه لما احتمل كونه للفور فإيقاعه في مؤخر ليس بإيقاع في وقته المقدر ،شرعاً، فليس فيه امتثال بحكم الصيغة والإيقاع المطلوب، نعم : لو دل دليل على وجوب القضاء كان امتثالاً له لا للأمر، فلا قطع في التأخير بالامتثال بنفس الفعل، والقضاء عنده ليس واجباً بوجوب الأداء فتأمل (قلنا: لا نسلم أنه مشكوك فإن الدليل الأتم قد دل على أنه للقدر المشترك فافهم.
The Imam said, according to what is transmitted from al-Burhan: As for the Shafi'is, their extremists (ghulat) held that if one initiates immediately after understanding, he is not certain of having complied because it is possible that the commander's purpose was delay, and this is a great evil in the ruling of waqf. The moderates (muqtasidun) held that one who initiates at the beginning of the time is certainly compliant, but if he delays, he is not certain of having fulfilled the obligation, and this is the chosen view. In summary, what I am certain of is that the legally responsible person, whenever he performs the act, he by virtue of the formula is a fulfiller of the required act. The only hesitation is whether he incurs sin by delaying despite being compliant with the essential required act. End. Perhaps his argument is that the demand (talab) is certain, and permission for delay is doubtful, so fawr is obligatory. But this in its apparent form is flawed, because the proof only indicates fawr definitively, and the beginning of his speech indicates hesitation, then the beginning indicates non-exit from responsibility and the end indicates exit. The doubt is only about sin in delay, not about the remaining of responsibility for the obligation. So his speech can be interpreted as that the absolute command is for the common capacity (al-qadr al-mushtarak) as according to the majority, but the doubt is whether early action is obligatory due to an extra proof, like safety from loss, or not. By 'responsibility' he means the attachment of sin. The gist of the proof is that since delay is doubtful, caution requires immediate action to be safe from sin. On this there is no dispute, but one demands proof for the doubt about sin in delay. It is possible that his intention is that he does not know whether it is for fawr or for common capacity. In that case, early action exits from responsibility for the obligation certainly, but if he delays, he does not exit certainly because it is possible that fawr is required, so the sin of delay remains in his liability, even if the obligation itself does not remain. And the meaning of 'required act' in his saying 'by virtue of the formula he is a fulfiller' is the obligation itself, so he is judged compliant in its performance, and hesitation about sin in delay is due to hesitation about its restriction to fawr. Thus his speech is understood and his proof is formulated by his saying: 'The obligation of fawr and permission of delay is doubtful, and the demand (talab) is certain, so early action is necessary as a precaution to ensure compliance certainly.' For if he delays, even if he complies with respect to performing the essential required act (which is the act itself), he is liable to sin with respect to not performing it in its time. For the act has two aspects: its own aspect and the aspect of it occurring in time. So in delay, he is compliant as far as performing the act itself, and possible sin as far as performing it out of its time. But this is an apparent flaw: since it is possible that the command is for fawr, performing it late is not performing it in its legally determined time, so there is no compliance by the formula and the required performance. Indeed, if a proof indicated the obligation of qada, then it would be compliance to that, not to the original command. So there is no certainty of compliance in delay by the act itself, and according to him qada is not obligatory by the same obligation as the original performance. So ponder. (We said: We do not accept that it is doubtful, for the most complete proof has indicated that it is for common capacity. So understand.)
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage discusses the Shafi'i view on fawr. The extremists say that immediate action might not be compliance if the command intended delay; the moderates say immediate action is compliance but delay is uncertain. The Imam argues that the doubt is only about sin, not compliance. The Hanafi commentator responds that this argument is flawed because the doubt about delay is not justified. The Hanafi final response: we do not accept that there is any doubt, because the established evidence (dalil atamm) shows that commands are for a common capacity (not fawr). Hence, delay does not incur sin.

Key Terms
الامتثال (al-imtithal): Compliance with a command; performing the commanded act.
العهدة (al-'uhdah): The liability or responsibility to perform an obligation; here it refers to being free from sin or obligation.
الإثم (al-ithm): Sin incurred by failing to perform an obligation or delaying without excuse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The Shafi'i argument is too extreme.
B) The doubt about delay is not justified.
C) Immediate action is always better.
D) The command is not certain.

Key Takeaway
An obligation cannot be created by unfounded doubt; the burden of proof lies with the one claiming that delay incurs sin.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Command Does Not Necessarily Imply Immediacy (Fawr)
Hanafi refutation of the Shafi'i claim that an absolute command implies immediate performance (fawr).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that an absolute command does not imply immediate performance (fawr) and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the concept of common capacity (qudra mushtaraka).

Core Idea
An absolute command is for common capacity; fawr requires independent proof.

This passage presents a foundational dispute in usul al-fiqh: does an imperative (amr) automatically require immediate action (fawr) or merely permit it? The Hanafi school, represented here, argues that the default is permissibility of delay, and fawr only becomes binding when there is additional evidence. The Shafi'i school holds a more cautious view. Understanding this dispute clarifies how different usul principles lead to different fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What does 'amr implies fawr' mean? The issue is whether the imperative form alone demands immediate compliance or allows delay.
Fawr means immediate performance without delay.
2
Present the Shafi'i position
The Shafi'i argument: the demand is certain, the permissibility of delay is doubtful, so precaution (ihtiyat) requires immediate action. This is drawn from the passage's description of the Shafi'i views.
The passage distinguishes between extremists and moderates among the Shafi'is.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi refutation holds that the default is common capacity (al-qudra al-mushtaraka). The doubt about delay is not valid because the strongest evidence (al-dalil al-atamm) indicates the command is for common capacity. The precautionary argument would require proof of sin in delay, which is lacking.
The text says: 'We do not concede that it is doubtful, for the most perfect evidence indicates that the command is for the common capacity.'
4
Examine the nuance of timing
The Shafi'i moderates differentiate between compliance with the essence of the act and compliance with its timing. The Hanafi response shows this leads to contradiction: if timing is part of the command, delay negates compliance entirely.
The passage discusses the two aspects of the action: its essence and its occurrence in time.
5
Conclude with the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is based on the most perfect evidence; it is the relied-upon view. Delay is permissible until an additional proof for fawr emerges.
The practical consequence (thamarah) is that a person who delays a commanded act without evidence for fawr is not sinful in Hanafi law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية الإيمان واجباً على الفور ، ولا يلزم كون سائر الأوامر له فتدبر، وقلنا سادساً: سلمنا أن المراد بالمسارعة المبادرة إلى الفعل، فغاية ما يلزم منه المبادرة إلى الفعل الذي هو سبب المغفرة، وقد يكون الأداء على التأخير كما في ظهر الصيف، فلا يدل على الفور أصلاً، ثم الأمر على هذا يكون للندب البتة، فإن من أسباب المغفرة ما هو مندوب فلا يجب المبادرة إليه قطعاً فتدبر قال الإمام على ما نقل عن البرهان أما الشافعية فذهب غلاتهم إلى أنه إن بادر عقيب الفهم لم يقطع بكونه ممتثلاً لجواز أن يكون غرض الآمر هو التأخير، وهذا شر عظيم في حكم الوقف، وذهب المقتصدون إلى أن من بادر أول الوقت كان ممتثلاً قطعاً. وإن أخر لم يقطع بخروجه عن العهدة، وهذا هو المختار، وبالجملة الذي أقطع به أن المكلف مهما أتى بالفعل فإنه بحكم الصيغة موقع للمطلوب، وإنما التوقف في أنه هل يأثم بالتأخير مع كونه ممتثلاً بأصل المطلوب ؟ انتهى . ولعل احتجاجه بأن الطلب متحقق والشك في جواز التأخير، فوجب الفور، وهذا بظاهره ،مختل، فإن الدليل إنما يدل على وجوب الفور قطعاً، وكان أول الكلام يدل على التوقف، ثم أول كلامه يدل على عدم الخروج عن العهدة وآخره على الخروج، والشك إنما هو في الإثم في التأخير لا في بقاء العهدة بالواجب، فيمكن أن يحمل كلامه على أن الأمر المطلق للقدر المشترك كما عند الجمهور، لكن الشك في أن المبادرة واجب بدليل زائد، كالأمن عن الفوات أم لا، والمراد بالعهدة تعلق الإثم، وحاصل الدليل أنه لما شك في جواز التأخير وجب الفور احتياطاً ليأمن عن الإثم، وعلى هذا لا نزاع، لكن يطالب بالدليل على الشك في الإثم بالتأخير، ويمكن أن يكون مقصوده أنه لا يدري أنه للفور أو للقدر المشترك، ففيه المبادرة يخرج عن العهدة بالواجب قطعاً، وإن أخر لم يقطع بالخروج عن العهدة، لأنه يحتمل أن يكون الفور فبقي إثم التأخير في الذمة، وإن لم يبق نفس الواجب في الذمة، والمراد بالمطلوب في قوله : فإنه بحكم الخ ... نفس الواجب، فيحكم بأنه في أدائه ممتثل والتوقف في الإثم بالتأخير فيه لأجل التوقف في تقييده بالفور، وعليه حمل كلامه وقرر دليله بقوله : (وجوب الفور، وجواز التأخير مشكوك والطلب ،محقق، فيجب البدار احتياطاً لثبوت الامتثال فيه قطعاً (إذ لو أخر فإنه وإن امتثل باعتبار إيقاع أصل المطلوب) وهو نفس الفعل لكنه يحتمل الإثم باعتبار عدم إيقاعه في زمانه فإن للفعل حيثيتين حيثية نفسه وحيثية كونه واقعاً في زمان، ففي التأخير ممتثل باعتبار أنه أداء نفسه واحتمال الإثم باعتبار إيقاعه في غير أوانه، وفيه نظر ظاهر، فإنه لما احتمل كونه للفور فإيقاعه في مؤخر ليس بإيقاع في وقته المقدر ،شرعاً، فليس فيه امتثال بحكم الصيغة والإيقاع المطلوب، نعم : لو دل دليل على وجوب القضاء كان امتثالاً له لا للأمر، فلا قطع في التأخير بالامتثال بنفس الفعل، والقضاء عنده ليس واجباً بوجوب الأداء فتأمل (قلنا: لا نسلم أنه مشكوك فإن الدليل الأتم قد دل على أنه للقدر المشترك فافهم.
The third discourse on linguistic principles: Faith is obligatory immediately (fawr), but it does not necessitate that all commands are for fawr, so ponder. And we said sixthly: We concede that the meaning of al-musāra‘ah is hastening to the action, but the utmost that follows from it is hastening to the action that is a cause of forgiveness, and sometimes performance is delayed as in the midday prayer in summer, so it does not indicate fawr at all. Then, the command on this basis is definitely for recommendation (nadb), for among the causes of forgiveness are recommended acts, so hastening is not obligatory absolutely, so ponder. The Imam said, as narrated from al-Burhān: As for the Shāfi‘īs, their extremists held that if one hastens immediately after understanding, one cannot be certain of being compliant because the commander’s intention may be delay, and this is a great evil in the ruling of suspension. The moderates held that one who hastens at the beginning of the time is certainly compliant, but if one delays, one is not certain of being free of liability, and this is the chosen view. In summary, what I am certain of is that whenever the obligated person performs the action, by virtue of the formula he is fulfilling the required act; the hesitation is only whether he sins by delaying while being compliant in terms of the essence of the required act. End. Perhaps his argument – that the demand is established and the permissibility of delay is doubtful, so fawr is obligatory – is outwardly flawed, for the evidence actually indicates the obligation of fawr definitively, and the beginning of his speech indicated hesitation, then the first part indicated non-exit from liability and the latter part indicated exit. The doubt is only concerning sin in delay, not concerning the liability for the obligation. So his speech can be interpreted as: the absolute command is for the common capacity (qudra mushtaraka) as per the majority, but the doubt is whether hastening is obligatory by an additional evidence like security from loss or not. ‘Liability’ refers to sin. The purport of his evidence is that since the permissibility of delay is doubtful, fawr is obligatory as a precaution to be safe from sin. On this there is no dispute, but he is required to prove the doubt concerning sin in delay. It can be that his intention is that one does not know whether the command is for fawr or for the common capacity. So in hastening one is certainly free of liability for the obligation, but if one delays one is not certain of being free of liability because it is possible that fawr is required, so the sin of delay remains in the conscience, even if the essence of the obligation is no longer in the conscience. The ‘required act’ in his statement ‘by virtue of the formula he is fulfilling the required act’ refers to the essence of the obligation. So it is judged that in performing it he is compliant, and hesitation about sin in delay is due to hesitation about its restriction by fawr. Thus his speech is interpreted and his evidence is formulated as: ‘The obligation of fawr, and the permissibility of delay is doubtful, and the demand is established, so hastening is required as a precaution to confirm compliance definitively. For if he delays, even though he complies in terms of performing the essence of the required act (the action itself), he may be sinful for not performing it in its time, for the action has two aspects: its essence and its occurrence in time. So in delay he is compliant in that he performs the essence, but possibly sinful for performing it at the wrong time.’ This is clearly flawed, because if one assumes the command is for fawr, then performing it later is not performing it in its legally appointed time, so there is no compliance by virtue of the formula and the required performance. However, if there is evidence for making up the act (qaḍā’), then it would be compliance with that (qaḍā’), not with the original command. So one cannot be certain of compliance with the act itself in delay, and in his view qaḍā’ is not obligatory with the same obligation as performance. So ponder. (We said: We do not concede that it is doubtful, for the most perfect evidence indicates that the command is for the common capacity, so understand.)
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is a detailed dispute (ikhtilāf) between the Hanafī and Shāfi‘ī schools regarding whether a command (amr) implies immediate performance (fawr) or allows delay (tarākhī). The text begins by establishing that although faith is obligatory immediately, this does not mean all commands share that property. The author then critiques the Shāfi‘ī argument that since the demand is certain and the permissibility of delay is doubtful, one must hasten as a precaution. The Hanafī refutation says: if the command is for the common capacity (al-qudra al-mushtaraka) – the default view of the majority – then delay is permissible until evidence for fawr appears. The Shāfi‘īs' precautionary argument would require proof that delay incurs sin, which they lack. The author dissects the Shāfi‘ī claim: even if the essence of the action is performed, the timing may be wrong, negating compliance. The final Hanafī rejoinder is that the strongest evidence (al-dalīl al-atamm) indeed shows the command is for the common capacity, so the doubt is resolved. The passage follows the author's order: first presenting the opposing view, then analyzing it, then rebutting, and finally affirming the Hanafī position.

Key Terms
الفور (al-fawr): Immediate performance of a commanded act without delay.
العهدة (al-ʿuhdah): Liability or responsibility for performing an obligation; here it often refers to the liability for sin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the central evidence that the Hanafi school relies upon to argue that the absolute command is for common capacity?
A) A specific Quranic verse.
B) A prophetic hadith.
C) The most perfect evidence (al-dalīl al-atamm) indicating the default.
D) Ijma of the Sahaba.

Key Takeaway
An absolute command does not imply immediate performance; it is for common capacity unless there is separate evidence for fawr.
