Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Al-Amr bi al-Amr: Commanding Someone to Command Is Not a Command to the Second
Establishing the Hanafī rule that commanding someone to command is not a command to the third party.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafī rule that 'al-amr bi al-amr' is not a command to the second party and evaluate the opposing argument using the distinction between a conveyer (muballigh) and a direct commander.

Core Idea
Commanding someone to command another is not a command to the third party; only the intermediary's command binds the second.

In this passage, the Hanafī usul rule about al-amr bi al-amr is established and defended against objections. You will learn why a statement like 'Tell your slave to sell my slave' does not directly obligate the slave. This distinction matters in the construction of a mufti's fatwa when dealing with layered commands.

Guided Path
1
State the Rule and Its Scope
Identify the chosen Hanafī position: commanding someone to command another (e.g., 'Command your slave to sell') is not a command to the third party. Note the exception when the first command uses 'qul' (say) addressed directly to the second, which is a command by agreement.
The scope excludes cases like 'Say to so-and-so: do X' which are commands to the second by consensus.
2
Examine the First Proof: Implication of Disobedience
If the utterance were a direct command to the slave, then if the master disobeys and does not command, the slave would still be commanded but would not act – thus disobedient. This consequence is false because the slave is not considered to have disobeyed the original commander.
The master is merely a messenger; the slave's obligation depends on the master's actual command.
3
Analyze the Second Proof: Contradiction with Simultaneous Prohibition
If the utterance 'Command your slave to sell' were a direct command to the slave, then saying 'Do not sell' simultaneously would create a contradiction. Rational usage permits such constructions only when the second statement is understood as abrogation or context-bound, not as a direct contradiction.
The Hanafī view resolves this by denying that the first utterance is a command to the slave.
4
Address the Objection: Analogy with God and the Prophet
Opponents analogize with God commanding the Prophet to command us, arguing that this makes God's command a command to us. The Hanafī response: the Prophet is a muballigh (conveyer), not an independent commander. The context (idhn dalālah) shows the command is mediated.
This distinction is crucial: the messenger's role changes the legal nature of the command.
5
Note the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: if a master is told 'Command your slave to sell my slave,' the slave is not directly obliged by the original speaker. The slave only becomes obliged if his own master commands him. This affects liability in transactions and agency.
This ruling protects the slave from being liable to two commanders and preserves the master's authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة 6 الأمر بالأمر بشيء لغيره (ليس أمراً) من الآمر (للثاني) الغير (على المختار، كقوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم: «مروهم بالصلاة لسبع رواه أبو داود عن عمرو بن شعیب عن أبيه عن جده مروا أولادكم بالصلاة وهم أبناء سبع فلا أمر للصبي من قبل الشارع وهذا النزاع ليس في مثل قل لفلان افعل كذا فإنه أمر للثاني من الأمر بالاتفاق، كما نقل المصنف في الحاشية عن السبكي إنما النزاع في مثل مر لفلان بكذا، وقيل: النزاع مطلق، والظاهر هو الأول، لأن المصدر بقل الخطاب فيه للثاني والمخاطب بقل مأمور بنقله، فلا يصح فيه الخلاف أصلاً فتدبر لنا كما أقول لو كان هذا أمراً للثاني (لزم معصية العبد عند معصية السيد في قوله للسيد مر عبدك أن يبيع عبدي فلم يأمر السيد فلم يبع، على هذا العبد مأمور بالبيع منه ، ولم يفعل وهو المعصية، واللازم باطل قطعاً، فإن قلت : يلتزم الخصم بعصيانه عند العلم كيف والسيد سفير ومعبر ،محض لكن عصيانه غير معتد به لعدم الولاية للآمر عليه قلت هذا مكابرة فإن العبد لا يقال له لغة وشرعاً إنه عصى أمر هذا الأمر فافهم واستدل أولاً أنه لو كان الأمر بالأمر أمر للثاني (لكان ذلك) أي مر عبدك أن عبدي (تعدياً، لأنه أمر لعبد الغير وتصرف فيه بالاستخدام، وأورد عليه أن التعدي أمر عبد الغير من غير توقف على أمر السيد وههنا أمره متوقف على أمر السيد، فالملازمة ممنوعة، وأجيب أن الكلام في أن المقدر الأمر الصادر للسيد بأمره هل هو أمر له منه، وحينئذ فلا توقف للأمر له على أمر السيد وأمره وعدم أمره سواء في تعلق الأمر للعبد من الآمر، فلزم التعدي قطعاً، فلذا غير المصنف الإيراد بوجه آخر وقال (أقول : إنما يلزم التعدي لو لم يكن أمر) الآمر للعبد (بالواسطة) أي بواسطة الأمر للسيد فإنه إذا أمر وجعل السيد سفيراً له فهو إذن دلالة وليس تعدياً، والتعدي هو التصرف في ملك الغير من غير إذنه حقيقة أو دلالة (تأمل) فإنه حقيق بالقبول (و) استدل (ثانياً) لو كان ذلك القول أمرا للعبد الكان ذلك مناقضاً لقولك للعبد : لا تبعه لأن الأمر بشيء والنهي عنه متناقضان بالضرورة والتالي باطل (ورد بمنع بطلان التالي لجواز النسخ أي لجواز أن يكون قوله : لا تبعه بعد ذلك، نسخاً له (وهو معنى المناقضة في الإنشاءات، وفيه أنه فرض هذا النهي مقارناً لذلك القول، فيكون مناقضاً، وهذا من الكلام ليس كذلك بالضرورة، فإن العقلاء يفعلون هكذا قالوا: فهم ذلك أي كون أمر الآمر أمر المأمور من أمر الله رسوله و أمر الملك وزيره بأن يأمرنا) والأوامر على منوال واحد، فيكون الكل كذلك (قلنا) إنما فهم ذلك بقرينة أنه أي الرسول أو الوزير (مبلغ) ومعبر لأمر الله أو الملك، ولا كلام فيه فافهم.
Issue 6: Commanding someone to command something for another is not a command from the commander to the second person, according to the chosen view. For example, his saying (peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions): 'Order them to pray at seven' – narrated by Abu Dawud from 'Amr ibn Shu'ayb from his father from his grandfather: 'Order your children to pray when they are seven years old.' There is no command for the child from the Lawgiver. This dispute is not like 'Say to so-and-so: do such-and-such,' for that is a command to the second person by agreement, as the author transmitted in the footnote from al-Subki. The dispute is only in cases like 'Tell so-and-so to do such-and-such.' It is said the dispute is absolute, but the apparent view is the first, because the infinitive in 'qul' addresses the second person and the one addressed by 'qul' is commanded to convey, so disagreement is invalid. So ponder. Our evidence: If this were a command to the second, then when a master disobeys in the statement to the master 'Command your slave to sell my slave' – and the master does not command, so the slave does not sell – on this view the slave is commanded to sell but did not, which is disobedience. The consequent is false. If you say: The opponent admits the slave's disobedience when he knows, but how? The master is merely a messenger and conveyer. But his disobedience is not considered due to lack of authority of the commander over him. I say this is obstinacy, for the slave is not said linguistically or legally to have disobeyed the command of this commander, so understand. First proof: If commanding someone to command were a command to the second, then it would be transgression (ta'addī), because it is a command to another's slave and using him. It was objected that transgression is commanding another's slave without depending on the master's command, but here the command depends on the master's command, so the implication is denied. The answer: The discussion is about the supposed command issued to the master by his command – is it a command from him (the commander)? In that case, the command to the slave does not depend on the master's command; whether the master commands or not is the same regarding the attachment of the command to the slave from the commander. So transgression necessarily follows. Hence the author changed the objection to another form and said: 'I say transgression would follow only if the command to the slave were not by mediation (al-wāsiṭa), i.e., through commanding the master. For if he commands and makes the master a messenger for him, that is permission by indication (idhn dalālah), not transgression. Transgression is disposing of another's property without permission, real or by indication. Ponder: it is worthy of acceptance. Second proof: If that statement were a command to the slave, it would contradict your saying to the slave: 'Do not sell it' – for commanding a thing and prohibiting it are contradictory by necessity, and the consequent is false. It was refuted by denying the falsity of the consequent, because abrogation (naskh) is possible – i.e., it is possible that his statement 'Do not sell it' later abrogates it, which is the meaning of contradiction in innovations (inshā’āt). But it is assumed that the prohibition is simultaneous with the command, so it would be contradictory. This is not the case, for rational people do this. They said: They understood that – i.e., that the commander's command is a command to the commanded from God's command to His Messenger and the king's command to his vizier to command us – and commands are on the same pattern, so all are thus. (We said:) They understood that only because of the context that the messenger or vizier is a conveyer (muballigh) and expression of God's or the king's command. There is no dispute about that, so understand.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage lays down the Hanafī rule that when someone tells another 'Command so-and-so to do X', this utterance does not constitute a direct command from the original speaker to the third party. The chosen view (al-mukhtār) is that it is not a command. The text distinguishes this from cases like 'Say to so-and-so: do such-and-such,' where by agreement it is a command to the second. Two proofs are given: First, if it were a command, then if a master disobeys, the slave would be disobedient, which is false. The objection that the master is merely a messenger is dismissed because the slave is not considered to have disobeyed the original commander. Second, if it were a command, it could lead to contradiction with a simultaneous prohibition, but rational usage shows it is permissible. The passage also addresses the analogy with God commanding the Prophet to command us: the difference is that the Prophet is a conveyer (muballigh), not a commander in his own right. The Hanafī position is that the command is mediated, so it is not transgression against the slave owner's rights.

Key Terms
الأمر بالأمر (al-amr bi al-amr): Commanding someone to command another to do something.
التعدي (al-taʿaddī): Transgression, i.e., acting in the property or rights of another without permission.
الواسطة (al-wāsiṭa): Mediation; in this context, the command is mediated through the master, making it permissible.
مبلغ (muballigh): Conveyer; one who transmits a message without independent authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen Hanafī view in this passage, what is the status of the utterance 'Command your slave to sell my slave' in relation to the slave?
A) It is a direct command to the slave to sell.
B) It is not a command to the slave; the slave is only commanded if the master commands him.
C) It is a command to the slave but depends on the master's permission.
D) It is a prohibition for the slave to sell.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafī rule: commanding someone to command another does not constitute a direct command to the third party; the intermediary's command is necessary.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
8 min
Repeated Commands: Ta'sis vs Ta'kid
Dispute on whether second of two repeated commands establishes a new obligation or emphasizes the first

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that repeated commands without conjunction indicate ta'kid (emphasis), and identify when ta'sis (establishment) applies.

Core Idea
Two identical commands without conjunction usually emphasize, not double, the obligation — unless the act can't be repeated or there's conjunction.

When a command is repeated without conjunction, does the believer owe one act or two? This usul dispute hinges on linguistic default (asl al-wad') and the presumption of freedom from obligation (bara'at al-dhimma). The Hanafi preferred view, chosen by Ibn al-Humam, is ta'kid — a single obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the repetition pattern
Determine if the commands are conjoined or not; only the non-conjoined case is disputed. If conjoined, the rule flips to ta'sis by default.
2
Apply the rule of ta'kid for non-conjoined repetition
State the Hanafi default: the second command emphasizes the first, yielding one obligation. Support with two proofs: (1) abundance of repetition in emphasis, (2) the principle of bara'at al-dhimma favors one obligation.
3
Recognize the exception: conjunction
When the second command is conjoined (e.g., with 'and' or 'then'), the default becomes ta'sis — two obligations. This follows from the absence of customary emphasis in conjoined repetition, unless an external preponderator indicates emphasis.
The text says: 'In conjunction, the second is for ta'sis.'
4
Distinguish agreed-upon cases of emphasis
Identify the three cases where repetition is emphatic by consensus: (1) the act cannot be done twice, (2) the second phrase refers to the same definite instance, (3) a contextual clue (qarina) indicates emphasis.
These are not in dispute even for opponents of the ta'kid view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة إذا تكرر أمران متعاقبان غير متعاطفين فيما يقبل التكرار بخلاف صم اليوم صم اليوم التأكيد من تعريف نحو صل الركعتين صل الركعتين (أو غيره، كاسقني (ولا صارف) من اسقني فإنه أي فإن كل واحد من الثلاثة (مؤكد (اتفاقاً أما الأول فظاهر لعدم قبول المحل للفعل مرتين، وأما الثاني فلأن المعاد معرفة عين الأول، وأما الثالث فلدلالة قرينة جزئية، كالحاجة في المثال المضروب، وهي تندفع بالأول (فقيل) الأمر الثاني (تأسيس) جزاء لقوله إذا تكرر (فالمطلوب) ههنا (الفعل) مكرراً) فالوجوب وجوبان (وقيل) الثاني (تأكيد) للأول (والمطلوب المرة من الفعل فالوجوب وجوب واحد واختاره الشيخ ابن الهمام (وقيل بالوقف) أيهما واقع (للأول) أي القائل بالتأسيس (أن) وضع الكلام للإفادة) الجديدة (لا لنفي الوهم كما في التأكيد فالتأسيس هو الأصل، فهو أولى وهو معنى ما قال الآمدي أن في التأكيد مخالفة ظاهر الأمرين من الوجوب للفعل مرتين إلى غيره أي الوجوب مرة (فما قيل) في حواشي مرزاجان) لا يلزم في التأكيد استعمال صيغة الأمر في غير معناه حتى يكون مخالفة الظاهر لأن زيد الثاني : في جاء زيد لم يدل إلا على ما دل عليه) زيد (الأول مندفع مرده خلاف الفرض من وضع التركيب وهو إفادة الفائدة الجديدة، ولا شك أنه في التوكيد لا يحصل البتة (وفيه) أي في الدليل ما فيه لأن أصالة إفادة التركيب فائدة جديدة، إنما هي في غير التكرار، وأما في التكرار فالغلبة للتأكيد ودفع الوهم (وللثاني) أي القائل بالتأكيد (كثرة التكرار في التأكيد والظن تابع للأغلب (ورجح) هذا الرأي بأن الأصل براءة الذمة ويوا التأكيد، إذ فيه الذمة مشغولة بواحد بخلاف التأسيس، فإن فيه وجوبين (وعورض بالاحتياط) فإنه في التأسيس، إذ بالعمل به يفعل مرتين وبالعمل بالتأكيد يفعل مرة، ففي الأول الخروج عن العهدة بيقين وفي الثاني احتمال الإثم لاحتمال كون الواجب الفعل مرتين، هذا وفيه كلام ظاهر، فإن الاحتياط إنما يجب فيما إذا كان الأصل الوجوب ثم طرأ المشكك من بعد، كصوم ثلاثين من شهر رمضان وقد غمّ ليلته فلم ير الهلال، وأما إذا كان الأصل عدم الوجوب فلا، كصوم يوم الشك في أنه من شعبان أو شهر رمضان وههنا لم يكن الأصل الوجوب في المرة الثانية، فليس ههنا موضع الاحتياط فتأمل، ثم إن غلبة إتيانه لا يعارضه شيء فتدبر (وفي العطف) أي فيما إذا كان الثاني معطوفاً على الأول يكون الثاني للتأسيس) فيجب الفعل مرتين (وهو الوجه، لأن التأكيد فيه أي في العطف (لم يعهد فيعمل بهما إلا بمرجح من خارج) يصرفه إلى التأكيد. ويوافقه مسألة
Issue: If two commands occur consecutively without conjunction in something that admits repetition, unlike 'fast today, fast today' which is emphasis (ta'kid) by definition, similar to 'pray two rak'ahs, pray two rak'ahs' or other, like 'give me a drink, give me a drink' without a disambiguating factor, then each of the three is emphatic by agreement. As for the first, it is apparent because the locus does not admit the act twice. As for the second, because the repeated word is a definite reference to the first. As for the third, because of a partial indicative context, like need in the example given, which is repelled by the first. So it was said: the second command is establishment (ta'sis) as a consequence of his statement 'if repeated'; so what is required here is the act repeated, so the obligation is two obligations. And it was said: the second is emphasis (ta'kid) of the first, and what is required is one instance of the act, so the obligation is one obligation; and Shaykh Ibn al-Humam chose this. And it was said to stop (waqf) as to which is actual. For the first (the one saying ta'sis), the argument is that speech is placed for new benefit, not for negation of illusion as in emphasis; so ta'sis is the original, so it is more appropriate. And this is the meaning of what al-Amidi said: that in emphasis there is opposition to the apparent of the two commands from obligation to act twice to something else, i.e., obligation once. And what was said in the marginal notes of Mirzajan: that in emphasis it is not necessary to use the imperative form in other than its meaning such that it would be opposition to the apparent, because 'Zayd' the second in 'Zayd came' does not indicate except what 'Zayd' the first indicated — this is refuted, its refutation is contrary to the assumption of the composition's purpose, which is to convey new benefit, and there is no doubt that in emphasis it is absolutely not obtained. And within it (the proof) is what is in it, because the principle that composition conveys new benefit is only in non-repetition, but in repetition the dominant is emphasis and negation of illusion. And for the second (the one saying ta'kid), the argument is the abundance of repetition in emphasis, and conjecture follows the more frequent. And this view was preferred because the principle is the immunity of the dhimma, and it agrees with emphasis, for in it the dhimma is occupied with one, unlike ta'sis, which involves two obligations. And it was objected with precaution (ihtiyat): precaution is in ta'sis, because by acting on it one does it twice, and by acting on emphasis one does it once; so in the first there is certainty of discharge, and in the second possibility of sin due to the possibility that the obligatory is two acts. This contains apparent discussion, because precaution is only necessary when the original is obligation and then uncertainty arises later, like fasting thirty days of Ramadan when the night is cloudy and the crescent is not seen. But when the original is non-obligation, it is not, like fasting the day of doubt whether it is Sha'ban or Ramadan. Here, there was no original obligation for the second time, so there is no place for precaution. Reflect. Then, the dominance of its occurrence is not opposed by anything. So consider. And in conjunction (al-'atf): i.e., when the second is conjoined to the first, the second is for ta'sis, so the act must be done twice. And this is the correct view, because emphasis in conjunction has not been customary; so one acts on both unless there is an external preponderator turning it to emphasis. And it agrees with the issue.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage addresses a key usul dispute: when two identical commands are repeated without conjunction (like 'pray two rak'ahs, pray two rak'ahs'), does the second command establish a new obligation (ta'sis) or merely emphasize the first (ta'kid)? The author presents three cases where repetition is clearly emphatic by agreement: (1) when the act cannot be done twice (e.g., 'fast today' twice is impossible), (2) when the second refers to the same definite instance, and (3) when a contextual clue indicates emphasis. In the general case, three views exist: the second is ta'sis (obligating two acts), ta'kid (one obligation), or waqf (suspense). Ibn al-Humam chose ta'kid. Arguments for ta'sis: the default is that speech gives new benefit, and ta'sis follows that principle; emphasis contradicts the apparent meaning of two obligations. Arguments for ta'kid: repetition is most often used for emphasis, and the principle of bara'at al-dhimma (presumed innocence of liability) favors one obligation. The precaution argument for ta'sis is rejected here because there is no original obligation for the second act. Finally, if the second command is conjoined with 'and' or 'then' (al-'atf), it becomes ta'sis by default, requiring two acts, unless a preponderating factor indicates emphasis. This analysis shows Hanafi usul's careful balancing of linguistic principles (asl wad' al-kalam li al-ifada) and legal maxims (al-bara'at al-asliyya).

Key Terms
التأسيس (at-ta'sis): The second command establishes a new obligation, requiring the act to be done twice.
التأكيد (at-ta'kid): The second command emphasizes the first, resulting in a single obligation.
براءة الذمة (bara'at adh-dhimma): The legal presumption that the dhimma (liability) is free from obligation until proven otherwise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a repeated command when the second command is conjoined (e.g., 'do X and do X')?
A) The second is ta'kid (emphasis), so only one act is required.
B) The second is ta'sis (establishment), so two acts are required.
C) It is waqf, pending further evidence.
D) It is prohibited to repeat commands.

Key Takeaway
Repeated commands without conjunction default to emphasis (ta'kid) in the Hanafi school, but conjunction flips the default to establishment (ta'sis).

Repetition of Command: Ta'sis vs Ta'kid – The Hanafi Usul Debate
Analyzes debate on whether repeated imperative establishes new obligation or emphasis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three positions on repeated commands, explain the arguments for ta'sis and ta'kid, and apply the rule that conjunctive commands default to ta'sis.

Core Idea
When a command is repeated, default is a new obligation (ta'sis), not mere emphasis (ta'kid), unless there is a preponderant for ta'kid.

This lesson tackles a fundamental usul problem: when the Lawgiver says 'do it' twice, is the second command a new independent obligation or just an emphasis? The Hanafi school analyses this through linguistic principles and practical implications. Understanding the debate sharpens your ability to interpret repeated legal texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Read the passage and list the three views: ta'sis (new obligation), ta'kid (emphasis), and waqf (suspension). Note that Ibn al-Humam chose ta'kid.
Also note the author's own conclusion for conjunctive commands.
2
Examine the ta'sis argument
Find the linguistic principle: speech is for new benefit (wad' al-kalam li al-ifada al-jadida). Understand why this supports ta'sis.
Critique: this principle applies only when there is no repetition.
3
Examine the ta'kid argument
Identify the two proofs: frequency of repetition in emphasis, and the original innocence of liability (bara'at al-dhimmah).
The caution counterargument is rejected because caution requires prior obligation.
4
Analyse the naqd (critique)
Follow the passage's critique of the ta'sis argument: the principle of new benefit is not absolute; in repetition, prevalence is for ta'kid.
Consider the hashia's point about the imperative not changing meaning, and how it is refuted.
5
Apply the rule for conjunction
Note that when the second command is conjoined (with 'waw'), ta'kid is not customary, so the default is ta'sis unless an external preponderant indicates otherwise.
This is the preponderated view according to the author.

Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is a usul rule discussion: whether repeating a command (e.g., 'do it! do it!') establishes a new obligation (ta'sis) or is merely emphasis (ta'kid). The author, commenting on the matn, presents the debate. First group: the second command is ta'sis, requiring two acts, so two obligations. Second group: it is ta'kid, requiring only one act, one obligation—this view was chosen by Ibn al-Humam. A third view: suspend judgment. The proponent of ta'sis argues that language is set for new benefit, not negation of misconception; ta'kid violates this principle. A response from glosses of Mirzajan? is rejected. The proponent of ta'kid argues that repetition is prevalent in ta'kid, so conjecture follows prevalence; also the original is innocence of liability (dhimma), so ta'kid is lighter. Counterargument: caution favors ta'sis because it ensures exit from liability. But the author notes caution applies only when original is obligation then doubt arises, not here. The preponderated view: when commands are conjoined (with 'waw'), the second is ta'sis, requiring two acts, unless an external preponderant indicates ta'kid. This is the sound opinion.

Key Terms
تأسيس (ta'sis): The second command establishes a new, independent obligation for the same act, requiring repetition.
تأكيد (ta'kid): The second command only emphasizes the first, without adding a new obligation; one instance suffices.
وقف (waqf): Suspending judgment on whether the repeated command establishes a new obligation or emphasis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Ta'sis
B) Ta'kid
C) Waqf
D) Conjunctive default

Key Takeaway
The default for a repeated command is ta'sis (new obligation) except when there is a preponderant for ta'kid, and conjunctive commands are always ta'sis unless overridden.

Debate on Qada' After Valid Zann: Hanafi vs Mu'tazila
Debate on whether qada' is required after performing obligation with valid zann

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Mu'tazilite dichotomy and articulate the Hanafi third option regarding the requirement of qada' after performing an obligation based on valid zann.

Core Idea
The command is for the real condition; zann excuses sin but does not waive qada'.

This passage presents a classic usul dispute between the Hanafi school and Abd al-Jabbar's Mu'tazilites on whether an act performed with valid opinion (zann) must be repeated if the opinion turns out wrong. The Mu'tazilites pose a forced dichotomy; the Hanafi response breaks out of it by distinguishing between the commanded condition and the excuse for the agent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing dichotomy
The Mu'tazilites argue that either the command requires certainty (sin + qada') or zann suffices (no qada'). Understand this forced choice.
This is a classical straw-man approach; the Hanafi response rejects the dichotomy.
2
Recognize the Hanafi third option
The Hanafi response selects a third possibility: the commanded act requires real condition (e.g., real tahara), but zann is a valid excuse. If zann corresponds, it's sufficient; if not, qada' is obligatory but no sin.
This is the core Hanafi position.
3
Follow the refutation of alternative answers
The text critiques three other attempted answers: (1) waiver based on dispute, (2) qada' as a new obligation, (3) suspended status of the act. Each is shown to be weak or inconsistent.
This demonstrates the rigor of Hanafi usul.
4
Compare the thamarah al-khilaf in practice
Apply to a concrete scenario: a person prays with zann of wudu, then discovers hadath. Under Hanafi: qada' wajib, no sin. Under Mu'tazila: either sin and qada' or no sin and no qada'.
The practical difference is clear in fatwa.
5
Assess the epistemological implication
The passage ends with a critique: if the fourth answer were accepted, there would be no difference between correct and mistaken zann, contradicting the hadith on ijtihad rewards.
This shows the issue's depth and the need for a consistent epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الموجبين للأداء ثانياً قد اتفقوا على أنها تؤدى بنية القضاء، فلا صحة لهذا الجواب بوجه فافهم. والجواب رابعاً على ما قيل: الأمر بظن الطهارة ما دام الظن باقياً وإلا فبإتيان المثل هذا أيضاً اختيار للشق لأن العمل متى صار مسلماً إلى صاحب الحق الذي هو أكرم وأرحم على العبد لا يجعله معدوماً وممحواً من ديوان الثواب، قلت: إن الصلاة المؤداة ليست صحيحة ولا فاسدة بل حالها موقوفة فإن استمر الظن تكون صحيحة، وإلاّ لا، فتبقى الذمة مشغولة فيجب القضاء لتفريغها وهذا ليس من الانقلاب في شيء بل مثله كمثل سلام من عليه السهو، فإنه يخرجه خروجاً موقوفاً فافهم. أقول: لو تم هذا الجواب لم يكن فرق بين الظن المخالف للواقع والمطابق له لأن الأمر ليس إلا بظن الطهارة الباقي على ما سلم، فهذا الظن إن كان خطأ ولم يظهر إلى أن مات فقد امتثل فيلزم أن يكون الأجر في المخالف والمطابق على السوية وقولهم في الاجتهاد إن للمخطىء أجراً وللمصيب أجرين مطابقاً لحديث حكي في الصحيحين: إذا حكم حاكم فاجتهد ثم أصاب فله أجران فإذا حكم فاجتهد ثم أخطأ فله أجر واحد يدل على خلافه لأنه يوجب عدم المساواة في الأجر فتدبر. وهذا قياس بعمل جزئي على إخراج حكم كلي واجب العمل إلى يوم القيامة.
Abd al-Jabbar and his followers said: If performing the commanded act perfectly would require qada', then the one who prays with a zann of tahara would be sinful and qada' would be waived when it becomes clear that hadath occurred after the time, because if he is commanded to pray with certainty of tahara and he did not do so with certainty, he sins because he left the commanded act—that is the first possibility. And if zann sufficed for the commanded prayer, then he performed as commanded, so qada' is waived—that is the second possibility. The answer: First: I say: The command concerns real tahara; that is, we choose a third possibility: the commanded act is prayer with real tahara, but zann of its existence is sufficient, and the correctness of the zann is because it is evidence with correspondence. If it corresponds to reality, then it is sufficient; otherwise, qada' is obligatory because he did not perform the commanded act with its condition, and he is not sinful due to the excuse of zann because it is what is within his capacity, and error in it is not due to his negligence, like error and forgetfulness which remove sin. So understand. Second answer: It is denied that qada' is waived, because the issue is disputed; one could choose the waiver and say the command was with zann of tahara, but the issue is agreed upon by the majority, so this answer does not come from them. Third answer: That qada' is a new obligation by another command and is not qada' in reality, and the first obligation has fallen. Apparently this answer chooses the second possibility, meaning he was commanded to perform with zann of tahara, and he performed, so it has fallen, and qada' also has fallen, and this is another obligation. As in al-Mukhtasar, but it contains what it contains because it is not known in the Shari'ah for Fajr, for example, a duty other than ada' and qada'. Even if granted, this applies to every qada', so no qada' exists in reality. This second is extremely weak, for qada' was legislated to free a dhimmah that was occupied by the obligation due to its loss, unlike our case because if it is conceded that the one with zann of tahara performed as commanded, nothing remains on the dhimmah for qada' to release. It may be answered first: This is ada' that follows the first ada' by command, and the second is from something else, and this does not repel the objection, for such a sequence is not known in the Shari'ah at all. And if it is said that this prayer exists, it is a citation of the disputed matter. Second: That it is qada' even if metaphorically, so it is not unknown. This is extremely absurd, for there is no dispute about naming, and the second performed act is not qada' in reality nor ada', so the unknown necessarily follows. Moreover, those who obligate a second performance have agreed that it is performed with the intention of qada', so this answer is unsound in every aspect. So understand. Fourth answer according to what was said: The command is with zann of tahara as long as the zann remains, otherwise by performing the like. This also chooses the second possibility, but not absolutely; rather with the remaining zann as long as its error does not appear, even if it is erroneous in reality. After the error appears, qada' is necessary and there is no sin because he is not negligent. If you say: The first command on this assumption necessitates prayer with zann of tahara, and he performed it, so the obligation of qada' is either by the first cause which has ended and does not necessitate qada', or by another cause, and it has been stated that qada' is by the first cause. And because what was performed became invalid due to the invalidity of the zann, and validity was conditioned on its remaining, this invalidation is incorrect because when a deed reaches its Owner who is more Generous and Merciful to the servant, He does not make it non-existent and erase it from the register of reward. I say: The performed prayer is neither valid nor invalid; rather its status is suspended. If the zann continues, it is valid; otherwise not. So the dhimmah remains occupied, so qada' is obligatory to discharge it, and this is not invalidation at all; rather it is like the case of one who says salam while he has forgetfulness, for his exit is suspended. So understand. I say: If this answer were complete, there would be no difference between the zann that contradicts reality and the one that corresponds to it, because the command is only with the remaining zann of tahara as conceded. So if this zann is erroneous and does not appear until he dies, he has complied, so it follows that the reward for the mistaken and the correct is equal. And their saying in ijtihad that the mistaken has one reward and the correct has two rewards corresponds to a hadith narrated in the Sahihain: 'When a judge judges and strives and hits, he has two rewards; when he judges and strives and misses, he has one reward.' It indicates the opposite because it necessitates inequality in reward. So reflect. And this is an analogy of a particular act to the extraction of a universal ruling that is binding until the Day of Judgment.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute about qada' of an obligation performed based on zann (preponderant opinion). Abd al-Jabbar and his followers (Mu'tazila) argue by dichotomy: either the command requires certainty—then if one prays with zann he is sinful and must repeat—or zann suffices—then he has performed as commanded and qada' is waived. The Hanafi author responds by rejecting the dichotomy and selecting a third possibility: the command is for the act with real condition (e.g., real tahara), but zann is a valid excuse. If the zann matches reality, it is sufficient; if not, qada' is required but no sin. He then critiques the second answer (that the issue is disputed and one could claim waiver) and the third answer (that qada' is a new obligation), arguing they are inconsistent with shar'i precedent. The fourth answer distinguishes between zann that remains until death versus error that becomes apparent, and discusses the suspended status of the performed act. The author concludes that even this answer fails to differentiate reward between correct and mistaken zann, citing the hadith on ijtihad rewards. The passage demonstrates a typical Hanafi usul method of rational argumentation and refutation of opposing views, rooted in the concept of istihsan and the suspension of rulings pending clarification.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qada'): The performance of an obligation a second time to free the dhimmah, originally commanded in the first order
الظن (az-zann): Preponderant belief without certainty, considered a valid excuse in ritual law when certainty is unattainable
الاستحقاق (al-istiḥqāq): The right of the Owner (God) over the servant's action; once accepted, it is not nullified

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response to Abd al-Jabbar, what is the ruling for a person who prayed with zann of tahara and later discovered hadath after the time?
A) The prayer is valid, no qada' and no sin
B) Qada' is required but no sin because the person has a valid excuse
C) The prayer is invalid, but no qada' because the person did his best
D) Qada' is required and the person is sinful for not ensuring certainty

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, performing an obligation based on valid zann excuses from sin but does not waive the duty of qada' if the real condition is later found absent.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
Qada When Dhan of Purity is Proven Wrong: The Hanafi Principle of Suspended Validity
Dispute on qada when dhan of purity is proven wrong

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of suspended validity (al-'amal al-mawquf) to cases where an act of worship is performed based on dhan and later found erroneous, and derive the ruling on qada.

Core Idea
An act with dhan is valid only while dhan persists; error discovery demands qada with no sin.

This passage addresses a critical usul dispute: what happens when you perform a worship act relying on a speculative belief (dhan) that later turns out to be false? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced answer: the act's validity is suspended pending the persistence of that dhan. Understanding this ruling clarifies when reparation (qada) is required and why the performer is not sinful. The discussion involves multiple attempted answers, refutations, and a key qiyas to the suspended exit (khuruj mawquf) of one with forgetfulness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Contested Issue
Recognise that the debate centres on whether qada is obligatory when an act (e.g., prayer) performed with dhan of purity (thinking one has wudu) is later proven to have been invalid due to actual impurity. The two main positions: some hold qada is not required (the act was performed as commanded), while the Hanafi position insists qada becomes necessary once error is known, but no sin is incurred.
The passage also discusses sub-answers before settling on the fourth.
2
Examine the Hanafi Argument of Suspended Validity
The fourth answer (and the preferred one) posits that the performed act is neither valid nor invalid; its status is suspended until the dhan is proven wrong. If the dhan persists (even if factually wrong) the act is valid; if error appears, the act becomes invalid and qada is required. This is not a 'change' but a condition attached to the act's validity.
The analogy of the salam (salutation) of one who has forgetfulness: his exit from prayer is suspended pending whether he remembers or not.
3
Trace the Qiyas to the Case of Forgetfulness
The Hanafi argument uses qiyas: just as a forgetful person's salutation is a suspended exit (if he remembers before leaving, he must return), so too the act based on dhan is suspended in validity. The asl is the well-known ruling that forgetting does not nullify but suspends. The far' is the act with dhan. The illa is the absence of full certainty at the time of action.
This qiyas is considered jali (clear) because the shared illa is evident.
4
Refute the Opposing Use of the Ijtihad Hadith
Opponents cite the hadith about judges differing in reward (correct judge gets two rewards, mistaken gets one) to argue that error in dhan should also affect reward. The commentator distinguishes: that hadith applies to ijtihad in universal rulings (ahkam kulliya), not to individual acts of worship. Acts based on dhan are not subject to such graduated reward when performed with sincerity.
The passage stresses that considering error in particular acts would cause great hardship (haraj), which the Sharia does not intend.
5
Derive the Practical Ruling
From the suspended validity principle, the practical ruling is: if someone prays with dhan of purity and later discovers he was without wudu, he must repeat that prayer (qada) but is not sinful. If the dhan persists until death, the prayer is valid and no qada is needed. This ruling applies analogously to other acts based on dhan, such as mistaking the direction of qibla.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Like error and forgetfulness, sin is removed by them. Understand. (And) the second answer: He denies the invalidity of dropping the qada, because the issue is subject to disagreement. I say: The performed prayer is neither valid nor invalid, but its status is suspended. If the dhan continues, it is valid; otherwise not. So the liability remains occupied, so qada is necessary to clear it. This is not a change at all, but its example is like the salutation of one who has forgetfulness: he exits in a suspended exit. So if this dhan is erroneous and does not appear until he dies, he has complied, so it would necessitate that the reward for the contrary and the conforming be equal. And their statement in ijtihad that the mistaken one has one reward and the correct one has two rewards) conforms to a hadith narrated in the two Sahihs: 'When a judge judges and makes ijtihad and then is correct, he has two rewards; when he judges and makes ijtihad and then errs, he has one reward.' This indicates the opposite, because it necessitates inequality in reward. (So ponder.) And this is a qiyas on a particular action for deriving a universal ruling obligatory to act upon until the Day of Resurrection. For correctness of the conforming ruling is possible by the Lawgiver establishing evidence or sign upon it, so error in it is due to heedlessness. So there is no improbability in disparity between correctness and error. As for the particular action, if error were considered in it, great hardship would occur, and it is not possible to avoid it with the absence of sincerity that necessitates reward. Do you not see that acting upon an erroneous ruling derived by ijtihad does not decrease reward at all? So likewise is this. Understand.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of a dispute in usul al-fiqh regarding the ruling on qada (making up) for an act performed based on dhan (speculative belief) that later turns out to be wrong. The specific case is someone who prays with dhan of purity (i.e., he thinks he has wudu) but later discovers he was not pure. The question: Is qada required? The text presents multiple answers and refutations. The first answer: He is not sinful because error is excused. The second answer: The issue is disputed; one may choose the view that qada is not required. The third answer: Qada is a new obligation, not the original one. The fourth answer: The command with dhan continues as long as the dhan remains; if error appears, qada becomes necessary. The commentator critiques these answers, ultimately arguing that the performed act is suspended in validity: if dhan persists, it is valid; if not, it becomes invalid and qada is required. This is analogous to a salutation by someone forgetful, which is a suspended exit. The passage also cites a hadith about ijtihad to argue that reward differs for correct vs. erroneous judgments, but that is about ijtihad in issuing rulings, not about individual acts of worship. The commentator distinguishes between universal rulings and particular actions, asserting that error in particular actions does not diminish reward due to sincerity. The discussion emphasizes the Hanafi position that the validity of an act based on dhan is contingent on the persistence of that dhan, and once error is known, reparation is required.

Key Terms
الظن (al-dhann): Speculative belief or predominant opinion that does not reach certainty
القضاء (al-qada'): Making up or performing an obligation after its time has passed due to a valid excuse
الإثم (al-ithm): Sin or culpability for violating a command
الموقوف (al-mawquf): Suspended; a term used for an act whose validity is conditional on a future event or state

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the ruling on an act performed with dhan of purity when the dhan is later discovered to be wrong?
A) The act is valid and no qada is required.
B) The act is invalid and qada is required immediately.
C) The act is suspended: if the dhan had persisted, it would be valid; after error, qada becomes necessary.
D) The act is valid but sinful.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi view: an act based on dhan has suspended validity; if dhan persists, it is valid; if error appears, qada is required and no sin.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Does Prohibition Linguistically Imply Invalidity?
Dispute over whether prohibition linguistically indicates corruption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that prohibition does not linguistically imply corruption and to evaluate the counterargument from the opposing school.

Core Idea
Prohibition demands refraining; it does not entail that the forbidden act is void.

Is a forbidden act automatically invalid? This Usul al-Fiqh debate examines whether the linguistic form of prohibition (صيغة النهي) necessarily implies that the act is corrupt (فساد). The Hanafi school argues that language itself does not force this connection. In this lesson, you will trace the reasoning and understand the practical effect on fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested claim
State clearly what each side asserts: does the word of prohibition linguistically imply that the prohibited act is corrupt (lacking legal effects)? The chosen view in the text is that it does not.
Corruption here means non-occurrence of rulings (عدم ترتب الأحكام).
2
State the Hanafi proof
Explain the Hanafi reasoning: prohibition only demands refraining (طلب الترك); it does not address the legal effects of the act if performed. A linguistic test: one can say 'If you do this, its rulings apply, but do not do it' without contradiction.
This shows refraining is separate from invalidity.
3
State the opposing proof
The opponent argues: command implies validity; prohibition is the opposite of command; so prohibition must imply the opposite of validity, i.e., corruption.
This is an argument from logical symmetry (naqid).
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the implications of opposites are not necessarily opposite. Command implies validity, but prohibition does not have to imply invalidity; it may simply not imply validity.
The text offers multiple rebuttals including the distinction between implication and non-implication.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If prohibition does not imply corruption, then a prohibited act (e.g., a sale with a prohibited condition) may still be valid in its intended effect, but blameworthy. The act is haram but its legal effect (ownership transfer) can still occur.
This shapes Hanafi rulings on forbidden transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة النهي هل يدل على الفساد وهو عدم ترتب الحكم عليه (لغة) أم لا يدل (المختار لا) يدل لغة عليه، وقيل : يدل لنا أن فساد الشيء عبارة عن سلب أحكامه، وليس المدلول (في لفظ النهي سوى طلب الترك) حتماً (وهو لا يستلزم ذلك أي عدم ترتب الأحكام (قطعاً) وكيف لا ومن البين أنه لو قال : إذا فعلت هذا الشيء ترتبت أحكامه، لكن لا تفعل، إن فعلت عاقبتك، لم يعد مناقضاً لغة وعرفاً، ولم يحكم بتغير النهي عن موضعه، وإنكار هذا مكابرة، القائلون بالفساد لغة (قالوا: الأمر يقتضي (الصحة) وهو ظاهر جداً فإنه لا يأمر عاقل بأمر لا يكون هو صحيحاً ومعتبراً عنده والنهي نقيضه، ومقتضى النقيض نقيض المقتضى) فمقتضى النهي نقيض مقتضى الأمر وهو الفساد (والجواب : أن المتقابلين لا يجب تقابل أحكامهما فلا نسلم أن مقتضى النقيض نقيض المقتضى ؛ فيجوز أن يكون مقتضى الأمر الصحة غیر اقتضاء مع النهي الفساد، بل مع اقتضاء الصحة كيف ومقتضى الأمر إيقاع الفعل، ولا يكون واقعاً إلا مترتب الآثار، بخلاف النهي، فإنه يقتضي الكفَّ عنه وهو لا يقتضي عدم ترتب الآثار فافهم على أن للاقتضاء مع عدم الاقتضاء نوعاً من (التقابل فحينئذ يجوز أن يكون الأمر مقتضياً للصحة والنهي لا يكون مقتضياً لها وفيه أيضاً تناف في الأحكام، وفيه أن المستدل ادعى وجوب التنافي بين مقتضييهما ومقتضى الأمر الصحة فيكون مقتضى النهي منافيها وهو الفساد، فإن منع وجوب التنافي بينهما فهو الوجه الأول، وإلا فليس في اليد منع فتدبر. وقد يجاب بأن هذا قياس في اللغة ورد بأنه استدلال باستقراء حال المتنافيين فتدبر، وقد يجاب أيضاً بمنع اقتضاء الأمر لغة الصحة فإنه لطلب المأخذ ولا يوجب ترتب الأحكام وإنما يجب شرعاً أو عقلاً وفيه أنه لطلب الإيقاع للمأخذ في العين، وظاهر أن الوجودية هي ترتب الثمرات والآثار فهو مقتض لترتب الثمرات في نظر الآمر ، وإلا لما صح منه طلب الإيقاع فتأمل فيه .
Issue: Does prohibition indicate corruption, i.e., non-occurrence of the ruling upon it, linguistically or not? The chosen view is no, it does not indicate linguistically. And it is said it does. For us: the corruption of a thing is the deprivation of its rulings, and the indicated meaning in the word of prohibition is only the demand to refrain decisively, and it does not necessitate that, i.e., non-occurrence of rulings, absolutely. How not? And it is clear that if one says: If you do this thing, its rulings occur, but do not do it; if you do, I will punish you, it would not be considered contradictory linguistically or customarily, nor would it be judged that the prohibition has changed from its place. Denial of this is stubbornness. Those who say it indicates corruption linguistically say: The command implies validity, and that is very apparent, for no rational person commands a thing that is not valid and considered in his view, and prohibition is its opposite, and the implication of the opposite is the opposite of the implication. So the implication of prohibition is the opposite of the implication of command, which is corruption. The answer: Two opposites do not necessarily have opposite rulings, so we do not accept that the implication of the opposite is the opposite of the implication. So it is possible that the implication of command is validity without the implication with prohibition being corruption. Rather, with the implication of validity, how? The implication of command is the occurrence of the action, and it is not actual except with consequences, unlike prohibition, for it implies refraining from it, and it does not imply non-occurrence of consequences. So understand that between implication and non-implication there is a kind of opposition, so then it is possible that the command implies validity and the prohibition does not imply it. And in it also there is contradiction in rulings. And in it: the arguer claimed the necessity of contradiction between their implications, and the implication of command is validity, so the implication of prohibition would be contradictory to it, which is corruption. If one denies the necessity of contradiction between them, that is the first aspect; otherwise, there is no denial in hand, so ponder. And it may be answered that this is analogy in language, and it is met by that it is inference by induction of the condition of the two opposites, so ponder. And it may also be answered by denying that command linguistically implies validity, for it is a demand for the subject matter and does not necessitate the occurrence of rulings, rather it is obligatory legally or rationally. And in it: it is a demand for the occurrence of the subject matter in the entity, and it is apparent that existence entails the fruition and effects, so it necessitates the fruition in the view of the commander, otherwise his demand for occurrence would not be valid. So ponder over it.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute regarding whether the linguistic meaning of prohibition (النهي) implies corruption (الفساد), i.e., that the prohibited act does not produce its intended legal effects. The chosen view (المختار) in the text is that it does not. The argument is that prohibition only demands refraining (طلب الترك), and refraining does not entail that the act, if done, would be devoid of its rulings. A clear example is given: one can say 'If you do this thing, its rulings apply, but do not do it; if you do, I will punish you' — this is not contradictory linguistically. Those who argue for implication claim that command implies validity, and prohibition being its opposite, must imply invalidity. The Hanafi response, explained in the text, is that opposites do not necessarily have opposite implications; it is possible that command implies validity while prohibition does not imply invalidity. The response also notes that command implies occurrence of the act with consequences, while prohibition only implies refraining, not non-occurrence of consequences. Additional counterarguments are presented, including that the opponents' reasoning is based on qiyas (analogy) in language, which is refuted. The passage concludes with a nuanced discussion about the nature of demand and existence, urging careful reflection.

Key Terms
الفساد (al-fasad): عدم ترتب الأحكام عليه (non-occurrence of rulings upon it), i.e., invalidity
الاقتضاء (al-iqtida'): الطلب (demand) as implying something necessarily
النقيض (al-naqid): The opposite or contradictory

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen view in this passage, does the prohibition form (صيغة النهي) linguistically imply that the prohibited act is corrupt (باطل)?
A) Yes, always
B) No, never
C) It depends on the context
D) Only in matters of worship

Key Takeaway
The mere existence of a prohibition does not linguistically or logically imply that the prohibited act is invalid; the Hanafi school maintains this distinction to allow for valid but sinful acts.

Does Prohibition Imply Invalidity? The Hanafi View
Dispute over whether prohibition indicates corruption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on whether prohibition implies corruption and distinguish between intrinsic and extrinsic corruption in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Prohibition does not void an act; a forbidden sale is still valid but sinful.

In this passage, we encounter a classic usul dispute: does the legal prohibition of an act automatically render it invalid? The Hanafi school takes a nuanced position, distinguishing between the act's sinfulness and its legal validity. Understanding this distinction clarifies many rulings in fiqh, especially in transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The central question is whether the legal prohibition (al-nahy) indicates corruption (al-fasad) such that the prohibited act is legally ineffective.
The text frames it as 'هل النهي يدل على الفساد شرعاً'.
2
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that prohibition does not necessitate corruption; rather, it necessitates validity in both worship and transactions. The act is sinful but legally effective.
The passage notes that the author will choose this view.
3
Identify opposing positions
Some scholars (al-Ghazali, al-Razi) say prohibition implies corruption absolutely or only in worship. Others (like Ibn al-Humam) say it indicates validity in transactions.
These positions are presented as alternatives.
4
Analyze the key distinction
The text distinguishes between intrinsic corruption (fasad bi-dhat) – which is nullity – and extrinsic corruption (fasad bi-ghayr) – which is sinfulness. The Hanafi view rejects intrinsic corruption from prohibition alone.
This distinction is crucial: 'الفساد بالذات' vs. 'بالغير'.
5
Note the practical consequence
For Hanafis, a prohibited sale (e.g., selling alcohol) transfers ownership but is sinful; for others, it is null and void. This difference has real-world effects.
The thamarah al-khilaf lies in contract validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة (النهي هل يدل على الفساد شرعاً) أم لا (المختار (نعم) يدل مطلقاً في العبادات والمعاملات وقيل : يدل في العبادات فقط دون المعاملات (وإليه (مال) الإمام حجة الإسلام (الغزالي) والإمام (الرازي) وصاحب هذا المذهب قائل بعدم دلالته في المعاملات على الفساد وعند البعض فيها يدل على الصحة واختاره الشيخ ابن الهمام ثم ههنا بحث، لأنه إن أراد الفساد بالذات وهو عدم ترتب الثمرات الذي هو البطلان في اصطلاحنا ففيه أن الحنفية ذهبوا إلى أنه لا يقتضي الفساد، بل يقتضي الصحة في الشرعيات عبادات كانت أو معاملات، وسيختاره المصنف، فلا يصح ههنا قوله : المختار ،نعم وإن أراد أعم من أن يكون الفساد بالذات أو بالغير وهو ما يوجب ارتكابه الإثم لأجل الذات أو مقارنة وصف أو مجاور، فلا
Issue: Does prohibition legally indicate corruption or not? The chosen view: yes, it indicates absolutely in acts of worship and transactions. And it is said it indicates only in acts of worship, not in transactions. And al-Imam Hujjat al-Islam (al-Ghazali) and al-Imam (al-Razi) inclined to this. And the follower of this madhhab says it does not indicate in transactions. And according to some, it indicates validity in them, and Sheikh Ibn al-Humam chose this. Then there is a discussion here: because if by fasad he means intrinsic corruption, which is non-occurrence of fruits – which is nullity in our terminology – then there is that the Hanafis have gone to that it does not necessitate corruption, rather it necessitates validity in legal matters, whether acts of worship or transactions, and the author will choose this. So here his statement 'the chosen view: yes' is not correct. And if he means more general, whether corruption intrinsic or extrinsic – which is that committing it entails sin due to the essence or accompaniment of a description or adjacency – then no.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute about whether prohibition (النهي) legally (شرعاً) indicates corruption (الفساد), i.e., that the prohibited act is invalid and does not produce its intended effects. The chosen position in this text is that it does indicate corruption absolutely, in both acts of worship (عبادات) and transactions (معاملات). Some, like al-Ghazali and al-Razi, restrict it to worship only. Others say it indicates validity in transactions, a view chosen by Ibn al-Humam. However, the passage points out a significant issue: the Hanafi school (الحنفية) holds that prohibition does not imply corruption but rather implies validity in both worship and transactions. The author (of the matn, presumably forthcoming) will choose this Hanafi view. Therefore, the statement 'المختار نعم' (the chosen view is yes) is problematic if it refers to intrinsic corruption. The discussion is left incomplete as the text cuts off, but it seems to distinguish between intrinsic corruption (فساد بالذات) and broader corruption that includes any sin (إثم). The Hanafi position is that prohibition does not affect the intrinsic validity of the act; the act may be valid but sinful.

Key Terms
الفساد (al-fasad): Differentiated: فساد بالذات (intrinsic corruption = nullity) or فساد بالغير (extrinsic corruption = sinful act)
العبادات (al-ibadat): Acts of worship
المعاملات (al-mu'amalat): Transactions, civil dealings

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, legal prohibition does not imply the invalidity of the act; the act remains legally effective but sinful, distinguishing between moral and legal dimensions.

Effect of Prohibition on Worship and Transactions
Usul dispute on the effect of prohibition on validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that prohibition (nahy) does not necessitate invalidity in transactions and evaluate the opposing views.

Core Idea
Prohibition nullifies reward in worship but not worldly benefit in transactions – the key Hanafi distinction.

This passage dives into a fundamental usul al-fiqh dispute: does a divine prohibition (nahy) automatically render an act invalid (fasād)? The Hanafi school maintains that the effect differs between acts of worship (ʿibādāt) and transactions (muʿāmalāt). By studying the arguments of major Hanafi scholars like Ibn al-Humam and the subsequent critique, you will grasp the nuanced reasoning behind this distinction and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Recognize that the passage debates whether prohibition implies invalidity in transactions as it does in worship. The Hanafi position is that it does not.
This is an usul disagreement with practical fiqh outcomes.
2
Examine the Hanafi proof (Ibn al-Humam's argument)
Ibn al-Humam argues that worship aims at reward; prohibition removes reward and voids the act. Transactions have worldly benefit (e.g., ownership) that remains despite prohibition, so they remain valid.
Understand the distinction based on the purpose of the act.
3
Analyze the counter-critique
The commentator ('this slave') refutes Ibn al-Humam by showing that in worship, reward and punishment can coexist if the act is legislated in itself but prohibited in description. This logic extends to transactions, so prohibition does not necessarily negate validity.
The critique relies on the concept of multiple aspects (wajh) in a single act.
4
Determine the relied-upon answer
The passage concludes that the correct response is creating a difference in the locus (iḥdāth al-taghāyur fī al-maḥall) – i.e., prohibition applies to a description, not the essence. This preserves the validity of the act while making it sinful.
This is the key methodological move in Hanafi usul.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

فلا تكون منهياً عنها كالعبادات، فانتقض الدليل ويمكن دفعه بأن المعاملات بعض منها غير مأمور به، فيجوز أن يكون منهياً عنه بخلاف العبادات فإن كلها مأمور بها، فتنافي النهي، كذا في «الحاشية»، ويرد مثله على أصل الدليل فإن العبادات منها ما هي مندوبة فلا تكون مأموراً بها إلا أن يراد بالمأمور به أعم، ثم قال بهذا يندفع أن المعاملات مباحة، فلا تكون منهياً عنها للتضاد، ولا يظهر وجه الدفع إلا أن منها ما هي حرام فيتعلق به النهي، وفيه نظر ظاهر، فإن دعوى المستدل أن النهي في المعاملات لا يقتضي الفساد بخلاف العبادات للتضاد في المأمورية والنهي والمعاملات غير مأمورة فتوجه الكلام فثبت أنها غير مأمورة لكنها مباحة الإباحة لذاتها والنهي للوصف انقلب على أصل الدليل، وجوب النقض بد أن يكون بحيث لا ينقلب أصلاً فتدبر فحينئذ قد بان لك أن الجواب المعتمد هو إحداث التغاير في المحل، وشيد أركان الاستدلال الشيخ ابن الهمام أن المقصود في العبادات الثواب، فإذا نهى عنها صار ارتكابها موجباً للعقاب، فخلا العبادة عن ثمرتها بالنهي، فلا تكون مشروعة أصلاً، وأما المعاملات فلا بعد أن يقول الناهي: جعلت هذا الشيء سبباً لهذا، لكن لا تفعله، ولو فعلت عاقبتك، لا تخلو المشروعية الذاتية عن فائدتها في الدنيا، وإن كان موجباً للعقاب في الآخرة، كالبيع، فإن حكمه الملك ويثبت مع الحرمة والعبادات ليس لها ثمرة دنيوية، بل ثمرتها تكون الثواب لا غير وقد انعدم بالنهي فلا يصح، وقال مطلع الأسرار الإلهية» في شرح المنار ما ذكره في العبادات صحيح، وينبغي أن تكون المعاملات أيضاً كذلك فإن النافي للصحة متحقق وهو النهي، وما ذكره من المثال ففيه دليل صارف لما جوزنا أن يكون الشيء عبادة ومشروعاً في نفسه ويكون منهياً وغير مشروع بوصفه، فإذا أتى المكلف بهذا الفعل استحق لأن يعطى أجر نفس الفعل، ويعاقب على إتيانه بوصف غير مشروع، وأن لا يوجب هذا الفعل نيل No, the third article on linguistic principles: necessary in prohibited acts of worship, and as for it not being in transactions, it does not indicate it at all, but it is possible that there is another evidence there, so contemplate that transactions may also be obligatory, so they are commanded, thus they are not prohibited like acts of worship, so the evidence is contradicted, and it can be refuted by saying that some transactions are not commanded, so it is possible that they are prohibited unlike acts of worship, for all of them are commanded, so they oppose prohibition, as in 'al-Hashiyah'. And the like is refuted on the original evidence, for among acts of worship there are those that are recommended, so they are not commanded unless what is meant by 'commanded' is general. Then he said by this it is repelled that transactions are permissible, so they are not prohibited due to contradiction, and the face of repulsion is not apparent except that among them are what is forbidden, so prohibition attaches to it. And in it is apparent consideration, for the claim of the arguer that prohibition in transactions does not necessitate invalidity unlike acts of worship due to contradiction in being commanded and prohibition, and transactions are not commanded, so the speech is directed, and it is established that they are not commanded but they are absolutely permissible, so prohibition contradicts it. If it is meant that among them is what is not permissible in essence, then invalidity is also necessary in it, and that is against the school of the arguer, and the refutation is completed. And if it is meant that they are forbidden due to a description, then they are permissible in essence, so prohibition contradicts it. And if it is distinguished that permissibility is in essence and prohibition is for the description, it overturns the original evidence. The refutation must be such that it does not overturn at all, so contemplate. Then it has become clear to you that the relied-upon answer is creating a difference in the locus. And Sheikh Ibn al-Humam reinforced the pillars of argumentation: the purpose in acts of worship is reward, so if they are prohibited, committing them becomes a cause of punishment, thus the worship becomes devoid of its fruit by prohibition, so it is not legislated at all. As for transactions, it is not far that the prohiber says: I made this thing a cause for this, but do not do it, and if you do I will punish you. The intrinsic legislation is not devoid of its worldly benefit, even if it causes punishment in the afterlife, like sale, for its ruling is ownership and it is established with prohibition. Acts of worship have no worldly fruit, rather their fruit is only reward, and it has been negated by prohibition, so it is not valid. And the author of 'Matali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyyah' said in the commentary of 'al-Manar': what he mentioned about acts of worship is correct, and transactions should also be the same, for the negator of validity is realized, and it is prohibition. And what he mentioned of the example contains evidence that diverts from the implication of prohibition. And this slave says: what Sheikh Ibn al-Humam mentioned is refutable, for suppose the purpose in acts of worship is reward, but we do not accept that it contradicts the attachment of prohibition which necessitates punishment, for it is permissible that one is rewarded and punished for one act. Since we permitted that a thing can be worship and legislated in itself and be prohibited and not legislated in its description, when the accountable person performs this act, he deserves to be given the reward of the act itself and be punished for performing it with the unlegislated description, and that this act does not cause attaining great degrees due to its inclusion of an unlegislated description. So it is not far to say that the persistence in committing the prohibited act nullified the reward of the good deed, but the liability (dhimma) occupied by it has fallen by its existence, so falling from liability by performing it is a kind of reward. And when the situation is known in acts of worship, then in transactions by primary route. And what the author of 'Matali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyyah' mentioned that the negator of validity is realized in transactions, and it is prohibition, this slave does not comprehend it, for prohibition in legal matters absolutely or in transactions only implies validity, so how can it be negating? Whoever claims must provide evidence. So understand, and He knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute about the effect of prohibition (nahy) on the validity of acts, distinguishing between acts of worship (ʿibādāt) and transactions (muʿāmalāt). The discussion opens with a claim that prohibition in transactions does not necessarily imply invalidity, unlike in worship. The author examines the reasoning: some argue that because acts of worship are commanded, prohibition contradicts their nature, but transactions are not commanded, so prohibition does not invalidate them. However, this is critiqued because some transactions are commanded (obligatory), and some acts of worship are recommended (not strictly commanded). The conclusion is that the relied-upon answer is to create a difference in the locus (iḥdāth al-taghāyur fī al-maḥall), meaning that the prohibition applies to a different aspect of the act. Sheikh Ibn al-Humam then presents a strong argument: the purpose of worship is reward, so prohibition removes reward and makes the act void; transactions have worldly benefit (e.g., ownership in sale) that remains even if prohibited, so they can be valid despite prohibition. The author of 'Mataliʿ al-Asrar al-Ilahiyyah' argues that transactions should be like worship, because prohibition itself negates validity. The commentator ('this slave') refutes Ibn al-Humam by showing that in worship, reward and punishment can coexist if the act is legislated in itself but prohibited in a description; similarly, in transactions, validity is not necessarily negated by prohibition, as prohibition itself implies validity in legal matters. The passage ends by emphasizing that the one who claims invalidity must provide proof.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): Prohibition; a divine address requiring abstention from an act.
العبادات (al-ʿibādāt): Acts of worship; actions whose primary purpose is reward in the afterlife.
المعاملات (al-muʿāmalāt): Transactions; worldly dealings with intrinsic worldly benefit.
الفساد (al-fasād): Invalidity; the state of being legally void.
إحداث التغاير في المحل (iḥdāth al-taghāyur fī al-maḥall): Creating a difference in the locus; distinguishing between the essence and description of an act so that prohibition applies only to one aspect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sale becomes invalid and produces no legal effects.
B) The sale is valid but sinful, and ownership is still established.
C) The sale is neither valid nor invalid; it is suspended until repentance.
D) The sale is valid only if the prohibition is lifted later.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul principle that prohibition does not necessitate invalidity in transactions is grounded in the distinction between essential benefit and descriptive prohibition, refined by the concept of 'creating a difference in the locus'.

Hanafi Refutation of Maliki Claim on Eid Fasting Prohibition
Refutes Maliki claim using usul distinctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between prohibition of essence and prohibition of attribute in Hanafi usul and apply this distinction to evaluate the Maliki position on Eid fasting.

Core Idea
A prohibited act is not automatically invalid if the prohibition is due to an attribute, not the essence.

This passage presents a Hanafi refutation of the Maliki claim that the hadith prohibition of fasting on Eid renders the act haram and invalid. The Malikis rely on the reported prohibition and the absence of objection as ijma. The Hanafis offer three logical critiques: the prohibition is more general than the claimed invalidity, the attribute is necessary, and the counterexample of prayer in a usurped house undermines the Maliki position. Understanding these distinctions is key to Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maliki position and its dalil
The Malikis claim that the hadith prohibition of fasting on Eid and the lack of objection from the Companions constitute ijma, proving that fasting on Eid is haram and therefore invalid.
The Hanafis do not accept the claim of ijma.
2
Examine the first Hanafi refutation
The prohibition (tahrim) claimed by the Malikis is a necessary consequence (lazim) of their argument, not the argument itself. Even if the prohibition is established, it does not automatically entail invalidity because the invalidity depends on whether the prohibition is on the essence or an attribute.
The Hanafis distinguish between prohibition 'li-aynihi' (due to essence) and prohibition 'li-wasfihi' (due to attribute).
3
Examine the second refutation
The attribute causing the prohibition in Eid fasting is a necessary attribute (wāṣf lāzim)—it cannot be separated from the act. Even if this case leads to invalidity (which Hanafis do not accept), it does not extend to cases where the attribute is separable (mufāriq), such as selling during Friday prayer. Thus, the Maliki evidence is not comprehensive.
This limits the applicability of the Maliki deduction.
4
Examine the third refutation
The Maliki claim is contradicted by the case of prayer in a usurped house, which is valid by consensus despite the prohibition due to usurpation. If prohibition always invalidated, this prayer would be invalid, but it is not.
This is a powerful counterexample that shows prohibition does not always imply invalidity.
5
Analyze the Maliki counter-response and its rebuttal
The Malikis respond that the prohibition is on the usurpation, not the prayer itself. The Hanafis rebut that authentic hadith prohibit using another's property without permission, yet the prayer remains valid. Hence, the Maliki distinction does not salvage their position.
The passage ends abruptly; the full argument continues in the source.

Source Text

Followers of Imam Malik said: The scholars deduced the prohibition of fasting on Eid from the prohibition (nahy) reported concerning it, and the lack of any objection constitutes ijma. It is refuted first: [by saying] that the prohibition (tahrim) is a necessary consequence (lazim) of your claim, and it is more general (a'amm) than it; so its existence does not necessitate your claim. If [you] mean prohibition in itself, it entails invalidity, but we deny the ijma, especially since the discussion is about invalidity due to a description. Understand. Second refutation: [it is said] that the description (the prohibited attribute in fasting on Eid) is a necessary description (lazim), so invalidity in that case does not necessitate invalidity in cases where the attribute is separable (mufariq). So the evidence does not cover your claim; the deduction is not established. It may be answered that the deduction is only from the prohibition itself, so there is no difference between necessary and separable. But this answer is rejected; it must be clarified. Understand. Third refutation: It is said this [claim] is contradicted by prayer in a usurped house and similar acts, which are valid by consensus, even though the prohibition is attached to them due to a description. The person performed prayer accompanying the usurpation, just as zakat is not disobedience even if given to the deserving while committing a disobedience. Commentary
This passage presents a Madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and the Malikis regarding the prohibition of fasting on Eid. The Malikis argue that the hadith prohibition and the absence of objection constitute ijma (consensus) that fasting on Eid is haram and therefore invalid. The Hanafis offer three refutations: (1) The prohibition (tahrim) they claim is a necessary consequence (lazim) of their argument but more general; the existence of a prohibition does not automatically entail invalidity of the act itself, especially when the invalidity is due to an external attribute. (2) The attribute that causes the prohibition in Eid fasting is a necessary attribute (lazim), meaning it cannot be separated from the act. Therefore, even if this case leads to invalidity (which Hanafis do not accept for the essence), it does not extend to cases where the attribute is separable (mufariq), like selling during Friday call. The Maliki evidence is thus not comprehensive. (3) The Maliki position is disproved by the case of prayer in a usurped house, which is valid by consensus despite the prohibition due to the usurpation. The Malikis respond by distinguishing that the prohibition is on usurpation, not on the prayer itself. The Hanafis counter that authentic reports prohibit using others' property without permission, implying the act is prohibited. The passage ends abruptly, likely continuing the argument.

Key Terms
تحريم (taḥrīm): Prohibition; in this context, the Malikis claim it is a definitive prohibition causing invalidity, while Hanafis distinguish between prohibition of essence and prohibition due to attribute.
إجماع (ijmāʿ): Consensus; the Malikis claim ijma on the prohibition of Eid fasting, which Hanafis deny.
لازم (lāzim): Necessary consequence; in this refutation, the prohibition claimed by the Malikis is a necessary consequence, not the essence of their argument.
مفارق (mufāriq): Separable attribute; an attribute that can be separated from the act, as opposed to a necessary attribute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the third Hanafi refutation against the Maliki claim that fasting on Eid is invalid due to the prohibition?
A) The hadith is not authentic.
B) The prohibition is only makruh, not haram.
C) Prayer in a usurped house is valid by consensus despite the prohibition.
D) The ijma is not proven.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, prohibition (nahy) does not necessarily imply invalidity (fasād); the distinction between prohibition of essence and prohibition of attribute is crucial.
