The Effect of Prohibition (Nahy) on Obligation: Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Usul dispute on the implication of prohibition (nahy) on obligation (wujub).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on whether a prohibition attached to a description invalidates the obligation of the act and apply this distinction to cases like prayer in usurped land.

Core Idea
Prohibition of a description does not invalidate the obligation of the act itself; they coexist in different respects.

This passage delves into a core usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the interplay of prohibition (nahy) and obligation (wujub). The specific issue is whether a prohibition that targets a description within an act—such as praying in usurped land—renders the entire act invalid. The Hanafis argue for a nuanced distinction, while the Shafi'is see inherent contradiction. This lesson unpacks the arguments and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Frame the dispute
Identify the scenario: an act is obligatory but includes a prohibited description (e.g., prayer in usurped land). Note that the Hanafi school rules the act valid but sinful, while the Shafi'i school rules it invalid.
The dispute is about nahy li-wasf (prohibition due to a description), not nahy li-aynihi (prohibition of the essence).
2
Examine the Shafi'i proof
The Shafi'i argument is that prohibition and obligation are contradictory; they cannot coexist in the same act. Therefore, if a description is prohibited, the entire act must be invalid.
This relies on the principle that a command and a prohibition on the same thing are mutually exclusive.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafis distinguish between the prohibition of the description (wasf) and the obligation of the act. They argue that contradiction only occurs when both apply to the same aspect; here, the prohibition targets the usurpation, while the obligation targets the prayer itself.
The Hanafi position uses the analogy of disliked (makruh) acts: a disliked act is prohibited yet not invalid.
4
Evaluate the analogy of the vow
The text presents the example of vowing to fast on Eid: the vow creates an obligation to fast, but fasting on Eid is prohibited. The vow remains binding, and the fasting is performed with sin. This shows that obligation and prohibition can coexist.
Similarly, prayer in usurped land is valid but sinful; the prohibition of the description does not negate the obligation.
5
Derive the practical consequence (thamarah)
For prayer in usurped land: the Hanafi school rules the prayer valid but sinful, requiring make-up (qada). The Shafi'i school rules the prayer completely invalid.
This difference significantly affects daily practice in cases where one unknowingly uses usurped property for worship.

Source Text

But when one vowed to fast on the day of Eid, fulfillment necessitated perpetration, and there is no disgrace in the Wise obligating such, so ponder. Then, since in fulfillment there is perpetration of a prohibited act, and in avoidance there is abandonment of an obligatory act, but falling short, and loss is not loss in every respect, the ruling was chosen for breaking the fast and requiring make-up, so understand. We have elaborated so that the viewer may be insightful and not be misled by what imaginations go to in the first impression of improbability of obligating something and prohibiting it from the right path.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on the implication of prohibition (nahi). The context is prayer in usurped land: Shafi'i argues that since the prayer is prohibited due to the description of being in usurped land, the obligation of prayer itself is invalidated. Hanafis respond by distinguishing between the prohibition of the description and the obligation of the act. They argue that the prohibition does not necessitate the invalidity of the original obligation because the prohibition is attached to a specific description (usurpation) while the obligation remains on the act itself (prayer). The Hanafi position uses the analogy of a vow: if one vows to fast on Eid, the fulfillment involves sin, yet the obligation remains. Similarly, the prohibition does not negate the obligation; rather, the obligated person is required to avoid the description if possible. The passage also refutes the Shafi'i claim by noting that if prohibition implied invalidity, then disliked acts would also be invalid, which is not the case. The Hanafi position is that the prohibition of a description is compatible with the obligation of the act, and the proper ruling is that the act is valid but sinful, hence the need for make-up.

Key Terms
نهي (nahy): A prohibition that calls for abandonment; in this passage, it is discussed whether it implies invalidity.
وصف (wasf): A quality or description attached to an act, such as 'in usurped land' for prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Prohibition of a description (wasf) does not inherently invalidate the obligation of the act; they can coexist with different legal effects.

Prohibited Acts as Legal Causes: Hanafi Usul Defense
Hanafi defense: prohibited acts can cause legal effects via underlying causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that prohibited acts can cause legal effects by distinguishing between the act's intrinsic evil and its underlying reality, and defend it against the Shafi'i objection using the concept of sababiyya.

Core Idea
A prohibited act's legal effect arises from its underlying reality, not from its moral status.

This lesson delves into a key usul dispute: can a prohibited act like zina produce a legal effect such as mahramiyya (kinship prohibition)? The Hanafi school argues yes, because the effect stems from the act's inherent cause (the act of intercourse and potential offspring), not from its being lawful or unlawful. We will unpack the Hanafi response to three Shafi'i objections, focusing on the principles of qabih li-aynihi (evil in itself) vs. qabih li-ghayrihi (evil due to an aspect) and the role of dhaman in usurpation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
State clearly that Hanafis consider zina capable of establishing mahramiyya, similar to lawful nikah, because the underlying cause (intercourse) is the same.
The key distinction: the legal effect is due to the act's reality, not its prohibition.
2
Understand the Shafi'i objection
The Shafi'i argument: a prohibited act cannot be a cause for a blessing (ni'mah) like kinship; the prohibition nullifies any positive causation.
This objection is based on the idea that legal effects should only flow from commendable acts.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response using talil
The Hanafi response distinguishes between the act's essence (qabih li-aynihi or li-ghayrihi) and its causal property (sababiyya). The prohibition is due to an extrinsic aspect (e.g., confusion of lineage), not the act itself.
The act's underlying reality (e.g., intercourse creating a child) is what causes the legal effect.
4
Apply the same reasoning to other cases
The author extends this logic to usurpation (ghasp) and seizure of Muslim property by a non-Muslim, showing that ownership arises from dhaman or loss of 'isma, not from the prohibited act itself.
In each case, the legal effect is attributed to a distinct cause that is not prohibited.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis rule that zina establishes mahramiyya, while Shafi'is do not. This affects marriage eligibility between the child and the biological father's relatives.
This difference flows directly from the usul principle being debated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الملل، ثم أورد الشافعية علينا أوّلاً: إنكم جعلتم الزنا سبباً لقرابة المصاهرة حتى حكمتم بالحرمة، كما في الحلال مع أنه محظور لعينه أو لجهة لا تقبل الانتساخ وهذا المحظور لا يصلح سبباً لنعمة أصلاً، وثانياً : أنكم تحكمون بتملك الغاصب المغصوب، مع قبيح لعينه لا يصلح سبباً للملك . ثالثاً : أنكم تثبتون ملك الكفار أموال المسلمين بالاستيلاء مع أنه قبيح لعينه أراد المصنف أن يجيب عنها فقال : (وثبوت حرمة المصاهرة بالزنا ضروري لحقيقة الوطء) الموجود (بسببية الولد) يعني أن النكاح إنما يوجب الحرمة لكونه سبباً للولد الموجب للجزئية، والوطء الحرام مثله في سببية تكون الولد حقيقة وإن أهدر الشارع هذه السببية والولد ليس فيه قبح إنما هو مخلوق الله تعالى من غير صنع الوالد والوطء يقوم مقامه في إيراث الجزئية المحرمة من حيث من حيث إنه فعل محرم كالتراب يزيل الحدث من حيث إنه قائم مقام الماء وإن كان من حيث ذاته ملوثاً، وبالجملة إن سببيته للحرمة ليست بالذات بل بالعرض، وهذا غير منكر، ومذهبنا مذهب أمير المؤمنين عمر وابن عباس وأكثر التابعين وهذا كثبوت ملك الغاصب) فإن الغصب بما هو غصب لا يوجب الملك ، وهو المحظور بعينه بل إنما يوجب (بسببية الضمان) يعني أن الغصب للضمان عند فوات الأصل، بأن يزول اسمه وإيجاب الضمان يصلح جزاء للفعل الحرام وليس فيه قبح أصلاً، وهو لا يجامع بقاء ملك المالك، وإلا لزم اجتماع العوض والمعوض في ملك واحد فوجب الخروج عن ملكه فلا بد من الدخول في الضمان لئلا يكون شائبة في الإسلام، فالموجب بالذات لثبوت الملك هو الضمان ولما كان الغصب سبباً له أضيف الملك إليه استناداً ، فإنه يحدث عنه إيجاب الضمان ويستند، ولهذا لا يملك الغاصب الزوائد، ويملك ربح عليه ملكاً محظوراً لكونه تبعاً، ولذا يجب التصدق به كذا قالوا، وينقض بالمدبر، فإنه الضمان فيه ولا يدخل في ملك الغاصب، وتفصيل المقام مع جوابه مذكور في «شروح أصول الإمام فخر الإسلام قدس الله سره (و) هذا كثبوت (ملك الكافر بالاستيلاء) وهو أيضاً ليس سبباً بما هو استيلاء بل بسببية زوال العصمة عن مال المسلم لانقطاع الولاية الشرعية الموجبة للإحراز بخلاف الباغي إذ لا ينقطع عنه الولاية الشرعية للشركة في الإسلام، فإذا زال موجب ما يجب أنه سبب لا العصمة انقطع ملكه فبقي المال غير مملوك فيملكه الكافر بالاستيلاء، وصار كالاحتطاب والاصطياد ثم هذا القدر يكفينا ههنا في الاستناد وأما إثبات زوال العصمة فبالنص القرآني وبالسنة كما سيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى فانتظر مطلقاً
And you obligate the guarantee that it is [for] reconciliation between parties, and the evil due to an aspect when no other good aspect outweighs it. The good meaning is that there is no good aspect at all. Likewise, it does not accept the removal of prohibition, like zina, for it is prohibited because it causes confusion of lineage, and there is no good aspect at all, let alone that it overcomes it. And they argued that the act with a married woman and this act are identical in reality, so there is no evil in its essence at all, only evil due to another aspect as we mentioned. The truth is what the words of the noble scholars indicate: that zina is evil in itself, and although the two acts are identical at first glance, rulings differ according to particularities and relations. So the act with a female slave is good, and with a foreign woman is evil with respect to this same attribution. Even if one claims difference in reality according to the Wise One, it is not far. So understand. And when something is evil in itself or evil due to an aspect and there is no other good aspect in it, it does not accept removal of prohibition at all, so it is not permitted. That is each of what Allah mentioned in some religion. Then the Shafi'is made objections: First: You made zina a cause for kinship through marriage, ruling prohibition as in the lawful, while it is prohibited in itself or due to an aspect that does not accept removal, and this prohibition cannot be a cause for any blessing at all. Second: You rule that the usurper owns the usurped object, while it is evil in itself and cannot be a cause for ownership. Third: You establish the property of disbelievers over Muslims' property by seizure, while it is evil in itself. The author intended to answer these: (The establishment of prohibition of marriage by zina is necessary due to the reality of intercourse) that exists (due to the causation of the child). That is, marriage only causes prohibition because it is a cause for the child that necessitates partiality. And unlawful intercourse is similar in causing the child in reality, even if the Lawgiver disregards this causality. The child itself has no evil; it is created by Allah without the parents' action. Intercourse takes its place in causing the prohibited partiality, insofar as it is a prohibited act, like dust removes the state of impurity insofar as it stands in place of water, even if it is filthy in itself. In summary, its causality for prohibition is not essential but incidental, and this is not denied. Our madhhab is that of Amir al-Mu'minin Umar, Ibn Abbas, and most of the Tabi'un. And this is like the establishment of the usurper's ownership: usurpation as such does not cause ownership—it is prohibited in itself—rather it causes (due to the causality of the dhaman (guarantee)). That is, usurpation necessitates dhaman when the original is lost, by its name being removed. And obligation of dhaman is a suitable recompense for the prohibited act and has no evil in it. And it is incompatible with the persistence of the owner's ownership, otherwise compensation and the compensated would be combined in one ownership, so exiting from his ownership is necessary. So there must be entry into dhaman to avoid any flaw in Islam. So the essential cause for the establishment of ownership is dhaman, and since usurpation is a cause for it, ownership is attributed to it derivatively. Therefore, the usurper does not own the increase, and he owns profit over it as prohibited ownership because it is subsidiary, and therefore he must give it in charity. So they said. And it is refuted by the mudabbar (the slave whose emancipation is delayed), for dhaman is present in it and it does not enter the usurper's ownership. The detailed discussion with its answer is mentioned in the commentaries on the Usul of Imam Fakhr al-Islam. And (this is) like the establishment of (the disbeliever's ownership by seizure). It is also not a cause as seizure, but due to the removal of protection (isma) from the Muslim's property because the legal authority that necessitates preservation is cut off, unlike the rebel, because legal authority is not cut off from him due to his share in Islam. So when the cause of what is obligated that it is a cause is removed... the protection is cut off, his ownership is cut, so the property becomes unowned, and the disbeliever owns it by seizure, and it becomes like gathering firewood and hunting. Then this much suffices us here in derivation. As for establishing the removal of protection, it is by the Quranic text and by the Sunnah, as will come, God willing, so wait absolutely.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul dispute responding to Shafi'i objections regarding the ability of prohibited acts to produce legal effects. It begins by discussing the categories of evil: evil in itself (qabih li-aynihi) and evil due to an aspect (qabih li-ghayrihi). The Hanafi position is that even though zina is prohibited, it can establish the prohibition of marriage through kinship (mahramiyya) because it shares the same underlying reality (the act of intercourse) that causes the birth of a child, which is the basis of kinship. The prohibition of zina is not due to the act itself but due to the confusion of lineage. Similarly, usurpation (ghasp) can lead to ownership through the obligation of dhaman (guarantee), not through the act of usurpation itself. The passage then addresses three Shafi'i objections: (1) zina causing mahramiyya, (2) usurper owning the usurped object, and (3) disbeliever owning Muslim property by seizure. The author answers each by explaining that the legal effect is not due to the prohibited act in itself but due to a distinct cause that arises from it. The response to the third objection uses the principle of 'isma (protection) being removed, leaving the property unowned. The passage ends by stating that proof for removal of protection is from Quran and Sunnah.

Key Terms
قبيح لعينه (qabīḥ li-ʿaynihi): An act that is inherently evil due to its intrinsic nature, not due to external factors.
سببية (sababiyya): The quality of being a cause that leads to a legal effect, regardless of the act's normative status.
ضمان (ḍamān): Legal guarantee or compensation that arises from wrongful possession, which can become a basis for ownership.
قبيح لجهة (qabīḥ li-jihah): Evil due to an aspect; an act that is not intrinsically evil but becomes so due to a specific circumstance or relation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objection, what is the basis for establishing mahramiyya through zina?
A) The prohibition itself makes it a cause.
B) The reality of intercourse and potential offspring, similar to lawful marriage.
C) The approval of the act by the Lawgiver.
D) The consensus of the companions.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that prohibited acts can cause legal effects through their underlying reality, not through their prohibition.

Dispute on Qisas with a Heavy Weapon: Manat and Dalil
Usul dispute on the manat for qisas with heavy weapons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the manat (effective cause) for qisas in the Hanafi usul dispute and apply the two competing criteria to classify homicidal weapons.

Core Idea
Qisas requires external bodily injury: Abu Hanifa demands a wound breaking structure inside and out.

The ruling on qisas (retaliation) for killing with a heavy weapon like a club or stone is a classic usul dispute. At its heart lies a disagreement over the manat (effective cause) that triggers the penalty. Abu Hanifa insists on an external wound, while his two disciples consider any unbearable hitting sufficient. This lesson unpacks their arguments, the proof texts used, and the final mufta bihi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
The case: a person is killed intentionally with a heavy weapon (muthqal) that crushes but does not cut the skin. Does qisas apply? Abu Hanifa says no (only diyah); the two disciples say yes.
The dispute is about analogy (qiyas) between muthqal and muhaddad (sharp weapon).
2
Locate the two manat definitions
Abu Hanifa defines the manat as 'a wound that breaks the bodily structure externally and internally' (al-jarh al-naqid lil-buniyya zahiran wa batinan). The two disciples define it as 'hitting with something the body cannot normally bear' (al-darb bima la yutiqahu al-badan al-insani adatan).
The difference in manat leads to opposite rulings.
3
Analyze the hadith 'La qawada illa bi al-sayf'
The two disciples cite this hadith to argue that the sword is an example of an unbearable weapon, so the manat is the unbearable hitting, not the sharpness. Abu Hanifa interprets the hadith literally: qisas is only with the sword, so heavy weapons are excluded.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalala; its interpretation is contested.
4
Evaluate the Quranic general texts
The two disciples also rely on general Quranic verses like 'al-hurru bil-hurr' (2:178) and 'al-nafsu bil-nafs' (5:45) which mandate qisas for intentional killing. They argue these cover all intentional killings except those with shubha (uncertainty). Abu Hanifa qualifies these verses by his manat condition.
The dispute reveals different usul approaches: restriction vs. generalization.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text states that the fatwa is according to the two disciples: qisas applies for killing with a heavy weapon. This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.
The tarjih is based on the strength of the general Quranic texts and the practical need for deterrence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نص الزنا وكفارة القتل والغموس صعب، فإن فهم المناط لغة هناك ممنوع، بل لا يحضر بالبال هذا المناط المذكور إلا بعد نظر أدق، فيجوزه العقل تجويزاً ضعيفاً، وفي القياس ربما يكون المناط فيه أظهر من هذا فتدبر وكذا قولهما بإيجاب القتل بالمثقل قصاصاً، بدلالة نص ورد فيه بالمحدد وهو قوله عليه وعلى آله الصلاة وسلام لا قود إلاّ بالسيف لأن المناط للقصاص الضرب بما لا يطيقه البدن الإنساني عادة فإنه موجب للموت والضرب به قصداً آية العمدية، فهو والمحدد سواء وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله المناط ما ذكر بل الجرح الناقض للبنية ظاهراً وباطناً والمثقل وإن كان ناقضاً باطناً لكنه غير ناقض ظاهراً، هذا: ثم إنهما لا يحتاجان في إثبات القصاص فيه إلى هذه الدلالة بل النصوص بالعبارة تدل على وجوب القصاص فيه، نحو قوله تعالى : الحُرُ بِالحُرِ ﴾ [البقرة: ۱۷۸] و النَّفْسَ بِالنَّفْسِ ﴾ [المائدة: ٤٥] وغير ذلك نعم : خص منه ما فيه شبهة الخطأ، وهي إنما تكون بآلة يطيقه البدن في العادة، ولا تقضي إلى القتل غالباً، وسمعت من مطلع الأسرار الإلهية أن الفتوى على قولهما، وأما هذه الدلالة ففيه أن الحديث المذكور يحتمل أن يراد به لا يقام القصاص إلا بالسيف فليس من الباب في شيء ولا تقوم حجة مع مع احتمال فافهم
The text of zina and the expiation for murder and ghamus are difficult [to understand the manat]. Indeed, understanding the manat linguistically there is prevented; rather, this mentioned manat does not come to mind except after a more precise consideration. The intellect may deem it permissible but weakly. In qiyas, perhaps the manat is more apparent than this, so ponder. Similarly, their statement [of the two disciples] obligating qisas for killing with a heavy weapon, by the indication of a text that came regarding the sharp weapon, which is his statement, peace be upon him and his family: 'No qisas except with the sword.' Because the manat for qisas is hitting with something the human body normally cannot bear, as it causes death, and hitting with it intentionally is a sign of premeditation, so it and the sharp weapon are equal. Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, said: the manat is what he mentioned, rather the wound that breaks the structure externally and internally; and the heavy weapon, although it breaks internally, is not breaking externally. Then: they [the two disciples] do not need this indication to prove qisas in it; rather, the textual expressions indicate the obligation of qisas in it, such as His statement: 'the free for the free' [al-Baqarah 2:178] and 'the soul for the soul' [al-Ma'idah 5:45] and others. Yes, an exception is made for what contains an element of error (shubha), which occurs only with a tool that the body normally can bear and does not usually lead to death. And I heard from the one who is aware of divine secrets that the fatwa is according to their statement. As for this indication, in it: the mentioned hadith is likely to be interpreted as 'qisas is not established except with the sword,' so it is not of the subject at all, and no proof stands with probability, so understand.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage presents a usul dispute regarding the derivation of the ruling of qisas for killing with a heavy weapon (muthqal) as opposed to a sharp weapon (muhaddad). The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that qisas applies to the heavy weapon because the effective cause (manat) for qisas is hitting with something the human body cannot normally bear, which causes death, and intentional hitting indicates premeditated murder. They support this by the hadith 'No qisas except with the sword,' interpreting it narrowly. Abu Hanifa, however, argues that the manat is a wound that breaks the bodily structure both externally and internally; the heavy weapon only breaks internally, not externally, so it does not meet the condition. However, the text notes that the two disciples do not need this specific daleel because the general texts like 'al-hurru bil-hurr' cover all intentional killing, except cases of uncertainty where the weapon is such that it usually does not cause death. The fatwa is according to the two disciples. The text also critiques the hadith evidence, noting that it may be interpreted differently and thus not a strong proof.

Key Terms
المثقل (al-muthqal): A heavy weapon that kills by crushing or blunt force, such as a club or stone, which does not cut or break the skin externally.
المحدد (al-muhaddad): A sharp weapon that cuts and causes external wound, such as a sword or knife.
المناط (al-manat): The effective cause or ratio legis (illah) that determines the application of a ruling.
القصاص (al-qisas): Retaliation in kind for homicide or bodily injury, as prescribed in Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following best describes the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two disciples regarding qisas with a heavy weapon?
A) Abu Hanifa requires external and internal bodily injury; the two disciples require only unbearable hitting.
B) Abu Hanifa requires unbearable hitting; the two disciples require external and internal injury.
C) Abu Hanifa and the two disciples agree that the hadith 'La qawada illa bi al-sayf' is decisive.
D) The two disciples do not use the general texts from the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The manat for qisas is disputed: external and internal wound vs unbearable hitting, affecting whether heavy weapons trigger retaliation.

Is Fahwa a Type of Qiyas? The Hanafi Position
Usul dispute on fahwa vs qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish fahwa from qiyas in Hanafi usul and state the three main arguments for the majority position that fahwa is not qiyas.

Core Idea
Fahwa is a linguistic self-evident implication, not a theoretical analogy — the original is subsumed under the branch, impossible in qiyas.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut tackles a key usul controversy: is the conclusive implication of speech (fahwa) a form of analogy (qiyas) or an independent linguistic proof? The disagreement has practical consequences for whether hudud penalties can be established by fahwa. We will examine the three main arguments of the majority Hanafi position and the responses to Imam al-Razi's challenge.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the two positions
Identify that the dispute is whether fahwa is qiyas jali or an independent linguistic dalil. The majority of Hanafis and Shafi'is say it is not qiyas; Imam al-Razi and some Hanafis say it is qiyas jali.
2
Examine the first argument: self-evidence (badihah)
The majority argues that fahwa is self-evident to any language speaker, while qiyas is theoretical. Therefore, fahwa is not qiyas.
The opponent may respond that fahwa can be more hidden than some qiyas, but the self-evidence of the core notion remains.
3
Examine the second argument: pre-Shari'ah understanding
Fahwa was understood even by non-Muslims before Islamic law, as in the case of 'uff' implying hitting. Qiyas, on the other hand, is a post-Shari'ah reasoning method.
The opponent objects that qiyas can also be used before revelation, but its legal validity is only with Shari'ah.
4
Examine the third argument: subsumption of the original in the branch
In fahwa, the original (e.g., a particle) is a part of the branch (e.g., a larger amount), so the original is subsumed under the branch. By consensus, this subsumption is impossible in qiyas.
The opponent may argue that the particle is not a part 'with the condition of no increase', but the majority counters that it is included without that condition.
5
Evaluate the counter-argument and the Hanafi response
Imam al-Razi argues that without the necessitating meaning (manat), the ruling would not apply; thus it is qiyas. The Hanafi response is that the meaning is only a condition for linguistic inclusion, not the establisher of the ruling.
The dispute is conceptual (ma'nawi) and the practical difference appears in the establishment of hudud.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة
،
(جمهور الحنفية والشافعية على أنه أي الفحوى ليس بقياس، وقيل) هو (قياس جلي، واختاره الإمام الرازي) من الشافعية وبعض منا أيضاً قيل فائدة الخلاف أن الحدود تثبت به عند من قال إنه ليس قياساً، بخلاف من قال إنه قياس قال صاحب الكشف : قد سمعت بعض شيوخي الذي كان من الثقات إنه لم يختلف في ثبوت الحدود به وإنما الخلاف في ثبوت الحدود بالقياس الخفي لنا أولاً أنه أي الفحوى بديهي ولهذا ثبتت بهد الحدود ولا شيء من القياس (كذلك أي بديهياً مثبتاً للحدود (وفيه ما فيه لأن الكبرى ممنوعة، لأن المخالف يدعي كونه قياساً جلياً، ويعتبر بكونه مثبتاً للحدود هذا ولك أن تمنع الصغرى كيف وربما تكون بعض الدلالات أخفى من القياس إلا أن يتحرر الدليل هكذا الفحوى فهم المناط فيه بديهي للعارف باللغة، وإن كان الحكم في المسكوت نظرياً لخفاء المناط فيه، والقياس ليس كذلك، والحق أن الذي يدعي فيه كونه دلالة نظرية فهم المناط ليس دلالة قياساً شرعياً لأنه بعد الشرع وفيه أن الاستدلال بالقياس لا يتوقف على الشرع) فيجوز كونه قياساً مفيداً قبل الشرع ولهذا أثبته الحكماء) وسموه تمثيلاً مع أنهم غير متشرعين بشريعة (نعم اعتباره أي القياس (شرعاً) إنما يكون بالشرع وذلك في غير الجلي وأما الجلي فاعتباره في الشرع لا يتوقف على الشرع أيضاً (و) لنا (ثالثاً الأصل في القياس لا يكون مندرجاً في الفرع) بحيث يسري حكمه إليه إجماعاً ، وههنا قد يكون مثل لا تعطه (ذرّة فإنه يدل على أن لا يعطيه أكثر منه ، مع أن الذرة جزء منه وداخل فيه فلا يكون قياساً، لأن اختلاف اللوازم يستلزم اختلاف الملزومات وفي المقدمة الأولى مناقشة بأن وجوب عدم اندراج الأصل في الفرع ممنوع، وإنما الممتنع الاندراج الذي يوجب الفردية، وليس الذرة فرداً من المال الكثير (كذا في شرح المختصر لكن هذا المنع إنما يتوجه لو منع ثبوت الإجماع، فإنه بعد ثبوته لا يقبل المجمع عليه المنع أصلاً، فإن قلت: لا يصح منع ثبوت الإجماع فإن النقلة ثقات، قلت : ما نقلوه إنما هو عدم الاندراج اندراج الجزئي تحت الكلي بحيث يكون الفرع متناولاً إياه لعمومه (أقول) ليس المناقشة في المقدمة الأولى فقط بل في المقدمة الثانية أيضاً من أن الأصل ههنا داخل في الفرع (لأن الأصل هو الأقل بشرط (لا أي بشرط عدم الزيادة عليه وهو ليس جزءاً من الأكثر، إنما الجزء الأقل لا بشرط الزيادة فتدبر) وأجاب عنه في التلويح بأن هذا الذي بكونه بشرط لا وإن لم يكن داخلاً فيه حقيقة، لكنه داخل لا بشرط الزيادة، وهذا ممتنع في القياس بالإجماع، وبالجملة : إن دخول الأصل في الفرع في بادىء الرأي ممتنع في القياس إجماعاً بخلاف الدلالة فافهم الإمام الرازي وأتباعه قالوا: لولا المعنى الموجب وجوده) أي وجود حكم الأصل في الفرع لما حكم فيه، فثبوت الحكم فيه لأجل المعنى الموجب وهو القياس (أقول) في الجواب ملاحظة المعنى الموجب لثبوت الحكم (لا يوجب النظرية حتى يكون قياساً كما في القضايا التي قياساتها معها فإنها ضرورية، مع أن القياس الموجب للحكم موجود هناك، وهذا غير وافٍ، إذ النظرية غير لازمة للقياس كيف وهو يقول إنه قياس جلي فافهم وأجيب في المختصر أن المعنى شرط لتناوله أي تناول الكلام لحكم المسكوت (لغة) فإن اللغة قد وضعت التركيب لتناول الحكم لما يوجد فيه المناط فملاحظة المناط إنما هي ليعلم تناول الكلام لا أنه مثبت للحكم حتى يكون قياساً، وتفصيله أن القياس يظهر في الفرع لوجود ما يقتضيه فيه لا لأن الكلام دال عليه لغة وعرفاً وأما دلالة النص فعند الجماهير دلالة لغوية للمركب والمناط شرط لتناول الحكم، وهو بمنزلة العنوان، ومن ظنها قياساً أن يزعم لا دلالة له عليه لغة ولا عرفاً، وإنما يلزم الحكم بوجود العلة غاية ما في الباب أن التعليل ووجود العلة ضروريان فصارت قياساً جلياً، فقد ظهر أن النزاع معنوي تظهر فائدته في بعض الأحكام، وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : المعنى الموجب لا يوجب الحكم في الفرع أصلاً، وإنما يلاحظ لكونه بمنزلة العنوان، فلا يثبت مدعاكم إلا إذا ثبت أن الحكم هناك لأجل هذا المعنى ودونه خرط القتاد وهو ممنوع وبهذا القدر تم الجواب، لكن لزيادة التوضيح قال : (ومن ثم) أي من أجل أن المعنى ليس مثبتاً للحكم بل شرط للتناول اللغوي قال به النافي للقياس) كداود الظاهري وغيره وعلى ما قررنا لا يتوجه إليه قوله (وقد يقال : إن القياس الجلي لم ينكر ) فقبول المنكر له الدلالة لا يلزم منه أنها غير القياس، وأما عدم التوجه فلأنه لا يزيد على الكلام على السند فافهم
Issue: (The majority of Hanafis and Shafi'is are of the opinion that fahwa is not qiyas, and it is said that it is qiyas jali, and Imam al-Razi from the Shafi'is chose this, and some of us [Hanafis] also.) It is said that the benefit of the difference is that hudud are established by it according to those who say it is not qiyas, contrary to those who say it is qiyas. The author of al-Kashf said: I heard from some of my trustworthy teachers that there was no difference regarding the establishment of hudud by it; rather, the difference is in the establishment of hudud by hidden analogy. For us, first: fahwa is self-evident (badihi), and therefore hudud are established by it, and no qiyas is [self-evident] likewise. (But there is something in it) because the major premise is denied, as the opponent claims it is qiyas jali and considers it as establishing hudud. You may deny the minor premise: how, and perhaps some indications are more hidden than qiyas, unless the proof is established. Thus fahwa: understanding its manat is self-evident for one acquainted with the language, even if the ruling concerning the unspoken object is theoretical due to the hiddenness of the manat. Qiyas is not like that. The truth is that he who claims it is a theoretical indication, his understanding of the manat is not a real indication but rather qiyasat, and therefore our shaykhs did not act upon it, so understand. (And for us, second: certainty of benefit, i.e., fahwa benefits the ruling before the legality of qiyas, and therefore it was understood by those not bound by religion, from the prohibition of 'uff' the prohibition of hitting, so it is not a legal qiyas because it is after the law. But the objection is that reasoning by qiyas does not depend on the law, so it is possible that it is a beneficial qiyas before the law, and therefore the wise ones affirmed it and called it tamthil, even though they are not followers of a law. Yes, its validity (i.e., of qiyas) legally is only through law, and that applies to non-evident qiyas; as for evident qiyas, its validity in law does not depend on law either.) (And for us, third: the principle in qiyas is that the original is not subsumed under the branch, such that its ruling extends to it by consensus. But here it may be like 'Do not give him a particle' which indicates that you should not give him more than that, even though the particle is a part of it and is included in it, so it is not qiyas, because difference in concomitants necessitates difference in the primary objects. In the first premise there is a discussion: the obligation of non-subsumption of the original in the branch is denied; rather, what is prevented is the subsumption that entails individuality, and the particle is not an individual of much wealth. (Thus in Sharh al-Mukhtasar, but this denial applies only if the consensus is denied; after its affirmation, the agreement of the community cannot be denied at all. If you say: It is not valid to deny the consensus because the transmitters are trustworthy, I say: What they transmitted is only the non-subsumption as subsumption of a particular under a universal such that the branch encompasses it due to its generality. (I say) The discussion is not only in the first premise but also in the second: that the original is here included in the branch (because the original is the lesser with the condition of 'not'? i.e., with the condition of no increase upon it, and it is not a part of the greater; rather, the part is the lesser without the condition of increase, so reflect. And the answer was given in al-Talwih that this which is with the condition of 'not', although it is not included in it in reality, but it is included without the condition of increase, and this is impossible in qiyas by consensus. In summary: the inclusion of the original in the branch at first glance is impossible in qiyas by consensus, unlike [fahwa] indication, so understand.) Imam al-Razi and his followers said: Were it not for the meaning that necessitates its existence (i.e., the existence of the ruling of the original in the branch), the ruling would not be given in it; so the establishment of the ruling in it is due to the necessitating meaning, which is qiyas. (I say) In the answer, consideration of the necessitating meaning for the establishment of the ruling (does not necessitate theoreticality so as to be qiyas, as in propositions that have their qiyas with them, for they are self-evident, even though the qiyas that necessitates the ruling is present there. This is insufficient, for theoreticality is not necessary for qiyas; how, since he says it is qiyas jali? So understand.) And it was answered in al-Mukhtasar that the meaning is a condition for its inclusion (i.e., the inclusion of the speech for the ruling of the unspoken) (linguistically). For language has been established such that the construction includes the ruling for what contains the manat; so consideration of the manat is only to know the inclusion of the speech, not that it establishes the ruling so as to be qiyas. The detail is that qiyas appears in the branch due to the existence of what necessitates it therein, not because the speech indicates it linguistically and conventionally. As for textual indication (dalalat al-nass), according to the majority, it is a linguistic indication of the compound, and the manat is a condition for the inclusion of the ruling; it is like a title. And whoever thinks it is qiyas claims that there is no indication for it linguistically or conventionally, and that the ruling is necessitated by the existence of the cause. The gist is that the reasoning and existence of the cause are necessary, so it becomes qiyas jali. Thus it appears that the dispute is conceptual; its benefit appears in some rulings. When you understand this, we say: The necessitating meaning does not necessitate the ruling in the branch at all; rather, it is considered as a title. So your claim is not established unless it is established that the ruling there is due to this meaning, without which it is impossible, and this is denied. So much for the answer. But for further clarification, he said: (Hence) i.e., because the meaning is not what establishes the ruling but a condition for linguistic inclusion, the denier of qiyas expressed it (like Dawud al-Zahiri and others). According to what we have established, his statement is not applicable to him that 'It is said: evident qiyas is not denied.' For the acceptance of the denier of the indication does not entail that it is not qiyas. As for the non-applicability, it is because it does not add anything to the speech on the chain, so understand.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute concerning whether fahwa (conclusive implication, e.g., 'do not say uff' implies 'do not hit') is considered a type of qiyas (analogy) or an independent linguistic dalil. The majority of Hanafis and Shafi'is hold that fahwa is not qiyas; Imam al-Razi and some Hanafis argue it is qiyas jali (evident analogy). The text discusses the practical consequence: whether hudud (fixed penalties) can be established by fahwa. Those who say it is not qiyas allow hudud with it; those who say it is qiyas restrict hudud by hidden analogy. Three arguments are presented for the majority position: (1) Fahwa is self-evident (badihi) while qiyas is theoretical; (2) Fahwa is understood even by non-Muslims before Islamic law, whereas qiyas is a post-Shari'ah reasoning; (3) In qiyas, the original (asl) is not subsumed under the branch (far') by consensus, but in fahwa the original (e.g., 'particle') is a part of the branch ('more'), which would violate this rule. Counter-arguments from Imam al-Razi and responses are given, including a refutation from al-Talwih. The passage concludes that the dispute is conceptual (ma'nawi) and its benefit manifests in specific rulings. The final position is that the meaning (manat) is a condition for linguistic inclusion, not the establisher of the ruling, so fahwa remains a linguistic dalil distinct from qiyas.

Key Terms
الفحوى (al-fahwa): The conclusive implication of speech that a stronger prohibition or ruling applies to a more severe case, e.g., prohibition of hitting implied by prohibition of 'uff'.
القياس الجلي (al-qiyas al-jali): Evident analogy where the cause is explicitly or obviously present, considered by some to be a type of qiyas.
الحدود (al-hudud): Fixed penal laws prescribed by the Shari'ah, e.g., for zina, theft, etc.
بديهي (badihi): Self-evident, requiring no reasoning; fahwa is claimed to be badihi for a language speaker.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is a key argument of those who say fahwa is not qiyas regarding the example 'Do not give him a particle'?
A) The particle is not included in the ruling for 'more', so it is qiyas.
B) The particle is a part of 'more', so the original is subsumed under the branch, which is impossible in qiyas by consensus.
C) The hadith about hudud prohibits using qiyas.
D) The particle is a metaphor, so qiyas does not apply.

Key Takeaway
Fahwa is a linguistic implication distinct from qiyas; the Hanafi arguments (self-evidence, pre-Shari'ah understanding, non-subsumption of original) support its independent status.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
8 min
Mafhum Mukhalafah: The Hanafi Critique of a Contested Linguistic Indication
Definition and critique of mafhum mukhalafah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of mafhum mukhalafah, explain the Hanafi objection to its validity as a linguistic indication, and identify the practical consequence of the dispute with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
Mafhum mukhalafah is not a linguistic proof because its validity depends on an unknowable absence of other benefits in speech.

In this lesson, we examine the concept of mafhum (understood meaning) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. While the agreed-upon mafhum muwafaqah (inferred meaning that agrees with the spoken) is accepted, the contested mafhum mukhalafah (opposite inferred meaning) is rejected by the Hanafi school. We will explore the precise definition, the conditions the Shafi'is require, and the rational argument that leads Hanafis to deny its status as a valid linguistic indication, emphasizing the absolute certainty required for deriving legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between mafhum muwafaqah and mafhum mukhalafah
Identify the two divisions of mafhum. Muwafaqah agrees with the spoken ruling and is accepted by all. Mukhalafah yields the opposite ruling for the unspoken case and is disputed. Note that the text calls muwafaqah 'lahn al-khitab' and mukhalafah 'dalil al-khitab'.
This distinction is foundational; muwafaqah is agreed upon, mukhalafah is the point of ikhtilaf.
2
List the conditions for mafhum mukhalafah according to its proponents
From the text: 1) No other benefit from specification (like priority/equality), 2) Not habitual speech, 3) Not a response to a question about the mentioned, 4) Speaker not ignorant of the unspoken case. These conditions must all hold for the opposite meaning to be linguistically indicated.
These conditions are drawn from the Shafi'i framework; Hanafis argue they are never fully satisfied.
3
Understand the Hanafi core argument: unknowability of the absence of benefits
The Hanafi claim: the validity of mafhum mukhalafah depends on the absence of any other benefit from the specification. But benefits of speech are unlimited and cannot be exhaustively known (especially in divine speech). Therefore, the condition is perpetually unknown, making mafhum mukhalafah a theoretical, not linguistic, indication.
The text stresses that even conjecture about absence is insufficient because benefits are infinite in number.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal to the counter-argument of conjecture
The text refutes the idea that a lack of apparent benefit can be conjectured: even if no obvious benefit is seen, there is always at least the benefit of providing a descriptive title (e.g., 'thief' gives a label). Hence, the absence of other benefits is never truly known, and mafhum mukhalafah falls.
This rebuttal shows the Hanafi commitment to certainty in usul: dhann (conjecture) is not sufficient.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf to a concrete example
Consider a condition like 'if she has a child' in a contract. A Shafi'i would infer that if she does not have a child, the ruling does not apply (mafhum mukhalafah). A Hanafi rejects this implication and treats the condition as only specifying the case, not creating a counter-inference. The ruling for the unspoken case must be sought elsewhere.
This practical consequence shows how the dispute affects real fiqh rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمفهوم إما مفهوم موافقة وهو دلالة النص) وقد مرت (ويسمى لحن الخطاب، وإما مفهوم مخالفة، وهو ثبوت نقيض حكم المنطوق نفياً كان أو إثباتاً (للمسكوت) بل الدلالة عليه (ويسمى دليل الخطاب وشرطه أي شرط تحققه (عدم ما يوجب التخصيص) بالذكر سوى نفي الحكم عن المسكوت والموجب سواه (كظهور الأولوية أو المساواة) إذ على هذين التقديرين يكون المسكوت مساوياً للمنطوق في الحكم بالدلالة أو القياس وخروج الكلام مخرج (العادة فإن الظاهر حينئذ التكلم على حسب العادة لا نفي الحكم (وكونه جواباً للسائل عن حال المذكور، إذ حينئذ الغرض المطابقة للسؤال (وجهل المتكلم بحال ما لم يذكر فلا يدل على النفي أصلاً للتخصيص بالذكر إلى غير ذلك من الفوائد، وهو) أي مفهوم المخالفة (أقسام : منها : مفهوم الصفة وهو ثبوت نقيض حكم المنطوق لما لا توجد فيه الصفة من أفراد الموصوف قال به الشافعي وأحمد والأشعري وجماعة من ونفاه الحنفية والقاضي) أبو بكر (و) الإمام الغزالي) حجة الإسلام كلاهما من الشافعية (والمعتزلة وهو المختار ومحل النزاع الدلالة (لغة) يعني أن التركيب لغة موضوع للمفهوم عند عدم فائدة أخرى عندهم خلافاً لنا وقد ويقال : إنه موضوع أو مستعمل استعمالاً شائعاً (لا كنكات البلغاء) فإنه لا نزاع في أنه قد يقصده البلغاء أحياناً، لا أن البلغاء يقصدونه دائماً عند الفائدة الأخرى حتى لا يكون الكلام الذي خلا عن نفي الحكم عما عداه ولم يظهر له فائدة أخرى بليغاً حتى يرد أن كلام الشارع في أعلى درجة من البلاغة فيلزم أن يكون المفهوم ثانياً فيه، وهو مدار الأحكام، وليس لنا كثير حاجة بالكلام الغير البليغ (لنا أولاً : أقول دلالة المفهوم نظرية مجهولة أبداً، ولا شيء من دلالة اللغة كذلك ضرورة) فلا شيء من المفهوم بدلالة اللغة (أما) المقدمة (الأولى فلأنها ههنا (موقوفة على عدم فائدة أخرى اتفاقاً، وهو مجهول أبداً فإن الفوائد عددها غير معلوم حتى يعلم انتفاؤها (سيما في كلام الشارع) فإن العقول تعجز عن الإحاطة بفوائده إن قيل: ربما يظن عدم الفائدة فيظن بالمفهوم ولا حاجة لنا إلى القطع به فإنا لا ندعي القطع بالمفهوم (قلت: هذا الظن أي ظن عدم فائدة أخرى، بل ظن المفهوم من الفوائد، فيجب انتفاؤه فيبقى مجهولاً) بل ينتفي المفهوم من الأصل (ولك أن تقول : الظن قد يلاحظ (قصداً كما إذا اقتضى الحال أن يذكر المتكلم كلاماً موهماً للتخصيص والقصر ولم يكن ،مراده فلا دلالة على نفي الحكم عما عداه، بل إنما الغرض الإيهام فقط، كذا في الحاشية» (وقد يلاحظ الظن (تبعاً) بأن يتكلم لإفادة حكم من غير قصد إلى فائدة أخرى، فيظن عدم الفائدة والفائدة المنفية (الأول) والشرط) للمفهوم الثاني فافهم) ولك أن تجيب عن أصل الإيراد بأنه لا يمكن الظن بفقدان الفائدة فإن الفوائد غير محصورة في عدد ولو ظناً حتى يعلم الانتفاء أو يظن ثم هي لكثرتها لا يتحقق مادة ينتفي فيها الجميع بأسرها إلا نادراً، إذ لا أقل من أن الفائدة التعبير عن المحكوم عليه بالموصوف بالصفة وجعله عنواناً له كما في التعبير باللقب، وعلى هذا يندفع ما يورد أن مقصودهم أن الكلام موضوع لنفي الحكم عن المسكوت والفوائد الأخرى صارفة عنه ، فإذا لم يظهر فائدة أخرى يظن به في سائر الحقائق فلا يضر عدم معرفة انحصار الفوائد، وذلك لأن فائدة التعبير عن كما دلالة
And the understood meaning (mafhum) is either mafhum muwafaqah, which is the indication of the text (already mentioned, and it is called lahn al-khitab), or mafhum mukhalafah, which is the establishment of the opposite of the ruling of the spoken (mantooq), whether negative or affirmative, for the unspoken (maskoot). Rather, the indication upon it is called dalil al-khitab. Its condition is the absence of what necessitates specification by mention other than negating the ruling from the unspoken, and that which necessitates besides it, like the appearance of priority or equality, because on these two estimates the unspoken is equal to the spoken in ruling by indication or qiyas; and the exit of speech in the manner of custom, for then the apparent is speaking according to custom, not negating the ruling; and it being an answer to a questioner about the state of the mentioned, for then the purpose is conformity to the question; and the speaker's ignorance of the state of what is not mentioned, for then there is no indication of negation at all due to specification by mention, and other benefits. It, i.e., mafhum mukhalafah, has types: among them is mafhum al-sifah, which is the establishment of the opposite of the ruling of the spoken for that individual of the described which does not possess the attribute. Al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, al-Ash'ari, and a group affirmed it, while the Hanafis, al-Qadi Abu Bakr, and Imam al-Ghazali (both from the Shafi'is), and the Mu'tazila negated it, and that is the chosen view. The point of dispute is linguistic indication (dhilalah lughawiyyah), meaning that the linguistic structure is linguistically placed for the mafhum when there is no other benefit, according to them, contrary to us. It is said: it is placed or used in a common usage, not like the subtleties of rhetoricians, for there is no dispute that rhetoricians may intend it sometimes, not that they always intend it when there is another benefit, such that speech that is devoid of negating the ruling from other than it and no other benefit appears would not be eloquent, leading to the objection that the speech of the Lawgiver is at the highest degree of eloquence, so the mafhum would be necessarily present in it, and it is the basis of rulings, and we have little need for ineloquent speech. For us, first: I say that the indication of the mafhum is theoretical, perpetually unknown, and nothing of linguistic indication is like that by necessity. So nothing of the mafhum is by linguistic indication. As for the first premise, because it is here dependent on the absence of another benefit by agreement, and that is perpetually unknown, for the number of benefits is not known such that their absence can be known, especially in the speech of the Lawgiver, for intellects are incapable of encompassing its benefits. If it is said: perhaps the absence of benefit is conjectured, so the mafhum is conjectured, and we do not need certainty, for we do not claim certainty for the mafhum. I say: this conjecture, i.e., the conjecture of the absence of another benefit, rather the conjecture of the mafhum from benefits, so its non-existence is necessary, so it remains unknown. Rather, the mafhum is negated from the origin. And you may say: conjecture may be considered intentionally, as when the context requires the speaker to mention speech that gives an illusion of specification and restriction while he does not intend it, so there is no indication of negating the ruling from other than it; rather, the purpose is only illusion, as in the hashya. And conjecture may be considered incidentally, as when one speaks to convey a ruling without intending any other benefit, so one suspects absence of benefit, and the negated benefit is the first, and the condition for the second mafhum, so understand. And you may respond to the original objection by saying that it is impossible to conjecture the lack of benefit, for benefits are not limited in number even conjecturally, so that their absence can be known or conjectured; then due to their multiplicity, a subject matter in which all of them are negated rarely occurs, for there is at least the benefit of expressing the ruled-upon by the described with the attribute and making it a title for him, like expression by title. Upon this, what is objected is repelled: that their intent is that speech is placed for negating the ruling from the unspoken, and other benefits divert it; so if no other benefit appears, it is conjectured in other realities, so the lack of knowledge of the limitation of benefits does not harm. This is because the benefit of expression is as indication.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on mafhum (understood meaning) from a Hanafi usul commentary. It first distinguishes between mafhum muwafaqah (agreed inferred meaning) and mafhum mukhalafah (opposite inferred meaning). The latter is the main focus. The commentator lists conditions for mafhum mukhalafah to be considered a valid linguistic indication, then presents the dispute: Shafi'is, Hanbalis, and Ash'aris accept it as linguistic dhilalah, while Hanafis, some Shafi'is (like al-Ghazali), and Mu'tazila reject it. The Hanafi proof is that mafhum mukhalafah would require knowing that no other benefit exists in specifying, which is unknowable, especially in divine speech. The passage refutes counterarguments about conjecture and benefits, showing that even without apparent benefit, specifying by attribute always has at least the benefit of providing a title/description. Thus, the concept remains theoretical and not a valid linguistic proof. This exemplifies the Hanafi rationalist approach in usul al-fiqh, emphasizing certainty before deriving rulings.

Key Terms
مفهوم الموافقة (mafhum al-muwafaqah): The inferred meaning that agrees with the spoken ruling, derived by analogy or implication.
مفهوم المخالفة (mafhum al-mukhalafah): The inferred meaning that is opposite to the spoken ruling, understood from a specific attribute or condition.
دليل الخطاب (dalil al-khitab): Synonym for mafhum mukhalafah; the indication from the address itself to the opposite of the spoken.
مفهوم الصفة (mafhum al-sifah): A subtype of mafhum mukhalafah where a specific attribute implies that the opposite ruling applies when that attribute is absent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafis, what is the primary reason for rejecting mafhum mukhalafah as a valid linguistic indication?
A) It contradicts Quranic texts
B) It depends on the unknown absence of other benefits in specification
C) It is based on weak hadiths
D) It is only used by Mu'tazilites

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rejects mafhum mukhalafah as a valid linguistic indication because it requires assuming the absence of other benefits—an assumption that is never certain, especially in divine speech.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

15 min
The Hanafi Rejection of Mafhum al-Sifa: A Usul Dispute
A textual analysis of the dispute over mafhum al-sifa with critical refinement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on mafhum al-sifa and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the distinction between dalala nawʿiyya and dalala shakhṣiyya.

Core Idea
A descriptive attribute does not imply negation of the ruling; type indication defeats individual indication.

This passage presents a foundational debate in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: does a descriptive attribute in a legal text imply the negation of the ruling for what is not described? The Hanafi school, led by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani and the linguist al-Akhfash, firmly rejects this. The Shafi'i school, via al-Shafi'i and Abu Ubayd, accepts it. The dispute cuts to the heart of how we derive rulings from linguistic forms. This lesson will walk you through the key arguments on both sides, focusing on the Hanafi critique that relies on a crucial distinction between indication by type and indication by individual instance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
State whether a descriptive attribute in a text implies the negation of the ruling for its opposite (mafhum al-sifa). Locate where the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions are stated.
The issue is not about whether attributes add meaning, but whether they automatically create a legal exclusion.
2
Analyze the induction argument
The Shafi'i claim that those who affirm the concept have stronger evidence because their induction yields certainty, while the negation is only conjectural. Understand why this seems plausible.
The Hanafi response refutes this by distinguishing two types of indication.
3
Apply the type vs. individual distinction
The Hanafi argues that indication by type (dalala nawʿiyya) is established when most instances of a construction lack a meaning. If most attribute+described constructions do not indicate negation, then the type does not indicate it—and this is certain, not conjectural. Individual indication (dalala shakhṣiyya) is about specific words, where lack of finding is only conjectural.
This is the critical refinement: the type-indication gives certainty to the negater, defeating the affirmer's claim of priority.
4
Evaluate the benefit argument
The Shafi'i argue that without the concept, specifying a description would be pointless (no benefit). The Hanafi responds that this is an argument from benefit, which is invalid for establishing linguistic meaning, and that it can lead to circularity.
The Hanafi does not deny eloquence but insists that linguistic meaning must be proven separately.
5
Assess the authority argument
The passage compares the scholarly authority of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani versus al-Shafi'i and Abu Ubayd, arguing that Imam Muhammad's earlier time and comprehensive knowledge give his position more weight.
This biographical argument supplements the linguistic reasoning but is secondary.

As for the statement of Abu Ubayd when it was said to him: the intended meaning of the hadith 'For the belly of one of you to be filled with pus is better for him than to be filled with poetry' is condemnation of poets and their satire of the Messenger of God, peace be upon him and his family and companions. If it were so, the mention of 'filled' would be devoid of meaning, for a little of it is likewise. So there is also in it no evidence for understanding the concept; rather, perhaps his purpose is that what is condemned is 'filled', but as for knowing a little, it is silent about it, so it remains on the original permissibility. And if the intended meaning were absolute condemnation, not condemnation of much and silence about little, the mentioned would be futile. And the statement that it is a mere permissibility does not harm the inference, because the apparent understanding of both from the description is prevented. And it appears that it is the companion of Sibawayh, because he is meant when unqualified, as it is said. (And) Imam Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani: that there is no concept for the attribute. And they are both imams in Arabic. (Imam Muhammad said: My father left thirty thousand dirhams; I spent half of it on grammar and poetry and half on hadith and jurisprudence. This is an explanation of his effort and perfection of his striving in acquiring the Arabic sciences.) So if the statement of such two imams is contradictory, then there is no proof in their understanding, even if one claims innate facility (salika) for Abu Ubayd and al-Shafi'i, may God be pleased with them, or extreme knowledge and strength of sound transmission from them. Then al-Shaybani is an imam in innate facility and knowledge, and the transmission from him is strong, more authentic, and mutawatir due to the multitude of his followers. In summary: claiming merit for Imam al-Shafi'i over Imam Muhammad in sciences and perfections is from intellectual obstinacy and dominance of desire, and is not valid at all, so understand. Perhaps they understood based on what they had adopted of the doctrine of concept and their taking it as a school. (I say: the indication of existence mentally through the intermediary of the signified is the speech here concerning indication by type, for the point of difference is whether the type of construction of attribute and described indicates negation or not. And if the inducer finds some constructions—indeed most—not indicating, then the lack of individual indication is certain, and its lack individually indicates its lack by type certainly, because everything that belongs to a thing by type belongs to it individually, because the type exists in the individual, and not conversely: i.e., individual indication does not indicate its existence by type, due to the possibility that a particularity has an entry. So the lack of finding indicates non-existence certainly. Thus the one who negates here is better than the one who affirms, or at least the like of him, so understand. Yes, in individual indication, non-existence does not indicate except conjecturally due to lack of encompassing all uses of the specific word, so it is possible that it is indicative but the inducer did not notice it. As for the indication of the type of construction, there is no room for this at all, because speech rarely lacks some of its particulars, so understand. This is solid speech, except that the difference between type indication and individual indication is unclear. For the judgment of the absence of conventional indication of a word is only when its uses are traced. So if one does not find in most uses an indication, one knows that there is no conventional indication for it. For conventional indication does not lag behind the word in its usage. So what Sheikh Ibn al-Humam said is better: that there is no priority for the one who affirms in transmitting conventional indication, because the one who negates also [has evidence]. And reflect on this. (And) they said, secondly: if it were not for the concept (concept of the attribute), the specification by the description would be devoid of benefit, because there is no benefit other than the concept by hypothesis, for the speech is about it. So if there were no concept, it would be devoid of benefit absolutely. And that is not permissible in the speech of eloquent speakers from among individuals, so the Lawgiver is more deserving of impermissibility in His speech. And this proof is among their strongest proofs. The first answer: this proof does not yield indication linguistically, whereas your claim was that. For sometimes something is not permissible in eloquence but is permissible linguistically, so do not try to approximate. And the purpose of this is to alert to the corruption of what the arguer depicted, i.e., your proof is corrupt because even if the premises are granted, they do not yield your claim. Not that we concede indication in eloquence and take it as a school, such that it could be responded that this much suffices for deriving legal rulings from the Book and Sunnah, because they are at the highest degree of eloquence, so the concept is established in them, so understand. (And) the second answer: this type of inference is establishing a linguistic meaning by benefit, and it has been prohibited, as previously mentioned. And by this, what they said is repelled: that it increases benefit by providing two rulings, so this is better than that which has less benefit. The repelling aspect is that this also is establishing language by benefit. (As for repelling it by the necessity of circularity: that increasing benefit depends on the establishment of the concept, so if the concept is established...

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute about the validity of 'مفهوم الصفة' (the implied concept of a descriptive attribute) as a source of legal rulings. The discussion is part of a broader treatment of linguistic principles. The text presents arguments from proponents (Abu Ubayd and al-Shafi'i) who claim that a descriptive attribute in a text implies the negation of the ruling for what is not described, and opponents (Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani and the linguist al-Akhfash) who deny this. The author (likely from the Hanafi school) defends the position that there is no such concept, and refutes the Shafi'i arguments, including a hadith about poetry. The passage also includes a substantial biographical comparison to establish the authority of Imam Muhammad over al-Shafi'i and Abu Ubayd, arguing for the priority of earlier scholars. The text then engages with the counter-argument that those who affirm the concept are stronger because their evidence (based on induction) is certain, while the negation is only conjectural. The Hanafi response refines this by distinguishing between 'individual indication' and 'type indication', arguing that when most instances of an attribute construction do not indicate negation, the lack of indication for the type is certain, making the negater's position stronger. Finally, the text addresses the argument that without the concept, specifying a description would be pointless (since it wouldn't add benefit), and responds that this is an argument from benefit, which is not acceptable for establishing linguistic meaning, and that the same reasoning could lead to circularity. The commentary follows the author's order, clarifying each step of the debate.

Key Terms
مفهوم الصفة (mafhūm al-ṣifa): The implied legal ruling derived from a descriptive attribute in a text, namely that the ruling applies only to the described and is negated for what is not described.
دلالة نوعية (dalāla nawʿiyya): Indication by type: a general characteristic of a class of linguistic constructions (e.g., attribute+described) that can be determined by induction to either indicate or not indicate a meaning.
دلالة شخصية (dalāla shakhṣiyya): Indication by individual instance: a specific use of a word or construction that may or may not indicate a meaning, requiring exhaustive knowledge of all instances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key Hanafi concept used to refute the Shafi'i claim that affirmers have stronger evidence?
A) The argument from benefit
B) The distinction between type and individual indication
C) The authority of earlier scholars
D) The hadith about poetry

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rejects mafhum al-sifa because induction of the type of attribute+described constructions shows that most do not indicate negation, making the type indication certain, and arguments from benefit cannot establish linguistic meaning.

The Linguistic Meaning of Conditional Statements: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Analyzing the Hanafi-Shafi'i disagreement on whether a condition linguistically negates the ruling in its absence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on whether a conditional statement linguistically indicates negation of the ruling in the absence of the condition, and evaluate the linguistic arguments of both schools.

Core Idea
A condition restricts the ruling to its presence; it does not negate the ruling in its absence.

This passage plunges into a foundational usul al-fiqh dispute: does the word 'if' in a conditional statement imply that the ruling is negated when the condition is absent? The Hanafis answer no, while the Shafi'is answer yes. The debate is purely linguistic, but it has profound consequences for how we interpret legal texts. The author defends the Hanafi view against an objection by Ibn al-Humam, clarifying that the two imams (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf or Muhammad) were not confusing linguistic and legal meanings but were illustrating the linguistic point through a legal example.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafis hold: a conditional statement only restricts the ruling to the presence of the condition; it does not imply the opposite ruling for its absence. The ruling for other contingencies remains on the original legal principle (istishab).
The key is that the condition is a restrictor (qayd), not an exception.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position and their proof
The Shafi'is argue that a condition functions like an exception (istithna), extracting the contingency of the condition's presence from a general ruling, thereby implying the opposite ruling for the absence of the condition. They compare it to specifying a general term.
Their proof is linguistic: without the condition, the ruling would apply generally; the condition specifies it, thus negating it otherwise.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that the condition is not an exception; it is a qualifier. There is no general ruling established before the condition; the ruling is only articulated with the condition. Hence, there is no negation implied.
The ruling for the absence is 'passed over in silence' (mashkuk), not negated.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference appears in conditional contracts and oaths. For example, 'If you enter the house, you are free' – Shafi'is consider the manumission to be immediate upon the condition (i.e., the condition triggers the ruling, and the absence implies no manumission), while Hanafis say the manumission only occurs at the condition's occurrence, but the slave remains enslaved until then; the condition does not negate manumission outside its scope.
The implication is that for Hanafis, the condition only affects the timing, not the existence of the ruling.
5
Evaluate the author's tarjih
The author sides with the Hanafis, criticizing Ibn al-Humam's objection as unfounded. He clarifies that the two imams were not making a mistake; they were showing that the Shafi'i view makes the condition a linguistic indicator of negation, while the Hanafi view treats it as a mere restrictor.
The final sentence 'so reflect' invites the student to ponder deeper.
