من ذلك؟ والموجب كما كان من قبل فليس لعدم الشرط دخل فيه بل هو عدم أصلي، قال الشيخ ابن الهمام هذا غلط، لأن السبب الذي يدعي الشافعية انتفاء الحكم بانتفائه في خلافية مفهوم الشرط هو مدلول لفظ الشرط، وفي هذه الخلافية المراد الجزاء الذي جعل سبباً شرعياً للحكم، هل تبطل سببيته بالتعليق أو لا، بل يمنع التعليق عن الحكم فقط، فأين هذا وهذا لا توجه له فإن الشيخين الإمامين لم يدعيا أن مراد الشافعية بالسبب الجزاء، بل مقصودهما أنه لما يمنع الشرط عن ترتب الحكم على السبب الذي هو الجزاء يكون انتفاء الحكم مضافاً إلى الشرط، فصار مدلولاً له وليس فيه غلط في معنى السبب أصلاً، فالصواب ما ذكره مطلع الأسرار الإلهية في وجه التغليط أن مسألة مفهوم الشرط مسألة لغوية حاصلها، هل يدل الشرط لغة على انتفاء الحكم عند انتفائه أولا وهذه الخلافية شرعية، فإن الحاصل أن الذي جعل سبباً شرعاً هل تبطل سببيته شرعاً بالشرط والتعليق أم لا، فلا يصح تفرع الخلافية في مفهوم الشرط على هذه الخلافية ولك أن تقول : بطلان السببية أو المنع عن حكم السبب إنما يتأتى في الإنشاءات التي جعلت أسباباً شرعاً ومسألة مفهوم الشرط تعم كل تعليق خبراً كان أو إنشاء : فلا يصح التفرع ولك أن تقول أيضاً لو سلم بطلان السببية كما هو مزعوم الحنفية فلا يوجب نفي مفهوم الشرط، فإن النزاع باقٍ ،بعد فإنه وإن لم يكن الجزاء سبباً للحكم وأن ينتفي عند عدم الشرط لانتفاء السبب فهل يدل هذا التركيب لغة على الانتفاء أولا، وكذا لو سلم عدم بطلان السببية وانتفاء الحكم بمنع الشرط فلا يلزم أن الشرط دال لغة على انتفاء الحكم بل يجوز أن يكون انتفاء الحكم عند انتفاء الشرط أصلياً ويكون بقاؤه لوجود المانع ولا ينفع الذهاب إليه فتدبر، ثم لك أن تقول في تقرير الكلام إن الجزاء عند الشافعية مفيد للحكم على جميع التقادير لغة وموجب له، والشرط خصصه بتقدير وجوده وأخرج تقدير عدمه ولهذا عدوا الشرط من المخصصات فانتفاء الحكم عند عدم الشرط إنما جاء من تخصيص الشرط، فأفاد حكماً مخالفاً لغة كالاستثناء، إلا أنه مفيد للحكم المخالف في المنطوق والشرط في المسكوت، وأما عندنا فالجزاء مع الشرط يفيد حكماً مقيداً، وما وراءه يبقى على الأصل، سواء كان الحكم في الجزاء، والشرط قيد بمنزلة الظرف والحال، أو كان الحكم بين الشرط والجزاء فإنه إذا ألحق المغير أيّ مغير كان يبقى الكلام موقوفاً فعلى هذا بناء خلافية مفهوم الشرط على أن الشرط هل هو بمنزلة استثناء تقديرات ما وراءه عن الحكم الجزائي وكان الجزاء عاماً لها لغة وأن الشرط مع الجزاء مفيد لحكم مقيد فقط لا غير، فعلى الأول الشرط دال على نفي الحكم عما عداه لغة بخلاف الثاني بل حكم ما عداه مسكوت عنه، ولعل هذا هو مراد الإمامين ثم لما كان من جزئيات التركيبات الشرطية ما جزاؤه سبب شرعاً لحكم آخر، ولم تكن سببيته إلا لإفادة حكمه في محله، وكان الجزاء في نفسه مفيداً لحكم عام لغة أو عرفاً على رأيهم فهو تام في السببية والشرط إنما استثنى بعض التقادير فمنع تأثيره عليها، وأما عندنا فلما لم يفد إلا حكماً مقيداً لم تتحقق سببيته قرر الكلام في هذا المثال وعبر عن الشيء بملزومه، وفي كلام القاضي الإمام إشارة جلية إلى ما قلناه فإن عبارته الشريفة في المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية الأسرار هكذا احتج الشافعي بأن تعليق الحكم بشرط ينفيه عما قبله أو بعده على اعتبار أنه لولاه لكان موجوداً، كقول الرجل لعبده : أنت حر موجبه وجود الحرية صفة للعبد، فإذا قال إن دخلت الدار وتعلق أوجب إعدامه عن محله ونفيه وجود قوله أنت حر، فثبت أن التعليق كما يوجب الوجود عند الشرط : أوجب النفي عما قبله ثم قال بعد بيان فروع الخلافية : أما الله تعالى فإنهم ذهبوا إلى أن الأسباب الموجبة للأحكام إذا علقت بالشروط كان التعليق تصرفاً في العلل بإعدامها لا في أحكامها وعند وجود الشرط يكون ابتداء وجود الأحكام كما عند وجود العلل لا فرق بينهما في حكم الابتداء، فقوله على اعتبار أنه لولاه لكان موجوداً أراد به أن قوله بإفادة الشرط انتفاء الحكم عند عدمه مبنى على اعتبار الشرط كالاستثناء مخرجاً لما عدا تقدير وجود الشرط، وأشار بقوله : فإنهم ذهبوا الخ ... إلى أنهم ذهبوا إلى أن الشرط، مع الجزاء يفيد حكماً مقيداً، ولا إفادة في الجزاء منفرداً حتى يصلح للسببية قبل وجود الشرط، وأما الإمام فخر الإسلام فقد أجمل أوّلاً إجمالاً تاماً، وقال: حاصله أن المعلق بالشرط عندنا لا ينعقد سبباً إنما الشرط يمنع الانعقاد وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : هو مؤخر، ثم بعد تفريع الخلافيات أشار إلى ما قلنا بقوله في استدلال الشافعي قال لأن الوجوب ثبت بالإيجاب لولا الشرط فيصير الشرط معدماً ما وجب وجوده لولا هو، فيكون الشرط مؤخراً لا مانعاً، أنه لولا الشرط لكان الجزاء إيجاباً الثبوت الحكم على جميع يعني التقادير في الحالة لغة، فالشرط استثنى ما عداه وعدم الحكم فيه، فيكون الشرط نفسه مؤخراً لغة لا مانعاً التكلم، وإنما قرر الكلام في هذا الذي من جزئيات المعلق وهو ما كان الجزاء سبباً لحكم آخر شرعاً لذكر بعض التفريعات كما هو دأبهما الشريف ومقصودهما ما ذكرنا، وعلى هذا لا يرد عليه شيء مما ذكر هذا غاية التقرير لكن بقي بعد فيه تأمل فتأمل
And from that? The obligator as before has no entry for the absence of the condition, rather it is an original absence. Sheikh Ibn al-Humam said: This is a mistake, because the cause which the Shafi'is claim the negation of the ruling by its negation in the dispute over the concept of the condition is the meaning of the word 'condition', and in this dispute the intended meaning is the result which is made a legal cause for the ruling; does its causality become invalid by suspension or not, rather the suspension prevents the ruling only. So where is this and that? There is no direction for it, because the two great imams did not claim that the Shafi'is intend by 'cause' the result; rather their intention is that because the condition prevents the attachment of the ruling to the cause which is the result, the negation of the ruling becomes attributed to the condition, so it becomes a meaning for it, and there is no mistake in the meaning of 'cause' at all. The correct view is what the author of Matali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya mentioned in the way of refutation: that the issue of the concept of the condition is a linguistic issue, its summary being: does the condition linguistically indicate the negation of the ruling at its absence or not? And this dispute is a legal one: does what is made a legal cause become invalid as a cause legally by the condition and suspension or not? So it is not correct to branch the dispute over the concept of the condition onto this dispute. And you may say: the invalidity of causality or the prevention of the ruling of the cause only occurs in constructs which are made legal causes, and the issue of the concept of the condition covers all suspended speech, whether news or construct, so branching is not correct. And you may also say: even if the invalidity of causality is accepted as the Hanafis claim, it does not necessitate the negation of the concept of the condition; for the dispute remains after: even if the result is not a cause for the ruling and it is negated at the absence of the condition due to the absence of the cause, does this structure linguistically indicate the negation or not? Similarly, even if the non-invalidity of causality is accepted and the negation of the ruling by the prevention of the condition, it does not necessitate that the condition linguistically indicates the negation of the ruling; rather it is possible that the negation of the ruling at the absence of the condition is original, and its remaining is due to the presence of a preventer, and going to that does not benefit. So contemplate. Then you may say in explaining the speech: the result according to the Shafi'is benefits the ruling for all contingencies linguistically and obliges it, and the condition has specified it to the contingency of its existence and excluded the contingency of its non-existence. For this reason they counted the condition among the specifiers. So the negation of the ruling at the absence of the condition comes from the specification of the condition, so it benefits a contrary ruling linguistically like an exception, except that it benefits the contrary ruling in the articulated and the condition in the unspoken. But according to us, the result with the condition benefits a restricted ruling, and what is beyond remains on the original, whether the ruling is in the result and the condition is a restriction like an adverb and state, or the ruling is between the condition and the result; for if an alternative is attached, the speech remains suspended. Upon this is built the dispute over the concept of the condition: whether the condition is like an exception of the contingencies beyond from the ruling of the result, and the result was general for them linguistically, and that the condition with the result benefits only a restricted ruling. On the first view, the condition indicates the negation of the ruling from what is other than it linguistically, unlike the second; rather the ruling for what is other than it is passed over in silence. Perhaps this is the intention of the two imams. Then, because among the particulars of conditional constructs is that whose result is a cause for another ruling legally, and its causality was only for benefiting its ruling in its place, and the result in itself benefits a general ruling linguistically or customarily according to their view, so it is complete in causality, and the condition only excludes some contingencies and prevents its effect on them. But according to us, since it only benefited a restricted ruling, its causality is not realized. He settled the speech in this example and expressed the thing by its necessary implication. And in the speech of the Judge Imam there is a clear indication to what we said, for his noble expression in the third article on linguistic principles, al-Asrar, is: The Shafi'i argued that suspending a ruling on a condition negates it from before or after, considering that without it it would exist, like a man saying to his slave: 'You are free' – its obligator is the existence of freedom as a attribute for the slave. When he says 'If you enter the house' and it is attached, it necessitates its destruction from its place and the negation of the existence of his statement 'You are free'. So it is established that suspension, as it necessitates existence at the condition, so it necessitates negation from before it. Then he said after explaining the branches of the dispute: 'As for our scholars, may Allah have mercy on them, they held that the causes that obligate rulings, when suspended on conditions, the suspension is an act in the causes by destroying them, not in their rulings; and at the existence of the condition, it is the beginning of the existence of rulings as at the existence of causes, with no difference between them in the ruling of the beginning.' So his statement 'considering that without it it would exist' intended that his statement that the condition benefits the negation of the ruling at its absence is based on considering the condition as an exception, extracting what is other than the contingency of the condition's existence. And he indicated by his statement 'they held that' that they held that the condition with the result benefits a restricted ruling, and there is no benefit in the result alone so as to be suitable for causality before the existence of the condition. As for Imam Fakhr al-Islam, he summarized first a complete summary and said: The summary is that the suspended on a condition according to us does not become a cause; rather the condition prevents the establishment. And al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: It is delayed. Then after branching the disputes, he indicated what we said by his statement in the Shafi'i's argument: He said because the obligation is established by the offer, were it not for the condition, so the condition becomes a destroyer of what was established to exist were it not for it, so the condition is a delayer, not a preventer. That is, were it not for the condition, the result would be an establishment of the ruling for all contingencies in the state linguistically. So the condition excepted what is other than it and the non-existence of the ruling in it. So the condition itself is a delayer linguistically, not a preventer of speech. And he only settled the speech in this which is among the particulars of the suspended – and that is when the result is a cause for another ruling legally – for the sake of mentioning some branchings, as is his noble habit, and their intention is what we mentioned. And upon this nothing of what was mentioned is objectionable. This is the utmost explanation, but after it remains a reflection. So reflect.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage addresses the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute over the concept of the condition (mafhum al-shart). The author first critiques Ibn al-Humam's claim that the two imams (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf or Imam Muhammad) made an error by confusing the linguistic meaning of 'condition' with the legal concept of a cause (sabab). The author defends the imams, stating that the Shafi'is consider the condition to negate the ruling in its absence linguistically, similar to an exception, while the Hanafis hold that the condition only restricts the ruling and that what is beyond remains on the original principle (istishab). The key point is that the dispute is linguistic: does the word 'if' in a conditional sentence imply the negation of the ruling when the condition is absent? The Hanafis say no; the ruling is silent about the absence. The Shafi'is say yes; the condition specifies the ruling to its presence and implicitly negates it otherwise. This linguistic analysis underpins the later legal branches. The author clarifies that the two imams were not confusing linguistic and legal meanings but were illustrating the linguistic implication through a legal example. The commentary then quotes the judge Imam's explanation from the third article on linguistic principles, comparing the Shafi'i and Hanafi positions. It concludes that the dispute fundamentally rests on whether the condition is like an exception or a mere qualifier.

Key Terms
مفهوم الشرط (mafhum al-shart): The linguistically inferred meaning from a conditional statement regarding the ruling when the condition is absent.
السبب (al-sabab): A cause that legally brings about a ruling; here, the result (jaza') is considered a cause for another ruling.
الشرط (al-shart): The condition upon which the ruling is suspended; linguistically, a particle indicating a conditional relationship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, how does a conditional statement (like 'If you enter the house, you are free') affect the ruling for the absence of the condition?
A) It linguistically implies that the ruling is negated when the condition is absent.
B) It provides a restricted ruling only for the presence of the condition; the absence is not addressed and remains on the original principle.
C) It delays the ruling until the condition is met but does not affect the ruling's existence.
D) It invalidates the cause entirely.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a condition in a statement does not linguistically negate the ruling beyond it; it only restricts the ruling to the presence of the condition.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
8 min
The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute on Conditional Clauses: Divorce and Manumission
Applying linguistic dispute to a practical branch

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on conditional divorce/manumission upon ownership and explain how each school's theory of causality (sababiyya) leads to its ruling.

Core Idea
A condition delays the cause; without the condition, the act has no legal effect.

This passage applies the fundamental dispute about the causal efficacy of conditional clauses to the practical case of suspending divorce or manumission on future ownership. The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools reach opposite practical conclusions, each rooted in their theory of when a cause (sabab) becomes effective. Understanding this branch clarifies how usul principles translate into concrete fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the branch
The first branch is ta'liq al-talaq wa al-'itaq bi al-milk: suspending divorce or manumission on the condition of acquiring ownership. Determine what exactly is being disputed.
The issue is whether such a suspended utterance is valid and whether it produces an effect when the condition occurs.
2
State the Hanafi position
According to the Hanafis, the suspended divorce/manumission is valid and takes effect upon the occurrence of ownership. Explain why: the utterance does not become a cause until the condition is met, so it meets a valid place (mahall) at that moment.
The passage says: 'عندنا لا يصح وعندنا يقع عند وجود الملك' (according to us it is not valid, and according to us it occurs upon ownership).
3
State the Shafi'i position
According to al-Shafi'i, the suspended divorce/manumission is invalid (batil) from the start and never takes effect, even after ownership arises. Explain: the utterance becomes a cause immediately, but the place (the slave) is not owned at that time, so the cause is nullified.
The passage says: 'لا يصح عنده، بل يبطل ... ولا يقع شيء عند وجود الشرط'.
4
Analyze the causal reasoning
Compare the two causal frameworks. Hanafis: the condition delays the causal efficacy until the condition is fulfilled. Shafi'is: the condition only prevents the immediate effect but the cause is already established; if the place is not valid at the moment of utterance, the cause fails entirely.
This is the core usul dispute about whether the conditional particle (in/if) negates causality or merely delays it.
5
Apply the same reasoning
Consider another example: an oath or vow suspended on a condition. Predict how each school would rule based on the same principle.
The next branches in the passage deal with vows and expiation, which follow the same logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتفرع عليه تعليق الطلاق والعتاق بالملك فإنه عندنا لا يصح وعندنا يقع عند وجود الملك عندنا لعدم سببيته في الحال، ويصير سبباً عند وجود الشرط وهو الملك فيصادف محلاً مملوكاً، ولا يصح عنده، بل يبطل لانعقاده عنده سبباً في الحال والمحل غير مملوك، فيلغو، ولا يقع شيء عند وجود الشرط (و) يتفرع عليه (تعجيل النذر المعلق) نحو : إن قدم ولدي فعليّ صدقة كذا، فعندنا لما لم يصر هذا النذر سبباً للوجوب إلا عند وجود الشرط لا يصح التعجيل لكونه أداء قبل وجود الوجوب وعنده لما انعقد سبباً في الحال، وإنما الشرط مانع عن وجوب الأداء لانفكاكهما في المالي عنده صح التعجيل به كالزكاة قبل الحول (و) يتفرع عليه أيضاً تعجيل (كفارة اليمين) إذا كان مالياً قبل الحنث، فعنده يجوز لأن الحنث عنده شرط واليمين سبب، وقد وجد السبب فوجب في الذمة، وإن لم يكن واجب الأداء للانفكاك المذكور صح الأداء قبل الحنث، وعندنا : لا يجوز التعجيل لأن سبب الكفارة عندنا الحنث لا اليمين فالتعجيل قبل الحنث أداء قبل وجود السبب، وفيه بحث، فإن التفرع في حيز الخفاء فإن الكلام في الشرط النحوي هل السببية أم لا، والحنث ليس شرطاً نحوياً، وما أجيب أن قوله تعالى: ﴿فَكَفَّرَتُهُ إطْعَامُ عشرة مسكين الله [المائدة: 89] الآية بمعنى إن حنثتم فكفارته الخ... فصار معلقاً بالشرط، فهل يمنع.
And it branches from it: the suspension of divorce and manumission upon ownership – for according to us it is not valid, and according to us it occurs upon the existence of ownership, because of the lack of its causality in the present, and it becomes a cause upon the existence of the condition, which is ownership, so it meets an owned place. And according to him (al-Shafi'i) it is not valid, rather it becomes invalid because it is established as a cause in the present and the place is not owned, so it is nullified, and nothing occurs at the existence of the condition. And it branches from it: the expediting of a suspended vow, like 'If my child arrives, then I must give such and such charity.' According to us, since this vow does not become a cause for obligation except at the existence of the condition, expediting is not valid because it is a performance before the existence of the obligation. According to him, since it established as a cause in the present, and the condition only prevents the obligation of performance due to their separation in financial matters, expediting is valid, like zakat before the year (elapses). And it also branches from it: the expediting of the expiation of an oath, if it is financial, before the violation. According to him it is permissible, because the violation according to him is a condition and the oath is a cause, and the cause has been found, so it becomes due in the liability, even if it is not obligatory to perform due to the mentioned separation; performance before the violation is valid. According to us: expediting is not permissible, because the cause of the expiation according to us is the violation, not the oath; so expediting before the violation is a performance before the existence of the cause. And there is a investigation: for the branching is hidden, because the discussion is about the grammatical condition: does it have causality or not? And the violation is not a grammatical condition. And it was answered that the saying of Allah Most High: 'Then its expiation is feeding ten poor people' [al-Ma'idah: 89] means 'If you violate, then its expiation is...' so it became suspended on a condition. So does it prevent?
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage applies the earlier linguistic dispute to practical legal branches. The first branch: conditional divorce or manumission upon ownership (e.g., 'If I own this slave, he is free'). According to the Hanafis, such a suspension is invalid because the cause (the utterance) does not become effective until the condition is met; at the moment of the condition, the slave is already owned, so the ruling applies. The Shafi'is, however, hold that the utterance is a cause immediately but the condition prevents its effect; since the slave is not owned at that time, the ruling is nullified and never takes effect even when ownership occurs. Second branch: expediting a vow that is suspended on a condition (e.g., 'If my son arrives, I must give charity'). Hanafis say the vow creates no obligation until the condition is met, so giving charity beforehand is invalid as there is no obligation. Shafi'is say the obligation is established from the moment of the vow, but the condition prevents the immediate duty; thus one can give in advance, like giving zakat before the year ends. Third branch: expediting the expiation of an oath before breaking it. Hanafis say the cause for expiation is the violation (the breaking of the oath), not the oath itself; thus giving before the violation is like giving before the cause exists, which is invalid. Shafi'is say the oath itself is the cause, and the violation is only a condition for the duty to perform; since the cause exists, the liability is established, and one may give in advance. The author notes a subtlety: the discussion is about grammatical conditions, but breaking an oath is not a grammatical condition; however, the verse 'If you break your oaths, then its expiation...' makes it analogous. The passage ends with a question about whether it prevents.

Key Terms
تعليق الطلاق والعتاق بالملك (ta'liq al-talaq wa al-'itaq bi al-milk): Suspending the issuance of divorce or manumission on the condition of ownership.
تعجيل النذر المعلق (ta'jil al-nadhri al-mu'allaq): Performing a vowed act before the condition of the vow is fulfilled.
كفارة اليمين (kaffarat al-yamin): Expiation for breaking an oath; its cause is disputed: Hanafis say the violation; Shafi'is say the oath itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a conditional divorce suspended on future ownership take effect?
A) Immediately upon utterance.
B) When the condition (ownership) occurs.
C) Never, because it is invalid.
D) It depends on the type of ownership.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a conditional clause delays the cause until the condition is met, while the Shafi'i school holds it takes effect immediately but is nullified if the place is not valid.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
10 min
Ta'liq Nullifies Causality: The First Hanafi Proof
First proof: causality requires effect in locus; suspension prevents effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi argument that a suspended cause lacks immediate effect and apply it to distinguish ta'liq from mere delay.

Core Idea
A cause requires effect in its locus; suspension blocks effect, so the cause is ineffective.

In this lesson, we examine the first proof that ta'liq (suspension) prevents a cause from being effective. The argument draws an analogy: just as selling a free man is not a valid cause for ownership because the locus (the man himself) is not owned, a suspended cause lacks an immediate locus because the condition is not yet met. We will follow the proof, consider an objection that ta'liq only delays the ruling, and see the Hanafi response that the condition enters upon the cause itself, suspending it entirely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core Hanafi claim
State that ta'liq prevents causality because a cause must have immediate effect in its proper locus (mahall).
2
Analyze the qiyas (analogy)
Examine the analogy: selling a free man is invalid because he lacks the locus of ownership. Similarly, a suspended cause has no immediate effect because the condition suspends its locus.
The analogy is based on the definition of sabab: an action from a qualified person added to the locus.
3
Evaluate the objection
Some argue that ta'liq only delays the ruling, not the cause's effect. In 'if you enter, you are divorced', the divorce is intended upon entry, not immediately.
The objector claims the cause remains effective but postponed.
4
Assess the Hanafi response
The condition is attached to the cause itself (الشرط إنما دخل على السبب), not just the ruling. Thus the cause is suspended and has no effect until the condition is fulfilled.
The intention of the speaker supports this: divorce is only intended upon entry, not now.

The third article on linguistic principles: 'Ahla' (hello) at the time of the existence of that thing. How is insanity not contrary to being absolute or unfree? Rather it contradicts the validity of speech and his speech during insanity is not considered. Here the speech occurred during lucidity. Its becoming a divorce according to him by virtue of law is not problematic. On this basis, suspending divorce and manumission on ownership is valid, because it is speech and not a divorce at the moment, so it does not require the capacity; rather it becomes a divorce upon the condition, which is ownership, and then there is no obstacle to validity (understand). Here some prolixity has occurred, but we preferred it because it was a subject of slippage for the firmly grounded. Reflect so that you might not transgress the truth from what we have conveyed to you. And reliance is on Allah, for He knows His rulings. He first argued: Causality is only through effect in the locus, because the cause is an action from a qualified person added to the locus. Hence, selling a free man is not a cause for ownership due to the absence of the locus. (And suspension) prevents effect, so no causality. (I say: It is directed to him: denial of the denial, i.e., denial of the prevention of effect by suspension, because it is possible that it does not deny effect, but merely delays the ruling. How is this not merely restating the claim? This is debatable, because it is a premise argued in exposition. That is because the condition entered only upon the cause, not the ruling, so the cause becomes suspended; hence no causality and no effect before it. How? If he says 'If you enter, you are divorced', he intends only divorce upon entry, not immediately. And the objector (the author of Matali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya) objected first: The cause is not 'you are divorced' but the entire conditional statement. As for 'you are divorced' alone, it is a cause for immediate divorce, but it left causality by being coupled with the condition, and the whole became a cause for divorce upon entry. You have known that it is not fit for causality, so remember. Secondly, we grant that the consequence alone is a cause, but what is suspended is the occurrence of separation, not the effectuation by the husband. If claimed, it is denied; there must be a witness. Rather by this the woman becomes such that she is divorced upon entry even if she was not this attribute before, and this is a type of effect. This is solid speech, but you may say: The divorce is only the import of 'you are divorced', especially according to the Shafi'is. And when suspended, the divorce becomes suspended too, not only the occurrence of divorce. And when it becomes suspended, it has no effect at all. And the meaning of the woman being such that she is divorced upon entry is only that she is fit for divorce to occur upon entry by the necessary suspension of divorce from him, just as at the time of marriage she is such that she would be divorced upon the attachment of the husband's divorce. But her being in that state by the divorce now existing is false, because it is suspended after. So reflect. And you can affirm that suspension prevents effect because it only establishes the ruling of the necessary connection between the two, not the actual existence of something, so it has no effect in occurrence nor causation. Then the denial is not directed to it, so understand. (And it was objected) to the proof: If suspension prevents attachment to the locus, then it should be null, like immediate divorce to a foreign woman is null due to lack of a locus, and like selling a free man is null. (And it was answered: The object of hope is exposed to causality, so it is useful at that time, so it is not null. (And it becomes null like 'you are divorced if Allah wills', which is like it, and its condition is not hoped to be known). (And) he argued (secondly: The cause without it, i.e., without the ruling, is like the whole without the part, because it is necessary for it like it. The existence of the whole without the part is invalid, so likewise the existence of the cause without the ruling. The ruling is non-existent by agreement, so the cause is likewise. This is debatable, because the necessity of the cause for the effect is denied such that it is like the whole without the part. It is better to say: The original rule is that the cause must have the ruling because it is a path to it, except if there is an external evidence, like menstruation for fasting. So here also it remains on the original rule as long as no evidence for non-occurrence is found, and there is no evidence, so reflect. And the sale with option and the specified divorce like 'you are divorced tomorrow' were adduced: both are causes, but the ruling (ownership in sale and occurrence in specified divorce) is delayed due to the barrier of option and restriction. They may also be adduced against the first proof: they become causes only if they meet the locus and effect it, and option and restriction prevent that. The truth is that neither of these is an objection to the proof, because we only claimed that suspension prevents effect and causation because it does not bring about the existence of anything in reality. And here there is no suspension; rather it is restriction, and its import is the realization of this restricted thing in reality, so it has causation and effect. The most is that the effect does not exist except when the restriction exists, so understand. And it was answered about the first: The option is contrary to analogy out of necessity to prevent loss, and analogy requires the binding of the contract. (And it, i.e., necessity) is only to the extent of the ruling. So the ruling is attached to it. As for the cause, its attachment is without necessity, because its attachment would necessitate the attachment of the ruling as well without the opposite, and analogy forbids its attachment, so it is not attached without evidence. (And) it was also answered: The condition 'ala' (upon) indicates that what follows is suspended, as it is said: 'I will come to you on condition that you come to me', meaning 'if I come, you come'. And if what is suspended is what follows, i.e., the option, then the sale is immediate, and what is suspended is the option of rescission because the sale exists. If you say: Then why is the ruling of ownership not established despite the existence of the cause? He said: 'The suspension of the ruling is only to prevent harm' from the one who has the option. And perhaps you say: It was stated earlier that 'you are divorced for a thousand' means 'if you pay a thousand, you are divorced', and the divorce is suspended on payment, so what precedes 'ala' is suspended on what follows. It is better to omit from the answer the narration of 'ala' being for suspension of what follows; rather one should say: The sale is immediate, and the option is only in rescission, because the intended meaning is 'I sold and I have the option of rescission' as indicated by the context of a partial matter. Then the true answer is the permission of non-occurrence due to evidence, except considering the difference in authority. So understand. (And) it was answered about the second: Suspension is an oath, and it is for prevention, meaning its purpose is the non-existence of the condition, otherwise this problem would occur; thus it does not lead to existence usually, but rather to an expiatory oath, so it does not become a cause. As for specification, it realizes the specified thing. For 'you are divorced tomorrow' indicates that divorce is realized tomorrow, so the purpose is the realization of divorce. Thus this becomes a divorce, in the present, leading to occurrence tomorrow, so it becomes a cause. It was objected: The oath may be for urging and encouraging the occurrence of the condition, not for prevention, like 'If you give me news of my son's arrival, you are free'. Then it should become a cause. Except it might be said: Since what is for prevention does not become a cause, what is for urging also does not become a cause due to lack of a differentiating principle. (And a distinction may be made by risk) and doubt (and its absence): That means in suspension, the thing upon which it is suspended is of doubtful existence, so it does not lead to the consequence usually, so it does not become a cause. As for the specified, the restriction is not doubtful but real, so it leads to the realization of what is restricted, so it becomes a cause. And I found a marginal note written with this wording: That if the oath is about a dangerous matter like divorce and similar, it is for prevention; otherwise it is for urging. On this basis, risk prevents prevention. This contradicts the reliable sources in which this speech is transmitted, and perhaps it is a scribal error. Rather it was connected to the preceding refutation. So the summary of the refutation is that prevention is only when the oath is about a dangerous matter; otherwise it is for urging. The claim of prevention in every oath is unreasonable, so reflect. (But this distinction) necessitates the non-permissibility of...
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute about whether ta'liq (suspension) prevents a cause from being effective. The author, commenting on a matn, first establishes that a cause must affect its locus (mahall) to be effective. Suspension prevents that effect, so no causality. An objection claims ta'liq only delays the ruling, not prevents effect. The author refutes this by showing that the condition enters upon the cause itself, suspending it, so there is no immediate effect. Further objections are answered: the cause is the whole conditional statement, but the immediate part 'you are divorced' loses causality when coupled with the condition. The author argues that suspending the cause means the divorce itself becomes suspended, not just the occurrence. The passage then compares ta'liq with temporal specification (idafa) and sale with option (bay' al-khiyar). The key distinction: ta'liq is like an oath aimed at prevention (i'dam), while idafa realizes the action in the future. Thus, ta'liq does not create causality, but idafa does. The author also addresses a marginal note about oaths being for prevention or urging, but concludes that the mainstream Hanafi view is that ta'liq for prevention is the default, and any distinction based on risk is unreliable. The passage guards against confusing suspension (ta'liq) with specification (idafa) or conditional contracts with options.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Suspension of a legal cause by a condition, such that the cause does not produce its effect until the condition occurs.
السببية (al-sababiyyah): The quality of being a cause, i.e., having the capacity to produce a legal effect immediately.
المحل (al-mahall): The locus or subject upon which the cause operates, e.g., the wife in divorce, the slave in manumission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main Hanafi proof that ta'liq prevents causality?
A) The cause is like selling a free man: no locus means no effect.
B) The condition delays the ruling only.
C) The cause is independent of the condition.
D) The locus is always present in suspended causes.

Key Takeaway
Ta'liq suspends the cause itself, not just the ruling, so the cause has no present effect until the condition is fulfilled.

Do Suspended Contracts Create Immediate Legal Causes? The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Dispute on whether suspended contracts function as causes immediately

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on whether ta'liq creates a sabab, explain the distinction between ta'liq and idafa, and evaluate the Shafi'i objections and Hanafi responses using the hadith and usul principles.

Core Idea
Ta'liq is not a cause; only the condition when realized can cause the legal effect, except for tadbir which is a separate institution.

This passage presents a detailed usul dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is on whether suspended contracts (ta'liq) function as immediate causes (asbab). The Hanafi school argues that a mere conditional statement does not create a cause at the time of utterance; it only indicates a connection (mulazama) between condition and result. However, certain expressions like 'I have a charity on the day so-and-so arrives' are treated as actual assignments (idafa) to a specific time, which do create an immediate cause. Additionally, emancipation suspended on death (tadbir) is a separate legal institution that creates a cause now. The Shafi'is object with hadith and logical consistency arguments. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi reasoning and responses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of expressions
Distinguish between idafa (appending to a specific time, e.g., 'I have a charity on the day Zaid arrives') and ta'liq (conditional suspension, e.g., 'If tomorrow comes, you are free'). The former creates a cause now; the latter does not.
Idafa is treated as a direct assignment of the act to a time, making it actual.
2
Understand the Hanafi concept of sabab
A sabab requires ifda' (direct leading to the result) and the condition must be specific or certain enough. A doubtful condition cannot be a cause.
Tadbir is an exception because it is a separate legal institution akin to a will.
3
Examine the Shafi'i objections
The Shafi'is argue (1) ta'liq only prevents, not causes; (2) if not cause now, never cause; (3) hadith prohibits divorce in non-owned.
They consider the hadith inclusive of ta'liq.
4
Analyze the Hanafi responses
Hanafis respond: the hadith refers to tanziz (immediate) acts; the Lawgiver can make prior speech effective at condition even if speaker is insane; conditional statements only indicate connection, not causality.
The concept of 'idafa' and tadbir are distinguished from pure ta'liq.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: If a slave is sold after a suspended emancipation, Hanafis allow sale if the condition is doubtful (e.g., 'if Zaid arrives') but prohibit if certain (e.g., 'when tomorrow comes') because the latter is idafa; Shafi'is would not recognize this distinction.
This shows how the usul principle affects daily transactions.

Third chapter on linguistic principles. ... (The translation of this long passage is extensive; key points: The Hanafi commentator distinguishes between types of suspended contracts. For a vow like 'I have a charity on the day so-and-so arrives', the expression is actual (fi'liyya) because the obligation is real but fixed at a specific time, so it creates a cause now, unlike a mere conditional which does not. For emancipation suspended on death (tadbir), it creates a cause now because it is a form of will. The Shafi'is object: (1) Suspension is only for prevention, not creation of cause. (2) If not a cause now, never a cause. (3) Hadith: 'No divorce etc. in what one does not own.' The Hanafi responses: The hadith refers to immediate divorce, not suspended; the speaker's prior speech can be made effective by the Lawgiver when the condition occurs; conditional statements only indicate connection, not causality.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage is a detailed dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is on whether a suspended contract (ta'liq) creates a legal cause (sabab) at the moment of utterance. The Hanafi commentator, following the text of Musallam al-Thubut, explains that the answer depends on the type of expression. For a vow like 'I have a charity on the day so-and-so arrives', the expression is not a true suspension but an assignment (idafa) to a specific time, so it creates an immediate cause. In contrast, a conditional statement like 'if tomorrow comes you are free' is a true suspension; it does not create a cause now because the condition is doubtful and the statement only indicates a connection (mulazama) between condition and result. However, a suspension tied to death (ta'liq bi al-mawt) is treated differently: it creates a cause for tadbir (posthumous emancipation) immediately, because tadbir is a separate institution akin to a will. The Shafi'is raise three objections: (1) ta'liq is only for prevention, not causation; (2) if it is not a cause now, it can never become a cause because the speaker has no role when the condition occurs; (3) prophetic hadith prohibit divorce, emancipation, and vows in what one does not own. The Hanafi responses clarify that the hadith refer to immediate (tanziz) actions, not suspensions; that the Lawgiver can make the prior speech effective when the condition occurs, even if the speaker is then insane; and that the very nature of conditional statements does not imply causality. The Hanafi view is that suspended contracts are not causes, except in the case of tadbir, which has a specific legal basis.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension of a legal effect (like divorce or emancipation) on a future condition.
سبب (sabab): A legal cause that triggers a ruling, either immediately or upon condition fulfillment.
تدبير (tadbir): A contract of emancipation effective after the master's death, considered a form of will.
إيقاع (iqa'): The act of effectuating a legal consequence by utterance.
إضافة (idafa): Appending a legal act to a specific future time, making it a present obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, which of the following creates a legal cause (sabab) at the moment of utterance?
A) 'If tomorrow comes, you are free'
B) 'I have a charity on the day Zaid arrives'
C) 'If Zaid enters, you are divorced'
D) 'If I die, you are free'

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a suspended contract does not create a legal cause at the moment of utterance unless it is a specific assignment (idafa) or a death-tied emancipation (tadbir).

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
10 min
Hanafi Refutation of Objections to Conditional Divorce
Hanafi refutation of objections against conditional divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi response to objections against conditional divorce (ta'liq al-talaq) regarding causality and speaker capacity, and apply the concept of mulazama to similar usul disputes.

Core Idea
Conditional statements indicate entailment, not immediate causation; prior intent suffices for legal effect.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut presents a Hanafi refutation of two key objections to the validity of conditional divorce (ta'liq al-talaq) before the condition is owned. The objections claim that there is no cause at the time of utterance and no speaker capacity at condition fulfillment. The Hanafi response relies on the nature of conditional statements (shartiyya) and Shari'ah's recognition of prior intent as the effective cause.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on conditional divorce
The Hanafi school holds that a conditional divorce (e.g., 'If I marry Zaynab, she is divorced') is valid and takes effect upon fulfillment of the condition, even if the condition is not owned at the time of utterance.
2
Identify the two objections raised by opponents
Opponents (e.g., Shafi'is) object: (1) The cause (sabab) for divorce does not exist at the time of utterance because the ruling is suspended; (2) The speaker has no volitional action (ṣunʿ) at the moment the condition occurs, so the divorce cannot take effect.
These objections challenge the causal mechanism and the speaker's capacity.
3
Follow the opponent's logical entailment claim
The opponents assert that if the conditional statement is not an immediate performative, then no cause exists; and if the speaker cannot act at condition fulfillment, the prior statement cannot become effective. They posit an entailment (mulazama) between absence of immediate cause/capacity and invalidity.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to each objection
First response: The conditional statement (shartiyya) only indicates entailment (mulazama), not immediate causation. The intended cause is the prior intent or performative utterance that the Shari'ah recognizes as effective upon condition occurrence. Second response: Prior action suffices; the Lawgiver (Shariʿ) makes the prior speech act effective at the condition's existence, irrespective of the speaker's immediate capacity.
The Hanafi emphasizes that Shari'ah recognition overrides the lack of immediate action.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: A man who says 'if I marry Zaynab, she is divorced' prior to marriage — Hanafi: valid divorce upon marriage; Opponent: no effect. This affects prenuptial agreements and conditional divorces in family law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عنده (وهو باطل، والجواب عنهما مما دريت لا يخفى أما عن الأول فلأن كون الحكم معلقاً مسلم لكن الكلام في أن سببه موجود الآن أم بعد تحققه اقتضاء إذ ليس في الثاني إيقاع أصلاً، إنما هو بعد وجود الشرط، ولعلك تقول من قبلهم إن كان الكلام هو الجزاء والشرط قيد له، فهذا المقيد يقتضي الوقوع ففيه اقتضاء إن كان إنشاء أو سبقه شيء فيه اقتضاء هو المخبر عنه، فوجد السبب وإن كان الكلام مجموع الشرط والجزاء فهو يقتضي لوقوع الطلاق عند الشرط، ويفضي إليه هذا إن كان إنشاء وإلا لا بد من تحقق اللزوم وهو بالإنشاء المتقدم عليه فقد تحقق السبب : ولك أن تجيب بأنا بينا سابقا أن الشرطية لا تفيد إلا الملازمة بين الشيئين إنشاء كان أو إخباراً، وهي لا تفضي إلى وقوع الجزاء أو إيقاعه فلا تصلح للسببية وكيف لا وقد يقصد منه عدم الوقوع فإنه يكون للمنع ، وأيضاً وقوع الشرط مشكوك الوجود في نظر المتكلم فما حال ما علق به ولو تنزلنا نقول من ادعى سببية المجموع فعليه الإبانة، فإنا من وراء المنع وأما إذا كان الجزاء كلاماً والشرط قيده فقد عرفت أنه يكون قضية تقديرية مساوقة للشرطية، فحكمها حكمها، بخلاف المضاف، فإنه لا تقدير فيه بل إنشاء بالتحقق الواقعي في وقت معين أو إخبار عنه فتدبر وأما عن الثاني فبمنع الملازمة وهو ظاهر وشيد أركان الاستدلال بأن المتكلم لا صنع له عند وقوع الشرط واعتباره مطلقاً عنده تقديراً مجرد اعتبار لا يصلح لابتناء الأحكام الشرعية، كيف : وقد يكون عند وجود الشرط غير أهل، بل مجنوناً لا يصلح مطلقاً، فلو لم يكن حال التكلم إيقاعاً لم يكن إيقاعاً عند الشرط أيضاً، فثبت الملازمة، ولك أن تجيب عنه بأنه لا يلزم الصنع عند الشرط، بل الصنع السابق يكفي لأنه وإن لم يكن معتبراً شرعاً ولا مفضياً إلى شيء لكن جعله الشارع مفضياً عند الوجود، فصار تطليقاً عند وجود الشرط حقيقة، والزوج مطلقاً لا بمجرد الاعتبار فقط، والجنون لا ينافي صيرورة الصنع السابق تطليقاً إنما ينافي اعتبار كلامه حال الجنون فافهم
He says: (And it is invalid, and the answer to both is as you have learned, not hidden. As for the first, the fact that the ruling is suspended is granted, but the discussion is whether its cause exists now or only after its realization as a matter of effect, because in the second there is no immediate occurrence at all; it is only after the existence of the condition. Perhaps you might say from their side: If the statement is the apodosis and the condition is its qualification, then this qualified statement necessitates occurrence, so it contains an effect if it is a performative utterance, or if something preceded it that contains an effect, then it is the thing being reported, so the cause exists. And if the statement is the combination of condition and apodosis, then it necessitates the occurrence of divorce at the condition, and it leads to this if it is a performative utterance; otherwise there must be verification of the binding, and that is by the preceding performative utterance, so the cause is verified. And you may respond: We explained earlier that the conditional statement only indicates entailment between two things, whether performative or declarative, and it does not lead to the occurrence of the apodosis or its immediate effect, so it is not suitable for causality. How so? Sometimes it is intended to indicate non-occurrence, as in prevention. Also, the occurrence of the condition is doubtful in the speaker's view, so what of that which is suspended on it? Even if we concede, we say: Whoever claims that the combination is a cause must provide proof, for we are at liberty to deny. As for when the apodosis is a statement and the condition is its qualification, you have known that it becomes a hypothetical proposition equivalent to a conditional clause, so its ruling is its ruling, unlike the genitive construction, for there is no hypothesis in it; rather it is a performative utterance about actual realization at a specific time or a report about it, so reflect. As for the second, it is by denying the entailment, which is apparent. He strengthened the pillars of the argument: The speaker has no action at the occurrence of the condition, and considering him absolutely effective is a mere hypothetical consideration not suitable for founding Shari'ah rulings. How so: At the existence of the condition, he may be incapable, even insane, absolutely unsuitable. So if at the time of speaking it was not an immediate effect, it will not be an immediate effect at the condition either, thus the entailment is proven. And you may respond: Action at the condition is not required; rather, the prior action suffices because even if it is not considered legally and does not lead to anything, the Lawgiver has made it effective upon existence. So it becomes a divorce at the existence of the condition in reality, and the husband is a divorcer not merely by consideration alone. Insanity does not negate the prior action becoming a divorce; it only negates the validity of his speech while insane, so understand.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi response to two objections raised against the validity of conditional divorce (ta'liq al-talaq) when the condition (e.g., marriage) is not yet owned. The first objection is that the cause (sabab) for divorce is not present at the time of utterance because the ruling is suspended. The Hanafi counter-argument: the conditional statement (shartiyya) indicates a logical entailment (mulazama) between condition and apodosis, not immediate causation. The intended effect is realized when the condition occurs, either through the performative nature of the utterance or through prior intention recognized by the Shari'ah. The second objection is that the speaker has no volitional action at the moment the condition occurs (e.g., if he becomes insane), so the divorce cannot take effect. The response: the prior speech act suffices; the Shari'ah gives it legal effect upon fulfillment of the condition. The passage carefully distinguishes between different types of conditional statements and emphasizes that the Shari'ah's recognition of the prior act as effective is independent of the speaker's immediate capacity at the condition's occurrence.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Suspending the occurrence of a legal effect (like divorce) on the occurrence of a future condition.
الشرطية (al-shartiyya): A conditional statement that creates an entailment between two things, not immediate causation.
السببية (al-sababiyya): The quality of being a cause (sabab) that brings about a legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response, why does the conditional statement not require immediate action by the speaker at condition fulfillment?
A) Because the condition is impossible
B) Because the prior intent suffices
C) Because the conditional statement is void
D) Because the speaker has no capacity

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school upholds conditional divorce by distinguishing between entailment and causation, and by affirming that prior intent, recognized by the Shari'ah, suffices for legal effect upon condition fulfillment.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Validity of Conditional Divorce Before Marriage: Hanafi vs. Opposing Views
Usul dispute over conditional divorce before nikah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on ta'liq al-talaq before nikah and evaluate the usul arguments used to refute opposing evidence.

Core Idea
Divorce before marriage is invalid because the husband does not yet own the right to divorce.

This passage presents the Hanafi argument against conditional divorce pronouncements made before marriage. It systematically refutes opposing hadith, Quranic interpretations, and claims of Companion consensus, demonstrating Hanafi usul methodology in action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The core question: Is a conditional divorce pronouncement made before marriage valid? If a man says 'If I marry Fatima, she is divorced,' does the divorce take effect when he marries her?
The issue is about the timing of the pronouncement relative to ownership of the right to divorce.
2
Examine the Hanafi proof structure
The Hanafis argue that divorce requires milk al-talaq (ownership of the right to divorce) at the time of pronouncement. Since before marriage the man does not possess this right, the ta'liq is void. Evidence includes the hadith 'no divorce before marriage' and Companion practices.
3
Analyze how weak hadith are rejected
Opponents cite hadith from al-Daraqutni that seem to allow divorce before marriage. The Hanafis critique the chains, declaring narrators like Amr ibn Khalid (a liar) and Ali ibn Qarin (a thief of hadith) as unreliable, making the hadith weak and inadmissible.
This demonstrates the usul principle of jarh wa ta'dil (criticism of narrators).
4
Evaluate the Quranic argument
The verse al-Ahzab 49 mentions divorce after marriage. Opponents interpret it as implying divorce cannot occur before marriage. The Hanafis respond that the verse only states a ruling for a specific scenario and does not negate conditional divorce. The interpretation of Ibn Abbas is not accepted because the wording does not bear it.
This shows the usul distinction between 'ibara al-nass (explicit meaning) and ishara al-nass (implied meaning).

Source Text

معين : كذاب وفي الأخير علي بن قرين، كذبه ابن معين وغيره وقال ابن عدي : يسرق الحديث، بل ضعف أحمد وأبو بكر القاضي شيخ الأحاديث وقال : ليس لها أصل ولذا لم يعمل بها مالك وربيعة والأوزاعي، والسهيلي: هذا، وقالوا رابعاً: روى عبد الرزاق عن أمير المؤمنين علي كرم الله وجهه، قال الحسن البصري : سأل رجل علياً قال : قلت : إن تزوجت فلانة فهي طالق فقال علي: ليس بشيء، وهذا إنما يتمشى منهم بطريق الجدل، وإلا فقول الصحابي ليس حجة عندهم، قلنا: معارض بما روى مالك في الموطأ أن سعيد بن عمر بن سليم الرازقي سأل القاسم بن محمد عن رجل طلق امرأته إن هو تزوجها، فقال القاسم إن رجلاً جعل امرأته كظهر أمه إن هو تزوجها، فأمره عمر إن هو تزوجها لا يقربها حتى يكفر كفارة المظاهر، كذا في «فتح القدير»، وأما الجواب بأن أهل الحديث قالوا : لم يلق الحسن أمير المؤمنين علياً كرم الله وجهه، فليس بشيء، فإنهم شهداء على النفي، وقد اتفقوا التواتر والرواة كلهم أولياء أصحاب كرامات وبالجملة : الشك فيه زلة عظيمة فافهم، وأما رد الجواب بأن المرسل ليس حجة فليس بوارد إذ مقصوده أنه ليس حجة عند معارضة المسند، وما عن أمير المؤمنين عمر مسند هذا، وقالوا خامساً : قال الله تعالى: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا إِذَا نَكَحْتُمُ الْمُؤْمِنَاتِ ثُمَّ طَلَّقْتُمُوهُنَّ﴾ [الأحزاب: ٤٩] روى عبد الرزاق عن ابن جريج قال: بلغ ابن عباس أن ابن مسعود يقول : إن طلق ما لم ينكح فهو جائز، قال ابن عباس: أخطأ في هذا، إن الله يقول : ﴿إِذَا نَكَحْتُمُ الْمُؤْمِنَاتِ ثُمَّ طَلَّقْتُمُوهُنَّ مِن قَبْلِ أَن تَمَسُّوهُنَّ﴾ [الأحزاب: ٤٩] ولم يقل : إذا طلقتم المؤمنات أطنبنا الكلام في هذه المسألة، فإنه مما زل فيه أقدام الراسخين والله أعلم بأحكامه .
It has already passed that custom is a specifier, so it specifies the hadith if its inclusiveness of divorce is conceded; understand. Then this interpretation is transmitted from al-Zuhri and al-Sha'bi, and Abd al-Razzaq narrated from al-Zuhri the interpretation of the hadith in this pattern, and Ibn Abi Shayba narrated from Salim, al-Qasim ibn Muhammad, Amr ibn Hazm, Abd al-Rahman, and Makhul similar to our statement, and it was also transmitted from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib, Ata', Hammad ibn Abi Sulayman, and Shurayh; thus in Fath al-Qadir. It may be objected with what al-Daraqutni narrated from Ibn Umar that the Prophet (peace be upon him) was asked about a man who said: 'The day I marry so-and-so, she is divorced.' He said: 'He divorced what he does not possess.' And also with what he narrated from Abu Tha'laba who said: 'Umar said to me: Work for me so that I marry you to my daughter. I said: If I marry her, she is divorced three times. Then I wanted to marry her, so I came to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) and his companions and asked him, and he said to me: Marry her, for there is no divorce before marriage.' In al-Talwih it is attributed to Abdullah ibn Amr ibn al-As that this oath does not receive this response in that case, and he said: This is an explicit hadith that does not accept interpretation. We said: Both hadiths are weak and cannot be used as evidence. Shaykh Ibn al-Humam said in Fath al-Qadir: The author of Tanqih al-Tahqiq said that both are false; in the first is Abu Khalid al-Wasiti, who is Amr ibn Khalid, a liar, and Ahmad and Ibn Ma'in said: a liar; in the latter is Ali ibn Qarin, whom Ibn Ma'in and others accused of lying, and Ibn Adi said: he steals hadith; rather Ahmad and Abu Bakr al-Qadi weakened the hadiths and said: they have no basis; therefore Malik, Rabi'a, and al-Awza'i did not act upon them. Al-Suhayli said: this. They said fourthly: Abd al-Razzaq narrated from the Commander of the Faithful Ali (may Allah honor his face) that al-Hasan al-Basri said: A man asked Ali, said: I said: If I marry so-and-so, she is divorced. Ali said: It is nothing. This is only consistent from them by way of argumentation; otherwise, the statement of a Companion is not a proof according to them. We said: It is countered by what Malik narrated in al-Muwatta' that Sa'id ibn Umar ibn Sulaym al-Raziqi asked al-Qasim ibn Muhammad about a man who divorced his wife if he married her, and al-Qasim said that a man made his wife like his mother's back if he married her, and Umar ordered him that if he married her, he should not approach her until he expiates the expiation of zihar; thus in Fath al-Qadir. As for the response that the hadith scholars said: al-Hasan did not meet the Commander of the Faithful Ali (may Allah honor his face), it is nothing, for they are witnesses to the negation, and they agreed that they were both in Medina for a period, so non-meeting is unlikely. Then all the transmitters of the chains transmitted the chain continuously, there is no doubt in its continuity and meeting, and a Muslim does not dare to attack them; children laugh at his intellect. Also the chains have reached the level of tawatur, and all the narrators are awliya' and possessors of miracles. In short: doubt concerning it is a great slip; understand. As for the response that the mursal is not a proof, it is not valid, for its purpose is that it is not a proof when opposing the musnad; and what is from the Commander of the Faithful Umar is musnad. They said fifthly: Allah said: {O you who have believed, when you marry believing women and then divorce them} [al-Ahzab:49]. Abd al-Razzaq narrated from Ibn Jurayj that he said: It reached Ibn Abbas that Ibn Mas'ud says: If he divorces before marrying, it is valid. Ibn Abbas said: He erred in this; Allah says: {when you marry believing women and then divorce them before you have touched them} and did not say: when you divorce believing women and then marry them. We said: The verse does not negate the validity of attachment to ownership; rather it contains the ruling of when he marries and then divorces before touching. Ibn Abbas's interpretation is not accepted because the wording does not bear it, and opposing the statement of Ibn Mas'ud is not a proof either; understand. We have spoken at length in this issue, for it is a matter in which the feet of the firmly grounded have slipped; and Allah knows best His rulings.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi position on the validity of a conditional divorce pronounced before marriage, along with responses to objections from opponents. The Hanafis hold that such a conditional statement is invalid because divorce requires actual ownership of the right at the time of pronouncement. The passage first argues that the hadith 'no divorce before marriage' is interpreted by early scholars (al-Zuhri, al-Sha'bi) in a way consistent with the Hanafi view. It then refutes opposing hadiths reported by al-Daraqutni, declaring them weak and fabricated. The response to the claim that a Companion's statement is not a proof is countered by citing actions of the Prophet's Companions (Ali, Umar) that support the Hanafi position. Finally, the Quranic verse (al-Ahzab 49) is interpreted not as negating conditional divorce, but as only describing a specific scenario. The passage concludes by emphasizing the perfection of the Hanafi argument.

Key Terms
تعليق الطلاق (ta'liq al-talaq): Suspending the occurrence of divorce on a future condition, e.g., 'if you enter the house, you are divorced'.
مِلْك الطلاق (milk al-talaq): Ownership of the right to divorce; a prerequisite for a valid divorce pronouncement.
الحديث الضعيف (al-hadith al-da'if): A hadith that does not meet the criteria of authenticity due to a weak narrator or discontinuity in the chain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Conditional divorce before nikah is invalid in the Hanafi school because the husband does not own the right to divorce before marriage.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Conditional Divorce After Removal of Mahall: Does It Survive?
Examination of whether a conditional divorce survives loss of legal capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi three's position on whether a conditional divorce survives removal of mahall, evaluate Zufar's qiyas argument, and apply the tamarah al-khilaf to a practical scenario.

Core Idea
Conditional divorce is extinguished when the legal capacity for divorce is permanently removed.

This lesson examines a nuanced usul dispute within the Hanafi school: if a man conditions divorce on an event (e.g., 'if you enter the house, you are divorced') and then irrevocably divorces his wife three times, does the conditional link remain so that if she later returns after a halala marriage and the condition occurs, she is divorced? Zufar says yes, by analogy with property. The majority (Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn) say no. We will dissect the reasoning and the critical response to it.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Position
Identify the position of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad: the conditional divorce does not survive removal of mahall.
They are referred to as 'the three scholars'.
2
Identify the Opponent's View
Zufar holds that the conditional remains, based on qiyas with conditional divorce attached to possession.
Zufar was a prominent early Hanafi jurist.
3
Follow the Opponent's Proof
Understand Zufar's analogy: just as a conditional divorce tied to future possession remains valid even if possession is temporarily lost, so does the conditional here.
4
Analyse the Hanafi Response
The three argue that condition is the last part of the cause; if mahall is absent when the condition occurs, the condition fails. Additionally, the man's conditional statement only covered the divorces he owned then, which he used up.
The Matla' al-Asrar objection questions whether temporary removal negates conditionality, but the response is that after three divorces the original mahall is completely destroyed; a new marriage creates a fresh capacity.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Concrete consequence: After three divorces, a valid halala, and return to first husband, if the condition then occurs, Zufar says she is divorced; the three say she is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(تذنيب). (التعليق هل يبقى مع زوال المحلية) للملك (فزفر) يقول (نعم) يبقى، فإذا قال : إن دخلت فطالق، فأبانها ثلاثاً يبقى التعليق كما كان حتى لو تزوج بعد زوج آخر ودخلت طلقت قياساً على الملك) يعني إن علق الطلاق بالملك يصح ويبقى هذا مثله (والعلماء الثلاثة) الإمام أبو حنيفة وصاحباه قالوا : (لا) يبقى له أن المعلق بالشرط ليس سبباً في الحال، إنما السبب وقت وجود الشرط، وفي ذلك الوقت المحل مع الملك متحقق (أقول : وهو) أي قول أئمتنا الحق لأن الشرط جزء أخير من العلة التامة حتى لا يتوقف المعلول بعده على أمر آخر، وإنما يكون الشرط جزءاً أخيراً (ببقاء المحلية وإلا فيتوقف على أمر زائد هذا خلف (فإذا انتفت المحلية انتفت الشرطية فلم يبق المعلق به شرط وقوع الطلاق (تدبر) وفيه نظر، هب أن الشرط جزء أخير لا يتوقف المعلول بعده على أمر آخر وههنا أيضاً كذلك، فإنه بعد النكاح بعد التحلل إذا وجد الشرط يوجد الطلاق من غير توقف على أمر آخر، وأما توقفه قبل انعقاده سبباً على الغير فلا يضركما أن يتوقف بعد الإبانة بواحد على الملك، واعترض مطلع الأسرار «الإلهية أبي قدس سره أن ارتفاع المحلية رأساً يمنع الشرطية كما في حرمة المصاهرة وغيرها لعدم الفائدة وأما ارتفاع المحلية موقتاً كما في المطلقة الثلاث فلا يوجب انتفاء الشرطية فقوله : إذا انتفت المحلية انتفت الشرطية ممنوع إن أراد ما يعم الارتفاع الموقت، وإن أراد الارتفاع رأساً ،فمسلم لكن لا ينفع، ثم أجاب بالبناء على مسألة الهدم أن حل المحل قد ارتفع رأساً، وهذا حل جديد حادث ابتداء، فإن الزوج الثاني محلل عندنا، هذا لا تخفى متانته، لكن لو رجع وقيل إن ارتفاع المحلية بحيث لا يأبى مجيء حل آخر لا ينافي الشرطية لم يبعد، فالأولى في الاستدلال لهم أن الظاهر أنه ما علق إلا ما في ملكه، وهو لم يملك حال التعليق إلا الثلاث، وقد بطلت بالتنجيز فلم يبق معلقاً، وأما الطلقات الثلاث المملوكة بعد التحلل فلم تكن داخلة في الطلاق المعلق فتدبر .
(Appendix). (Does the conditional remain with the removal of legal capacity) for possession? (Zufar) says (yes) it remains. If he says: 'If you enter, then you are divorced,' and he divorces her three times, the conditional remains as it was, so even if he marries another husband after and she enters, she is divorced, by analogy with possession. Meaning: if he attaches divorce to possession, it is valid and remains; this is similar. (The three scholars) Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions said: (no) it does not remain, because that which is attached to a condition is not a cause at present; the cause is at the time the condition exists, and at that time the legal capacity with possession is actual. I say: and it (i.e., the statement of our imams) is the truth, because the condition is the last part of the complete cause such that the effect does not depend on anything else after it; rather the condition is the last part (by the remaining of legal capacity; otherwise it depends on an additional matter, which is a contradiction). So if legal capacity is removed, conditionality is removed, so what is attached by condition no longer remains a condition for the occurrence of divorce (reflect). And there is consideration in it: grant that the condition is the last part, the effect does not depend on anything else after it; here also it is so, for after marriage after dissolution, when the condition exists, the divorce occurs without depending on anything else. As for its dependence before it becomes a cause on something else, it does not harm; likewise depending after separation by one on possession. And the author of Matla' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya (Abu, may Allah sanctify his secret) objected that the removal of legal capacity absolutely prevents conditionality, as in the prohibition of affinity and others, due to lack of benefit; but temporary removal of legal capacity as in the thrice-divorced woman does not necessitate the negation of conditionality. So his statement: 'if legal capacity is removed, conditionality is removed' is not granted if he intends what includes temporary removal; if he intends absolute removal, it is accepted but does not benefit. Then he answered by building on the issue of hadm (rebuttal) that the permission of the legal capacity has been removed absolutely, and this is a new permission arising de novo, because the second husband is a muhallil according to us; this is not hidden from its firmness. But if one returns and says that removal of legal capacity in a way that does not preclude the coming of another permission does not contradict conditionality, it is not far-fetched. The better in reasoning for them (the three) is that it is apparent that he only attached what was in his possession, and at the time of attachment he possessed only three (divorces), and they were annulled by the immediate (pronouncement), so nothing remained attached. As for the three divorces owned after dissolution, they were not included in the attached divorce; reflect.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This appendix discusses whether a conditional divorce (e.g., 'if you enter the house, you are divorced') remains in effect after the wife's legal capacity (mahall) to be divorced is removed, as when three divorces occur. The issue is whether the condition survives until a later time when the capacity is restored (after a valid intervening marriage). Zufar argues that the condition remains, by analogy with attaching divorce to future possession of property. The Hanafi three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) argue that the condition does not survive. Their reasoning: the condition is the last part of the complete cause; if the legal capacity is removed at the moment the condition would take effect, the condition itself is nullified. The passage then discusses objections: whether the removal of capacity is absolute or temporary. The response builds on the concept that after three divorces, the woman becomes completely forbidden (until a proper halala marriage), so the original capacity is absolutely removed; the new capacity after halala is a fresh beginning. The better argument for the Hanafi three is that the man's conditional statement only applied to the divorces he owned at that time, which he used up by the actual divorce pronouncement, so no conditional remains.

Key Terms
محلية (mahalliyyah): The legal capacity of the wife to be divorced; the status of being a valid subject for divorce.
شرطية (shartiyyah): The quality of being a condition; conditionality.
تعليق (ta'liq): Conditional attachment; suspending a legal effect on a future condition.
هدم (hadm): Rebuttal; the concept that the original marital bond is completely destroyed after three divorces, necessitating a new marriage to restore legal capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), what happens to a conditional divorce when the wife's legal capacity (mahall) is removed by three irrevocable divorces?
A) The conditional remains, and if she later returns after halala and the condition occurs, she is divorced.
B) The conditional is extinguished because the condition must be fulfilled while the mahall exists.
C) The conditional remains only if the husband explicitly states it will survive.
D) The conditional is annulled only if the wife marries another man.

Key Takeaway
A conditional divorce is tied to the original marital capacity; once that capacity is permanently removed by three divorces, the condition becomes void and does not carry over to a new marriage.
